THE RED PAGE.
An Eng. publishing club, “ At the Sign of the
Hop-Pole,” announces the issue presently of a translation
of M6rim6e’s Carmen, “ with illustrations by ” our Aus-
tralian “ Lionel Lindsay, and a biographical, analytical,
and critical introduction.” Lindsay writes brightly, and
we quote a passage of the subscriber-seducing circular—
A comparison of spirit (if not of motive) may be
entered between Shakespear’s Antony and Cleo-
patra and M6rim6e’s Carmen. In both cases the
world is well lost for love, in both women is that feline
capriciousness whiuh lifts them, along with the capacity
for instinctive cruelty, far from the tame herds of the
world’s femininity. The setting alone separates these
two sisters ; one is queen over Egypt, the other over a
little band of brigands ; but wilful caprice unites them
across the centuries : Yes ! all said and done, Carmen
is the Cleopatra of the picaresque.
Apart from graphic interest, Carmen is one of the
few convincingly real books the world possesses. A
modern would have padded such material to a three-
volume novel. But Merimde, having the true artist’s
eye for essentials, and without undue compression, has
given us something that lives almost by virtue of its
simplicity. M6rim6e knew that truth, as far as we can
know it, is in the world of appearances and surfaces—
things seen, things done. Truth to him was not the
bagatelle of M. Zola, a painful partnership with note-
books. He absorbed and his digestion was perfect.
Seeing clearly, he disdained alike romantic exaggeration
and superfluity ; his taste was impeccable, he wrote
with the ease and concision of a Roman of Augustus
time ■ and, in contradistinction to the story writers of
to-day, he did not shout in order to make himself
is a story that can never grow old. It ex-
presses human nature so profoundly, so simply. Its
construction is like a happening, one wonders that art
should be so easy . . .
The discretion ot it! the decorum of it! And, writes the
commentator,
The offer, I need not say, was accepted or my mother
would not have been born just outside Sydney Heads
and carried ashore by old Mr. John Palmer in a silk
pocket-handkerchief.
The commentator is the Rev. James Hassall, of Corinda
(Q.); and it is from his book In Old Australia: Records
and Reminiscences from 1794 (Brisbane: R. S. Hews)
that the letter is extracted. Mr. Hassall’s maternal
grandfather was the Rev. Sam. Marsden, as shown ; his
paternal grandfather was a missionary who sailed to
Tahiti in 1796, and shortly afterwards came to Australia
and settled in Parramatta ; “ no one was better known at
one period of the early days in New South Wales than
my father, the Rev.'Thomas Hassall,” for some time
chaplain at the convict establishment of Port Macquarie ;
and Mr. Hassall himself continued the ministering tradi-
tion of the family by being ordained to the Church of
England, and serving for some years as chaplain in
Berrima gaol. His book, apart from its quality as a per-
sonal and family record, contains much that is interest-
ing; and the student of early Australian days cannot
neglect it.
Landscape Painting from Nature in Australia,
a manual for the student in oil and water-colours, by A.
J. Daplyn (Sydney ; Penfold). With illustrations from
paintings by Daplyn and Lister, and educational
sketches. An interesting and useful little book. The
author expresses himself well, and tells the student just
what one wants to know. We like his acceptance of the
fact that Australia is Australia—not England or Italy.
That seems a truism ; but several of our painters never
seem to recognise its truth. Says Daplyn—
Australian scenery has been greatly maligned by
writers, tourists, and others; far too much is heard
of its sadness, its want of colour, and its monotony.
Certainly it is not so paintable as the old country,
for there are no venerable ruins clothed with ivy, no
old churches or castles, or quaint red-tiled cottages;
in fact, one does not encounter pictures ready made,
and that only require transferring to the canvas.
But, on the other hand, the scenery is more original,
and has not been painted by generations of artists,
till one is surfeited by repetition. The intense light
makes also for greater finesse of colour: this is
especially observable in the tree forms, which require
a lighter touch and more subtle tone than the thick-
foliaged trees of the old country.
Of Australian trees the eucalyptus is, of course, the
most uncommon. We often hear of the “ everlasting
gum tree,” but its merits have rarely been acknow-
ledged. Few trees are, in truth, so graceful in form
and of such variety. This latter quality is quite lack-
ing in European trees, where the oaks are oaks, and the
elms, elms, and are always alike. Now the gum is
hardly ever the same, and twenty species may be found
growing in the space of a few acres. Some shoot up
into the air straight and smooth like a ship’s mast;
others have rough and shaggy bark, and the trunks
twist and turn in endless convolutions ; many are of a
bright red colour, or spotted like a leopard, while others
shed their bark, which hangs round them in weird
festoons, revealing, so to speak, the creamy-white flesh.
The branches shoot out from the trunk at all manner
of unexpected angles, and go twisting and turning at
their own sweet will, seeming to rebel against all laws
that control the growth of branches in general.
And the foliage—how gracefully it hangs in feathery
clusters! —not giving much shade, it is true, being more
ornamental than useful, and in this much resembling a
fine lady, which the eucalyptus may be said to personate
in the world of trees.
This is well said ; and Daplyn says other good things—
characteristic daplynisms ; but he has not forgotten his
main business of instruction, and his lessons are thorough.
We cordially recommend hi 3 manual to Australian
students.
THREE CLIFFS.
JE'ROM that cliff Daley fell:
The Muses’ lover,
He east his line too well
And himself over.
Shrieked all the tuneful Nine I
Fled all the fishes ! . . .
(One basketful divine ;
Seventeen dishes.)
From, this cliff Lawson fell!
Our Bush reflector,
Losing in hydromel
His poise of Hector.
Were nerves awry his foes ?
Did his wrongs rankle ? . . .
(B roken, a noble nose ;
Item, an ankle.)
Sometimes , in dreaming dull,
By a cliff ghastly
I see a human hull,
Shroud and rent mast lie ;
And humbly meditate:
Here, in this life-hell,
But for the grace of Fate
I, even I fell.
One Realist and Another,
R€HtT$tTG art, as reiterated, is the universal
expression of a particular impression. Two books
published by Duckworth, London Millet ,* by
Bgjmain Rolland ; and Bush Studies , by Barbara
flay nton—come conveniently together to illus-
trate this text.
Millet was an epic realist—his pictures are both des-
criptions and types—in portraying peasants he painted
humanity. Rolland shows clearly that he had no notion
of the social-apostolie meaning attributed, e.g., to The
Man With the Hoe. “Nothing was further from
Millet’s mind : he detested sentimental and melodramatic
painting; he was indifferent to politics; he repudiated
socialism.” True, all Millet’s work can be taken as
symbolistic for those minds that see the symbol ; but for
Millet himself there was no symbol. He was content
with statement; yet he desired for his statement the
deepest basis and the widest possible extension on
the realistic line. He aimed to make his Soiver
one sower fully and clearly expressed, and in that
and by that the expression of all sowers, all scat-
tered of literal grain in an actual field; but
for him his picture held nothing more. He had stated
the fact, uttered the type ; and was content. Yet, with-
out his design—even, as it would appear, without his
sanction* The Soiver becomes the symbol of something
more than the peasant and his labour : it becomes the
figure of those who sow ideas, who sow worlds, the eternal
seminal. Such a picture reaches the apex of realistic
The steps of that art are thus three in number. First,
there is the statement of the thing seen. Second, there
is the statement of the thing seen so that it becomes a
type of its class. Third, there is the statement of the
type so that it is seen in relation to all things, all types ;
becomes universal in its reference—a key to unlock
every comprehending mind.
Millet—rightly, it would seem—contented himself with
taking the first two steps, and leaving the third to his
audience. Or rather, having taken the two first steps, he
took the third despite himself; for the reason that, his
message once given,, the meanings of his message passed
beyond his control —they lay in the eye of the gazer.
Barbara Bavnton has taken only the first step. Her
studies of some Australian people and scenes are realistic
beyond anything of the kind yet written here—beyond
Lawson, even beyond Miles Franklin. Bit by bit, with
careful epithet after epithet, the work is built up until
nothing could be closer to the life. Flaubert would have
been in ecstasy over such a pupil: for minute fidelity
there has been no writer anywhere to surpass this writer.
But always or nearly always Mrs. Baynton remains on
the first plane of realism—she describes wonderfully well
the thing she sees ; but that thing is not a human type,
or is a human type for Australia only. So that the reader,
on the third plane, has to strain his mind to an un-
finished piece of realistic art, if he would receive the
message which is that art’s justification and, climax.
And, if he have not Australian knowledge, this is a task
which the reader cannot achieve.
The value of Bush Studies for Australians is another
matter. Despite inequalities and some lack of finish,
they deserve the warmest praise. “ Bush Church ’’
simply could not be set down better. “Squeaker’s
Mate ” lacks an ending ; but as far as it goes it is almost
similarly perfect. “The Chosen Vessel” has been added
to since it appeared as the “The Tramp” in The Bulletin,
and the additional matter has been inserted in the wrong
place ; but none the less it is astonishingly graphic. To
“Billy Skywonkie” and “Scrammy ’And” the same
praise applies.
The book contains only half-a-dozen sketches ; and
four of them are in all essentials perfect so far as they go.
This is uncompromising commendation ; but Bush
Studies deserves uncompromising commendation. So
precise, so complete, with such insight into detail and
such force of statement, it ranks with the masterpieces
of literary realism in any language.
On the first plane only. <
Newly Published.
Hesper and Hedone: and English Verses , written
by Frederick Richardson, published by Melville & Mullen,
Melbourne. The title-page refers to Phoebus Apollo : the
introduction defers “to Margaret” : the contents merge
modern sentiment with memories of the classic lyre.
The author has no definite message; but he mumbles
musically enough : one can hardly differentiate the nest
of the English Verses from the not-best of Robert
Bridges. Hesper and Hedone is Stephen Phillips; but
fainter. For lack of a little more grace, a little more
force, a little more originality, Frederick must be
whistled down the hot wind. Overnight we did read
with mild interest, and did mark some pleasant lines
for quotation: this morning they look too worn, too
feeble. Yet . . . “To Dawn” —
0 Danin, eternity is in thy smile f * • •
IT.
Thy semblance wed to each fair thing that grow
Seem,s pregnant with the -wonder of the stars ;
Thou art of earth the everlasting Rose,
Thou art ofj/outh the hope no trouble mars :
Each day with eyes unwearied thou dost view
A changeful world, and, scattered from thy hands,
Flushed petals light on Himalayan snows.
Or float above the deep Atlantic blue,
Or down the horizon of far Austral lands
Drop sloxvly ; from the darkness and the dew
Emerge grey hills, loud woods a/nd rippled sands.
And lo ! thou hast created Earth anew.
This, you notice, attains nearly, not quite ; and too-
loud ” earns a bad mark: curious w'hat a noise that
epithet makes in contrast with that mood, that metre.
The moderate love-letter of the Rev. Samuel
Marsden, our historic parson—
London, March 14,1793.
Dear Betsy
I have not had an opportunity to thank you for the
Scripture Characters before now ; since I came to
London my time and thoughts being so very much
engaged. I hope you will not consider this as a mark
of "my Disrespect, but rather look upon my peculiar
situation as a sufficient Apology for my conduct in this
thing. . . . _ ,
Since my lot is now, seemingly, cast and God ap-
pears to be opening my way to carry the Gospel of His
Son to distant Lands ; the time is come for me to lay
open my thoughts to you, which have long been hid in
my own Breast. ..
I shall venture to submit to your Consideration the
following important Question (praying at the same time
that the Lord would enable you to answer it agreeably
to His own Will; and in such a way as may conduce to
vour own happiness and mine). The Question is this :
Will you go along with me ? If upon considering the
subject you can answer in the Affirmative and say : I
am willing ; then my heart (as far as it is proper I should
give it to the Creature) and all I have are yours. I
believe if it be for my good and His Glory He will pro-
vide me with an Helpmate ; and if not He will give me
a mind resigned to His Will. I persuade myself I shod,
be happy in the Enjoyment of you more than any other;
yet I do not wish to purchase my own Peace at the Ex-
pense of vour Comfort; but only if you think you
would be happy. . . . Then I cheerfully offer you my
Hand and my Heart whenever you please.
I remain,
Dear Betsy,
Yours most affectionately,
S. MARSDEN.
Current Commentary.
The fresh editor is godsent to Sydney Neios.
In the desert of evening journalism Paterson’s humorous
leaders are an oasis of joy.
Marchant & Co., a firm of Sydney printers,
noticing that other firms of Sydney printers have been
here “ mentioned,” desire to be mentioned also; and
send specimens of press-work in the form of collections o
photographs reproduced by the half-tone process. The
work is generally fair—better than the Sydney average
and interesting. Sometimes, in his laudable zeal to
“keep the colour up,” the pressman has destroyed
detail; but that is more often the fault of the photo-
graph. The photos, are by Swain & Co. and Kerry & Co.,
and on the whole we prefer Kerry’s : they hold more
tones and more detail : though pictorially one of Swam s
—“The Jamieson Valley ” (in Gems of Blue Mountain
Scenery)—is the best submitted. Both photographers
usually fail with water and scenery : in getting the trees
thev lose the water. Examples are Kerry’s “Katoomba
Falls ” (in Country Scenes and Pleasure Resorts),
looking like a long bathing towel ; and Swain s
“ Weeping Rock,” where the water might be a set
of stalactites. We think conscientious photo-
graphers should make two negatives, and piece them.
Always we would like softer pictures, with less violent
contrast : there is a soft, glowing brilliancy and a hard,
glaring brilliancy, and it is the former that “ a ‘‘-‘t o d e
printers should strive for. Some of the blocks would be
improved by a black-line border on the block. The
process-engraving seems generally fair. Marchant & Go.
suggest that their imprint “would look nice on your
next Catalogue or Booklet” ; but, as it is an excessnel)
ugly block, it could not possibly look well anywhere. It
the imprint is to be so prominent, the firm should set an
original and artistic design. A three-colour page headed
“Fine Printing” is well machined, but the compo-
sition is stiff and tasteless. On the whole, honourable
mention of Marchant & Co. must be confined to their
press-work, though the pressman might study the work
in the Inland Printer, for example, and (once more)
make his tones softer.
It is Miss Elizabeth Knight, of Sydney,
who tells us, in forwarding for publication an un-
printed poem, “The Madness of Ishtar, by Bliss Car-
man,” that her brother, Jasper Knight, is “ Bliss Car-
man B.C. being a pen-name only. This is news of
which Who 's Who gives u$ no hint ‘ that manual s pre-
cise statement reading (ed. 1903, p. 223) —
Carman, Bliss, journalist; b. Fredericton. New Bruns-
wick, Canada, 15 April, 1861; e.s. of William Carman
and Sophia Mary, d. of George P. Bliss.
A Dictionary of American Authors tells the same
story, adding that the name is William Bliss Carman.
That “George P. Bliss” looks very substantial; and as
Carman presumably supplied the information for Who's
Who, and added that his recreation is “canoeing,” we
want to know what Jasper Knight means by it all.
Cabled that Henry Norman, traveller and
writer, now editing The World’s Work, has divorced his
wife (n6e Menie Muriel Dowie—grand-daughter of Robert
Chambers, of the firm of W. and R., author of the
celebrated Vestiges of Creation). The other fellow is
Edward Fitzgerald, the Alpine (and Maoriland Alpine)
climber. The first'thing that the peculiar Menie suggests
s Mene, mene ; the next thing is Tickle ; and the climax
s IJpharsin. Names decide destinies ; and, reading long
ago A Girl in the Karpathians, one reflected that who-
ever christened the author had written prophetically on
the wall.
Ernest Favenc’s valuable articles on “The
Australian Desert,” in Sydney Telegraph, have just this
moral for those who bewail droughts and the ruin of
droughts-•“ Serve you riirht! ”
Nature has done her best with the inefficient means
at her disposal; but man has not ably seconded her. . .
She has made natural canals and reservoirs ,to store
any surplus flood-water, but he has not taken advantage
of the provision. She has given him subterranean
water to supplement the scanty rainfall, and he has let
it run to waste.
She has plainly warned us that Australia is a land of
droughts, and we have allowed our eyes to cheat our
minds when we have seen the grass of a good season.
The eastern interior has been overstocked till even the
grass-roots and bushes have been destroyed. Then hot
winds whirl the loosened soil aloft, and the desert ad-
vances. //Government be wise
Droughts will be fought by water conservation, the
proper and sensible utilisation of the artesian supply,
and the timely removal of stock from the chronically
dry districts. People will not be allowed to impoverish
the land to make a rapid fortune . . . The artesian
supply will be traced so far into the interior that the
natural depressions and lagoons will be kept full, and
the rigour of the climate ameliorated.
If government be wise ! Otherwise, we may look to see
Australia become “a fringe of habitable country encircling
a perennial desert dominated by a rainless climate and a
riot of dust-storms."
Swinburne’s characteristic study of King Lear, in Dec.
Haiper’s, has too much thunder and too little lightning.
His drum-and-trumpet “ criticism ” suggests the vehe-
mence of the man outside the circus, with his per-
petual invitation to walk in to “ the greatest show on
earth.” For example—
That Shakespeare’s judgment was as great and
almost as wonderful as his genius has been a
commonplace of criticism ever since the days of Cole-
ridge ; questionable only by such dirty and dwarfish
creatures of simian intellect and facetious idiocy as
mistake it for a sign of wit instead of dullness, and of
distinction instead of degradation, to deny the sun in
heaven and affirm the fragrance of a sewer . . .
After all, there is a considerable range of tones between
alabaster and the ace of spades. And we read—
Among all [the play’s] other great attributes,
among all the many other attributes which mark it
forever as matchless among the works of man, it has
this, above all, that it is the first great utterance of a
cry from the heights and depths of the human spirit
on behalf of the outcasts of the world—on behalf of
the social sufferers, clean or unclean, innocent or
criminal, thrall or free. To satisfy the sense of
righteousness, the craving for justice, as unknown
and unimaginable by Dante as by Chaucer, a change
must come upon the social scheme of things which
shall make an end of the actual relations between the
judge and the cutpurse, the beadle and the prostitute,
the beggar and the king. All this could be uttered,
could be prophesied, could be thundered from the Eng-
lish stage at the dawn of theseventeenthcentury. Were
it within the power of omnipotence to create a
German or a Russian Shakespeare, could anything of
the sort be whispered or muttered or hinted or
suggested from the boards of a Russian or a German
theatre at the d«,wn of the twentieth'? When a
Tolstoi or a Suderniann can do this, and can do it
with impunity in success, it will be allowed that his
country is not more than three centuries behind
England in civilisation and in reform . . .
As Swinburne’s own cry on behalf of the social sufferers
from the African war was a savage tirade against
Whelps and dams of murderous foes
Whom none save we had spared or feared to stai-ve and
slay,
it looks as if English civilisation, from Swinburne’s stand-
point, had degenerated considerably since Shakespeare’s
time.
Culled Contributors.
H.L.: Note that My Brilliant Career was pub-
lished against the judgment of Blackwood’s London
manager and reader, nor did William Blackwood think
greatly of the book. I told him that it would go in Aus-
tralia ; and he published it at a venture, as you may see
by the cheap get-up. And I was right. I had to barrack
for many religious, political, and sex-problem passages
which I blushed at myself, while 1 was assuring him the
Australians would n’t blush—and he saw tthe blush and
struck ’em out. Old Harris, the schoolmaster, is my
favourite character in that book. I think the school scene
where Harris shapes up to the inspector is a grand touch.
The McSwats are well drawn, and McSwat’s diary and
Harold’s letter to his sister are gems. I’ve read columns
of London reviews of “that extraordinary book a to-
be-continutd notice of four columns altogether, for
instance, in the Sunday Sun ; and I’ve had many letters
about that book ; but I never saw mention of old Harris,
for whose sake, especially, I took it to England. He is a
man and a gentleman, in spite of the drink and his dis-
reputable appearance—the one hero—though he only
has a page or two—the one solitary hero in My Brilliant
Career.
Tim: The Dramatic Critic , Sydney, recently
welcomed, appeared for one issue only. The old story-
no capital. Truth and Henslowe crushed again. Be-
surgant !
X : Reprint this extract from, the will of
Charles Dickens :
I emphatically direct that I be buried in an inex-
pensive, unostentatious, strictly private manner;
that no public announcement be made of the time or
place of my burial ; that at the utmost not more than
three plain mourning coaches be employed ; and that
those who attend my funeral wear no scarf, cloak, black
bow, long hat-band, or other revolting absurdity. I
direct that my name be inscribed in plain English
letters on my tomb, without the addition of “ Mr.” or
“Esquire.” I require my friends on no account to
make me the subject of any monument, memorial, or
testimonial whatever. I rest my claims to the remem-
brance of my country upon my published works, and to
the remembrance of my friends upon their experiences
of me in addition thereto.
R. : A story illustrating Brunton Stephens’s
boundless sympathy and thoughtfulness. A shower of
rain falling in Brisbane, someone remarked that it was
opportune and “ would do a lot of good—lay the dust and
all that you know'.” “Yes,” remarked Stephens, “but
it is a pity it does not fall at night. All the poor fellows
who drive watering-carts will, lose a day’s work—and
pay.”
S. : An advertiser in Oct. Edinburgh Review
desires to sell a maga. file containing papers by “ all the
eminent literati of the day,” including Matthew Arnold,
Fronde, John Morley, and T. Brassey. Er—er—er—
/hx-HuM!
L. : Recent linotype blunders that did not pass the
(Melb.) printer’s reader. A quotation from “As You Like
It ” was rendered “ Tongues in trees, books in the running
brooks, sermons in scones, and food in everything.” The
name of O’Farrell, licensed victualler, was appropriately
changed to O’Barrell. An alteration that would be
endorsed by Vic. railway men was “The restless spout
[spirit] at the head of affairs in the railway department.”
P.N. : Re pronunciation of “ Eyre.” Charlotte Bronte’s
novel hints that she, at least, intended “Eyre” to be
pronounced ire. In chap, xi Mdlle. Adela finds the
name difficult to Why (she being a French girl)
if pronounced with ie long a (air)? That sound, in
various spelling, is common in French ; whereas the
English sound of i in fire (a complex of ah-ee) does not
exist in the language, and is notoriously a stumbling-
block to French students of English.

re first published in‘The Passing Show’column

” and to The Argus —
“ WITH DEATH’S PROPHETIC EAR.”
Lay my rifle here beside me, set my Bible on my breast,
For a moment let the warning bugles cease ;
As the century is closing I am going to my rest,
Lord, lettest Thou Thy servant go in peace.
But loud through all the bugles rings a cadence in mine
ear,
And on the winds my hopes of peace are strowed,
Those winds that waft the voices that already I can hear
Of the rooi-baatje singing on the road.
Yes, the red-coats are returning, I can hear the steady
tramp,
After twenty years of waiting, lulled to sleep,
Since rank and file at Potchefstroom we hemmed them in
their camp,
And cut them up at Bronker spruit like sheep.
They shelled us at Ingogo, but we galloped into range,
And we shot the British gunners where they showed;
1 guessed they would return to us, I knew the chance
must change —
Hark! the rooi-baatje singing on the road !
But now from snow-swept Canada, from India's torrid
plains,
From lone Australian outposts, hither led,
Obeying their commando, as they heard the bugle’s strains,
The men in brown have joined the men in red.
They come to find 'the colours at Majuba left and lost,
They come to pay us back the debt they owed ;
And 1 hear new voices lifted, and I see strange colours
tossed,
’Mid the rooi-baatje singing on the road.
The old, old faiths must falter, and the old, old creeds
must fail —
I hear in that distant murmur low —
The old, old order changes, and ’t is vain for us to rail,
The great ivorld does not want us—we must go.
And veldt, aacl spruit, anil kopje to the stranger u
belong,
No more to trek before him we shall load ;
Too well, too well, 1 know it, for 1 hear it in the song
Of the rooi-baatje singing on the road.
Frank B. ;L. ; T.W.W. ; Gilrooney; S.J.R. ; H.P.; » nd

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Prayers and Threats.
Towers of daisies will I build,
Bine will I burn and frankincense,
And snowy flour all newly milled,
If thou wilt be my prop and fence,
If thou wilt red her face for me
With shining love’s temerity
As onyx cups with wine do be.
I will pile altars up with fruit,
And spill white oil across the ground,
And stroke the singing gold-haired lute
Into a sweeter tide of sound,
If me, uncompassed, without chart,
Thou, in supremely gracious part
Lead to the latchet of her heart.
But I will break and mutilate
Thy frost-white image into sand,
And strew the atoms, nor abate
Till nothing of thee stop to stand,
And I will devastate thy shrine,
If thou dost put but cold incline
Into her heart for heart of mine.
/.
HUGH R. McCRAE.
The House of Bliss.
The ways of men are sore to the feet, the ways of
men are cold;
H«,rsh is the quarrel of foes that meet —there is
never a friendly face ;
-tt-nd how shall a man find grace in life, or how
shall a man’s faith hold,
With no relief from the weary strife, nor any
quiet space ?
But I will marry the girl I love —we will live in a
hid retreat;
And I shall find solace and balm enough, and a
lazy, long delight
In a soft, clear voice that will float and ring with a
cadence calm and sweet,
And the days will pass like a murmuring, but rap-
ture come with the night.
And I will leave the House of Men, and go down
to our House of Bliss;
And her straight, white arms will hold me then,
and her deep, dark eyes enthrall . . .
Gods that sit in the West, behold ! is there any
life like this ?
That a man should live in a House of Gold, and
make his love his all ?
And we shall be out on the purple hills before the
moon is up,
When a great, cool wave of darkness fills the space
of earth and sky,
And faint night breezes will drift above, and dew
from the high stars’ cup,
While the only folk in the land of Love are mj
Lady of Night and I.
And we shall stand in the red moon’s beam, tossed
back from a silver cloud,
Watching the far horizon gleam where the moon
and the waters kiss,
And trace her path to the West away till she meet
the dawn-mist’s shroud,
And see her dim in the morning grey, and go down
to the House of Bliss.
M.L.
AWAKIG.
What!
What do you say with your eyes,
Looking right straight into mine?
Is it “ I love you !” or “Do you love me? ”
What are you looking at ? What do you see ?
Is it delight or surprise
That makes them gleam so, and shine?
There comes a shadow between,
So that I cannot see in !
Is it another’s, or is it my own ?
Now it has gone again ! Where has it flown ?
Whose was it ? What did it mean ?
Was it the shade of my sin ?
Flash ! There’s the message again !
What is it? What do you mean ?
How to decipher it! How to divine !
How ? If I have not the key ? Is it mine ?
Is it a portent of pain ?
Is it the love that has been?
Look ! Here ! Right straight into mine!
What do you say with your eyes ?
Is it a challenge, a warning, a fear ?
Is it a wish, that for words is too dear,
Born of a passion like wine ?
Is it the love that denies?
Oh ! But my very soul dies,
Calling to yours for a sign !
Must that inscrutable, fathomless gaze
Taunt me and haunt me the rest of my days ?
What do you say with your eyes,
Looking deep down into mine ?
S.A.
NIL.
A Toast.
M. L.
It were a fault to drink,
Save in a subtle wine
(The cup full to the brink,
That glancing gold may shine
Where velvet shadows sink),
To one who is divine !
No name shall we disclose,
But dream of her whose eyes
Have magic with the rose;
Within whose depths arise
Thoughts that are born of those
Cradled in paradise !
HUBERT CHURCH.

Low, Languid, and Dispirited.
The Vagaries of a Torpid Liver.
A Useful Lesson for Sufferers.
The Case of tyr. J. DECNAN-
(FOR THIS “ REDFERN TIMES.”)
Among the most important missions of
inquiry placed on record was that recently
made by our reporter to the home of Mr.
John Degnan, Garden-street, North Botany.
This is due as much to the magnitude of his
discoveries as to the interest of the incident
he elicited on inquiring.
“ Will you favour me with a complete
outline of the vicissitudes you successfully
combated ? ” 1
“ Right willingly,” answered Mr. Deg-
nan, as he offered the pressman a seat. Five
years went by from the time my illness
started till it was finally conquered, and if
I did not have a bad time it was a caution.
Bear in mind, it was all through the torpid
state of my liver, and, believe me, I got
into such a very wretched condition that I
did not care whether I lived or not. Before
I was really aware what disaster was be-
falling me 1 used to feel as though I had no
life in me, and was always low-spirited and
sleepy. There was only one thing that I
was fit for, and that was to lie down, but
when I did so 1 could not sleep, my mind
being actively engaged in going through a
multitude of unhappy thoughts which
seemed to be born of the melancholy condi-
tion I was in. What caused me considerable
uneasiness of mind was that my appetite
became poorer every day, and still more
anxiety was engendered by the knowledge
that what food I did eat was of no benefit
to me, my ability to digest any kind of
eatables having vanished. In this way it
transpired that my strength wasted away
till it was often compulsory for me to knock
off work, although my employment at that
time was not of a heavy description.”
“ How did you feel after your meals? ”
“Just as if I had swallowed so much
lead. Something like a heavy ball seemed
to form in my stomach, the pains arising
from which were simply horrible, and from
the stomach the heavy sensations travelled
up to my chest, thereby giving me extra-
ordinary difficulty in breathing. My head
ached to a degree which proved almost dis-
tracting, for I could not settle down to
read or do anything, and there was even a
swollen feeling in my eyes to annoy me.
Owing to a pricking sensation under my
shoulder-blades I used to think I had
caught a cold, but experience lias taught
me that that was a derangtd liver symptom,
while another one, although more torment-
ing in its persistency, was a nasty aching
pain across the loins, which I felt very dis-
tinctly when .Roving about. The bitter
taste in my mouth was enough to turn any
man off his food, and the dirty coating on
my tongue used to make me feel sick. At
times, when exhausted nature claimed a
few hours’ rest, I fell asleep, J>ut. there was
no comfort in doing so, as the ugly dreams
I had simply beggar description. The flesh
gradually fell away from my frame till my
bones were quite visible, and my prospects
were anything but rosy, especially as my
head had long since been troublesome.
Without any ostensible reason it used to
start beating violently against my ribs, the
sound it made being quite loud enough to
be heard several‘yards away. It seemed
more than likely that my heart was diseased,
particularly so as those fits of palpitation
were usually accompanied by feelings of
faintness. The worry of mind and pain of
body at last succeeded in wearing out my
patience, for it took but little to make me
irritable and excited, after which I sank
into the old gloomy groove of misery till
something else occurred to upset me. Ner-
vousness was not the least of my ailments,
as my nerves twitched involuntarily when-
ever ' nything happened to disturb me, and
in spite of the medicines I had taken, they
still trembled at the least provocation.”
“ Where did you get your medicine from,
Mr. Degnan ?” • f
“I refer- to the medicines which my
doctors prescribed for me, but which I
might as well have left untouched for all the
good they were. I was always paying
money away to doctors, and never got any
value back for it; but now if I should
have the ill-luck to fall into bad health
again I would go straight for Clements
Tonic, as I know it to be the btfst medicine
ever brought within the reach of suffering
humanity.”
“ That being your opinion, why did you
not take some earlier than you did ? ” -
“ Recause I had been suffering five years
before I was told about Clements Tonic,
and another reason was because I had de-
cided against taking any more medicine, as
I thought it was useless. But a relative
came upon the scene and told me how
Clements Tonic had cured his complaints,
which were like mine in nearly every detail;
so I promised to try it, and when I did so
it was the best day’s work I ever did, for
Clements Tonic piloted me to health. I
used to dread the very idea of taking any
food, but when I had been on Clements
Tonic a few days it was quite different, as I
was always the first to sit down to meals
and the last one to finish eating, and before
a week was over there was a great reduc-
tion in the severity of my pains in the
stomach. My food did not ferment and
turn to wind as in former years, but it
digested quite readily, and of course I got
the benefit of its The weary
feelings which had darkened my days were
wafted away by the invigorating qualities
of Clements Tonic, which likewise buckled
together my shattered nerves and ma le
them firm and true. The greatest surprise
I ever got in my life was when I discovered
what a splendid medicine Clements Tonic
was for promoting healthful sleep, and I
say ‘healthful’ advisedly, for there was no
languidness or depression on awaking, such
as is experienced after taking sleeping
draughts or other such means for producing
slumber. The anxious misgivings which I
had borne in silence when my heart was
given to palpitating were not long in being
dispelled, for Clements Tonic made that
organ beat with the regularity of a clock,
and I can make the same statement in
regard to my internal functions generally,
for I never suffered from constipation after
taking a few bottles of Clements Tonic,
which relieved my chest pains and head-
aches very easily. It was pleasant to get
up and notice that my tongue was clean and
that the bitter taste had left my mouth,
but what was still more to be appreciated
was the fact that every ache and pain had
deserted my body, which was regaining its
weight from day to day.”
“ Are you sure about the completeness of
your cure ? ”
“I am quite positive that Clements Tonic
made a new man of me by giving me better
health and more strength than I ever had
before. Not long ago I was helping to cut a
road through the mountains, about ten miles
from Nowra, and I found that I could do
the heaviest manual labour going without
being unduly fatigued, and, as Clements
Tonic gave me the health to do it, you can
publish my story in any way you want.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
i, J Ohs Degnan, of Garden-street, North Botany,
in the State of New South Wales, Commonwealth
of Australia, do solemnly and sincerely declare
that I have carefully read the annexed document,
consisting of five folios, and consecutively numbered
from one to five, and that it contains and is a
true and faithful account of my illness and cure
by Clements Tonic; and also contains my full per-
mission to publish in any way my statements—
which I give voluntarily, without receiving any
payment; and I make this solemn declaration con-
scientiously believing the same to be true, and by
virtue of the provisions of the Oaths Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Darlinghursh this twenty-
eighth day of February, one thousand nine hundred
and two, before me,
THOMAS MOORE. J.P.

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 14, 1903.
The Tasmanian Election.
Tasmania has a general election shortly, and
The Bulletin decides to give its vote for
Premier Lewis and Treasurer Bird and the
men in office. It doesn’t greatly admire
the people in office down at the little city of
the cobble-stones and the smells ; but taking
one thing with another they look a vast
deal better than the people out of office.
Tasmania has never yet raised a Ministry
over which any vigorous Democrat could be
wildly enthusiastic. Nature meant the island
to lead the Commonwealth in everything that
represents real progress, but for the time
being something has gone wrong with the
scheme. Tasmania should be another Maori-
land. It has a climate that is calculated to
produce a sturdy race—sturdy in mind and
body. It has scenery that is an inspiration ;
a good rainfall ; reasonably fertile soil;
and a rich supply of minerals. But it is
slow, insular, and sodden with imported pre-
judice ; it is haunted with a deep and horrible
respect for old tradition, and is strangely
reverential to anybody who has inherited a
griffin on a soup-plate for his coat of arms,
or anybody who can prove he had an ancestor
who did nothing, or a great deal worse than
that, in England very long ago ; it isn’t quite
sure even now that a dead crest isn’t better
than a live locomotive ; and its gods are
land, pedigree, vested interests, private
enterprise, and all the rags and appurten-
ances of the old ox-waggon order of things.
Tasmania certainly shows signs of beginning
to wake up, and its people, and the folk of
the Gippsland mountains and the N.S.W.
table-land, will do a vast amount towards
running the Commonwealth some day ; but
that day is not quite arrived yet.
* * * *
There are many things that The Bul-
letin does not hope for from the Lewis
Ministry. It ought to put on a graduated
land-tax that would strike the shameful
Y.D.L. Co.’s monopoly to the extent of 4d.
or 6d. in the £, and make that incubus cut
up its huge estate and clear out of the island
—and it won’t. It ought to abolish the
utterly ruinous habit of wasting about two-
thirds of the loan money on non-productive
public works, and it probably won’t do that
either. It ought to make a determined
effort to abolish its Upper House and
to pay its Lower House decently, and
though it certainly made an attempt to
close its Legislative Council, it presently
fainted by the way-side and left things as
they were. Still it appeals to the country
on a programme which looks quite as good
as anything likely to be found in Tasmania
just at present. The principal planks are
these :
Another attempt to be made at combining the two
Houses of. Parliament into one body ; failing that, a
reduction in the membership of one House.
No reduction in taxes till the deficit is paid off.
Borrowing to be on a very moderate scale, and only
“ for public works of a reproductive character, or for
such purposes as will secure undoubted national advant-
ages ” (which is painfully vague, and may mean anything
or not much),
A large amalgamation of local bodies (at present almost
every part of Tasmania is bossed by about six local bodies
of one kind or another.)
It isn’t a stirring programme, or one about
which any democrat can become largely
excited, but in a slow-going Tasmanian way
it is at least respectable.
• • * *
There is one point, however, on which the
Lewis Ministry can claim to have more
than negative virtues. When inter-State
duties were abolished the people were re-
lieved of a great load of taxation, and, inci-
dentally, the Treasury lost much money that
it could ill spare. Tasmania was already, by
many miles, the most cheaply-governed
State in Australasia, and it ranked among
the most cheaply-governed in the civilised
world. Nevertheless, the party which
opposes the Lewis Ministry, or a large sec-
tion of that party, demanded that the taxes
which had been abolished should not be re-
placed by others, but that the loss should be
made good by sweating, nigger-driving, and
general robbery of whomsoever could be
robbed with impunity. The Lewis Govern-
ment made some hard economies, but in
the main it refused to be the brutal and con-
scienceless sweater that its critics required.
It made new financial arrangements which,
there seems reason to believe, will make the
public accounts reasonably solvent hence-
forth, and most of the innovations consisted
in levying new taxes—a large proportion of
them on a class which can afford to do some-
thing to keep its State solvent. The bitterest
hostility manifested against Premier Neil
Lewis is because his crowd isn’ta worse sweater
than it is ; because it didn’t let the deficit
slide rather than raise more revenue ; and
because it has ideas of making even a slight
attack on the awful dignity of the Upper
House by proposing to make it and the
Assembly sit together and do with one
flunkey and one gas-bill. So if the
Cabinet of Lewis and Bird goes down it
seems more likely to go down by reason of
the good things it did or attempted (which
were not so very many after all) than by
reason of the things it neglected (which were
numerous). For these reasons The Bulle-
tin, if its address were Hobart, would give
the Lewis Government all the votes it could
raise on election day. A Government which
is violently assailed because it won’t sweat
more and rob harder deserves support.
• * * »
. The real awakening of Tasmania isn’t
likely to come either from the Lewis-Bird
combination or its opponents. The present
Ministry has its ox-cart pointed, to some ex-
tent, in the right direction, but an ox-cart
is a slow vehicle at the best. Tasmania has
almost the largest unproductive debt per
inhabitant in the civilised world. It has
an inferior education system, an anti-
quated Parliament, no State Land Bank,
no policy of breaking up big estates
for closer settlement, the most srnellsome
metropolis in Australasia, a petrified press,
a huge useless ballast of tradition, a grievous
antipathy to honestly paying its way, and a
profound faith in getting into debt as the
shortest and best road to affluence. The
best hope for it is that a time will come
when the Commonwealth Parliament will
impose a graduated land-tax which will kill
the island’s Y.D.L. Co. dead, will take over
its railways and extend them, take charge
of its education system, tax its absentees,
furnish it with a State Land Bank and a
closer-settlement system, take over its debt
and commence a vigorous system of repay-
ment, and take public affairs generally out
of the hands of the House of Dodery.
But these things haven’t arrived yet, and
meanwhile The Bulletin gives its vote
for Lewis and Bird, as representing
the more honest, more hopeful, and less
completely dead of the two parties which
practically make up Tasmanian politics.
The present Ministry has, at least, faced its
monetary difficulties much more courageously
than certain larger and more voluble com-
binations on this side, and that fact is some-
thing to its credit. It hasn't tried to borrow
its way wholesale out of its troubles like the
O’See Cabinet of N.S.W. ; nor tried to get
straight by cursing all and sundry like BkN¥ ;
nor gone hankering after new land-syndicate
steals like Jenkins Iscariot. And if it
hasn’t much of a policy, some at least of its
opponents seem to have much less of a policy
—and not so clean a one.
A Petrified Land.
We have in Victoria one of the richest countries in the
world, its progress brought almost to a standstill. Our
small population has practically ceased to grow .—Premier
Irvine of Victoria.
The above remark, or some remark very like it,
has grown to be quite common nowadays in
Australia. The State Premiers of Australia may
be roughly divided into two classes—those who
are searching for the cause of the present stagna-
tion and those who are searching for a loan ; and
neither sample seems to meet with any striking
success just at the moment. Though Prem
Irvine’s query applied only to Victoria, yet i
can find out the cause of the trouble there his
covery will apply almost equally to
Australia. There is a flatness m Ibvtne’s State
as well as in other States—an intangible idea that
the bottom has fallen out of something a lo -
opportunity feeling—a sensation of failure, or a
least a vague and shapeless impression that A -
tralia has not panned out as expected.
Irvine, who is paid to know what is wrong ana
to put it right, goes round asking wearily why
things don’t come right by themselves.
* • • *
Probably, to begin with, Australians expect too
much. During the ten years that elapsed between
the last two censuses the population of the
Commonwealth increased by 588,478. During the
nine months that elapsed between the taking ot
the last census and the end of 1901 the further
increase was estimated at 61,135, or at the rate ot
over 81,500 per annum. Every State had a share
in the movement. N.S.W.’s population increased
in that three-quarters of a year by 24,854; Queens-
land’s increased by 13,924; Westralias by 10,766 ;
Victoria’s by 7640; South Australia’s by 2196;A as-
mania’s by 1755. The three States with the large
increase of population were also the three huge
borrowing States, and owed their expansion
greatly to an artificial prosperity created by loans;
but even Victoria, which borrows less than one-
fifth as much in proportion to population as
N.S.W. does, exhibits a reasonable expansion of
population. Under the Federal tariff the popu-
lation of the Commonwealth has been growing 40
per cent faster than it did in the last decade
under the old separate system, and it is certainly
growing as rapidly as can be expected m the
case of an ill-governed, barren and drought-cursed
land which has sold the best of its public estate;
which has heaped up, in addition, the biggest un-
productive debt per head save one on this earth ;
and which lies so far from Europe that, even if
it had any special inducements to offer to white
immigrants, the cost of the voyage is, in many
cases, a serious difficulty.
* * * *
The population of Australia, of course, is not
increasing as it used to do. In the days when the
gold deposits were almost virgin, when the huge
loans were coming in and the huge tribute for
interest had hardly begun to go out, when the
scanty area of well-watered land was being sold
wholesale, when the <£130,000,000 which this
country owes to foreign creditors, apart from its
public debt, was being gaily borrowed, when
the West hadn’t yet been eaten up, and the
banks hadn’t burst —then population poured
in freely. That period will never come
back, but those who gabble about Australia’s
“splendid natural resources” insist on maintain-
ing an empty delusion, to the effect that the old
time of riot was a normal period, and because
things are not now as they were then, they suffer
from a vague sense of injury and a vague desire to
rend somebody. Also, they have a misty idea
that if payment of members, or something, could
be abolished, or if more people like the late
Frederick Sargood were put into Parliament,
the good old times would come back—the lands
could be sold over again, and the millions bor-
rowed over again, and the gold dug up over again,
and the banks could burst over again, and the
clock of time be put back generally.
• • • •
Premier Irvine’s pathetic inquiry as to what
is wrong with his particular State is not,
after all, so very difficult to answer. Victoria
had a stupendous increase of population
between 1881 and 1891, because in those years
it was willing to find work for everybody
who asked for it on building unprofitable rail-
ways with borrowed money. When the loan policy
burst, a great part of the multitude of people who
came to Victoria to build railways, and the like,
with borrowed money, naturally left again. They
went to the States where the loan policy was still
in the full blast of its iniquity—and when the
loan policy bursts in these States they will leave
them also, if they can find other places with a
loan boom to go to. At present this crowd isn’t
returning to Victoria—and it is well for Victoria
that it isn’t. Miners are not going to Victoria,
because the gold industry is a failing one. Factory
workers of various kinds come only in small num-
bers, because, though Victoria employs more
people in its manufactures than any other State,
the natural increase of population is sufficient to
keep its industries fully manned. Agriculturists
don’t come because, though there is still
much good land not closely settled, it is
kept locked up by selfish monopolists and
public enemies like the Clarkes and the
Chirnsides, and the new agriculturist finds it
very hard to get good soil to settle on except at
a ruinous price. As a certain incapable person
named Irvine refuses to make any serious
attempt to break up the big land-monopolies and
supply land for the people, the agricultural popu-
lation can’t increase very fast. And manufactures
don’t increase so fast as they might because the
agricultural population which would buy their
products is thus restricted. Also, railways don’t
pay in many cases because there is practically no
population along the line of route to make them
pay, and there can’t be any such population till
the big landlords are compelled to open up the
way for close settlement. And as there seems no
hope that the House of Landlords will do any-
thing to shift these big land-owners, or that the
Clarkes and Chirnsides will develop sufficient
patriotism to shift themselves to any great extent,
the best hope is that the Commonwealth Parlia-
ment will impose a graduated land-tax of such
vast dimensions that every one of them will fly to
cut up his land into small holdings and put them
on the market. That will set the whole machine
moving. It will bring population by thousands
to the bare, bald parts of Victoria ; and the popu-
lation will make the railways pay ; and it will pay
taxes which will enable Shiels, the Raven, to
square his financial statement; and it will make
a manufacturing boom by supplying more cus-
tomers for the factories; and the new rush of
manufacturers and operatives will supply more
taxes to gladden the heart of Shiels, the Raven.
But otherwise there is no visible hope save that
the country will plod along in the old groove
• • * •
Certainly even the old groove might be worse
In the ten years between 1891 and 1901—despite
the financial panic, the break-up of the great
insane loan policy, the drought, and the incapa-
city of various politicians, the population of
Victoria grew at the rate of 6000 a year It is
just now growing at the rate of about 10 200 a
year —nothing like the rate that might be
expected certainly, yet, after all, only four
European countries show a greater per-
centage of increase. No sane person expects
the old boom to come back anywhere in Aus-
tralia. This country burned the candle at both
ends in thirty years of stupendous recklessness
and ate up the prospects of a century in one wild
and whirling generation. But now that the candle
is burned, Australia sits about a great deal too
much mumbling over the ashes of the wick, and
asking where on earth the candle has gone to.
There is abundant room still for steady progress
if this country will make the best that can
be made of the small extent of good
soil it possesses. But there is so little that
it affords no space for big land monopolists,
and while they are allowed to block settlement,
and Victoria blandly offers the new arrival a bit
of dry Mallee scrub or a Gippsland mountain
top, it is to no purpose that Equity lawyer Irvine
addresses things in general and asks why things
in general don’t improve more rapidly.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Judicial Obiter Dicta.
In the performance of his actual duties the judge
should be so hedged in with reverence as to make
criticism impossible except under grave com-
pulsion. But what judges very generally
fail to understand is that, when they depart
from the actual business in hand—the expo-
sition of the law and the summing up of the
facts of a case —and wander vagrant in the field of
general comment, they are no longer entitled to
any special respect as judges, are indeed open to
the bluntest of criticism for going beyond their
duty, even if their obiter dicta are unexcep-
tionable in matter and expression. When, as
is often the case, their extra-judicial remarks
are foolish and objectionable, then the censure of
the public can be as weighty and as rude as the
circumstances demand, without its expression
being in any way derogatory to the dignity with
which a decent community will always surround
its judiciary. For the judge when making his
obiter dicta is no longer a judge, but a gentleman
foolishly stepping for the while out of his robes of
office to become a mere private citizen. Censure
should be expressed, and expressed firmly, until
the habit, that some judges have, of going out-
side their duties to air from the judgment seat
opinions which are often crude and nonsensical, is
cured; and if it is necessary to go to the extreme
step of degrading a judge before the evil practice
is abolished, the good attained will be worth
the cost. Judicial obiter dicta are of three
classes : (1) Humorous remarks, without any
particular offensiveness, made during the hearing
of cases (these are venial offences only, though
unwise, because judicial humor is usually of poor
quality, and its airing puts into the heads of many
of the community the idea that a court which
jokes with difficulty must also judge with
difficulty); (2) Opinions as to the merits of wit-
nesses or of litigants, which are not founded
surely on facts, but are flavored with bias (a
common judicial offence this—the Chief-Justice of
N.S.W. the other day branded a litigant publicly
as a liar on a hastily-formed opinion, and, though
circumstances have since shown the opinion to be
utterly wrong, the man injured has no remedy);
and (3) opinions quite foreign to the matter in
hand and expressing one-sided views on questions
of politics or sociology. The last are the most
mischievous of obiter dicta because they show a
judge, who should have a reputation for practical
infallibility, uttering opinions, often demonstrably
wrong, still more often wrong in the view of one
section of the community. The judge on the
Bench should have no pointedly-expressed humor,
no sect, no politics, no class bias, no views on
anything at all outside of the strict law and the
strict facts of the case before him. For in all these
things there is danger. N.S.W. Justice Prynnge,
airing his medieval, crusading-epoch frame of
mind about some question of politics, lays himself
open to a juryman arising and calling him a silly
old man, and adjuring him to mind his own busi-
ness ; and that would not be well for the judicial
system, which, in the community’s interest,
should be respected. But to be respected it must
be respectable; and to be respectable it must
avoid all occasions which bring it into derision.
N.S.W. Labor Conference, now sitting, has
decided to urge upon the Labor party of the State
to bring before Parliament two specially-glaring
cases of alleged judicial foolishness. Mr. Justice
Prynnge, during the course of that Griffith
libel action in which he—as Mr. Prynnge, not as
Justice Pring —made such a conspicuous idiot
of himself in matters of politics, is alleged by one
newspaper report to have also said :
The action of the Shearers’ Union during the recent
strike proves the necessity of a standing army to keep
workmen in their place.
It is open to doubt as to whether Mr. Prynnge
did make this remark ; it was not reported in the
Sydney daily papers. On much better authority,
Mr. Rogers, forgetting for the time that he was
Judge Rogers, is alleged to have said during a
recent case:—
We have seen a little of the shearers, for instance, and
how they behaved last season by endeavoring to take
advantage of employers during the drought. These men
do not command my sympathy, and I do not care if the
whole world knows it.
Probably Mr. Rogers did say this; it showed a
diseased self-esteem on his part, for Mr. Rogers’
opinion on the matter is of no more importance
than Mr. Smith’s or Mr. Brown’s ; it was, as
uttered from the Bench, a contempt of a higher
Court than that of Judge Rogers’ —the Industrial
Arbitration Court; and it was an utterly foolish
and misguided statement altogether. The Labor
party will do well to bring it before Parliament,
and with it the whole question of judicial conduct
on the Bench, which has of late been disfigured by
a good deal of foolish and strictly unnecessary
vagrant comment.

More About Capital Leaving the
Country.
Every now and then some scared Tory strikes the
alarming alleged fact that some State or other has
been paying off some of its debts, and warns the
public that this is another of the evil results of
socialistic legislation. Then some scared news-
paper rushes off and interviews some lawyer or
other who acts as agent for British money-lenders
here and makes a commission out of the country’s
embarrassments, and that non-productive person
speaks weighty words of warning. It is in conse-
quence of this habit that the following slush
appears in Melbourne Age :
However, the variations in the total assessments indi
cate that, while some years showed a gain and others a
loss, the general drift has been against the State to the
extent of more than £9,000.000 since 1895. That the
capital “driven out of the State,” as some say, has not
by any means been all sent back toEngland may be taken
for granted. Part of it doubtless has followed enterpris-
ing Victorians to other States and to other parts of the
world. Much of it, however, must have been withdrawn
by English investors.
The evidence as to the withdrawal of English money
from Victoria is strong. Mr. England, of the firm of Mal-
leson, England and Stewart, when seen on the subject
yesterday, said that since the income-tax on property,
ranging from Bd. to Is. 4d., was imposed, his finn has
remitted toEngland considerably more than £1,500,000,
withdrawn from investment here. He added : “We are
not now getting a penny from England for investment,
while, on the other hand, when money is repaid by the
borrower, we remit to Edinburgh. The investors expect
us to invest their money so as to return a certain rate of
interest with income-tax paid, and it is seldom now that


we can manage that. Yesterday we cabled £19,000 back
to Edinburgh, and, the other day, £55,000, and never a
month goes by without similar remittances having been
made. We ask only a moderate rate of interest, and the
highest we ever obtained was 6 per cent.” Mr. England
added that, if it were attempted to charge such a rate of
interest as would cover the tax, the money would not be
borrowed. There were, he said, so many charges upon
English money invested in Australia that an income-tax
ranging from Bd. to Is. 4d. made it practically as advan-
tageous for the investor to keep it at home. A day of
RETRIBUTION WOULD COME, HE ASSERTED, WHEN THE PEOPLE
WOULD FIND THE STATE DRAINED OF MONEY, AND WHEN IT
WOULD BE NECESSARY TO OFFER ATTRACTIONS FOR ITS RETURN
The income-tax was one of the chief causes of this deple-
tion, whereas in New South Wales the sixpenny tax was
not regarded as a heavy impost.
Another representative of English investors, Mr.
Riggall, of Blake and Riggall, holds very much the
same views as Mr. England. He says that having to pay
the two income taxes, English investors would not send
their money to Australia unless the transactions showed
a substantial margin. Of late years the margin in favor
of Victoria had been so small, owing to the income-tax,
that investors preferred to place their money at home’
His firm frequently received instructions from clients in
England to remit money invested in Victoria on its falling
due. Money was still going out of the State and would
continue to do so, not because it was not plentiful in
England, but because there was a lack of confidence in
local conditions and a feeling of irritation created by the
income taxes.
The experience of the two firms we have mentioned is
no doubt typical of that of others. Full inquiry into the
matter would probably show that such a revision of the
income taxes might be effected as would encourage again
the inflow of cheap money for the development of the
State's resources.
If it is true that, per medium of one firm of
Melbourne solicitors. Victoria has, since the
income-tax arrived in 189 G, paid off £1,500,000 of
its private foreign debt, then the shocking fact
that it has paid off. in all, £9,000,000 of private
debt since 1896, and thereby lamentably and sin-
fully saved about £500,000 or <£550,000 a year in
interest, may be quite reliable. But the Age,
instead of being overjoyed and lighting a bonfire
in Collins-street, is broken up and asks if the
income-tax can’t be readjusted so as to help the
State to get further into debt again. And the
Age perpetrates this shocking hunk of silliness in
spite of these facts :
(1) Victoria hasn’t lost any of its lands, buildings, rail-
ways, public works of any kind, financial institutions, or
any other visible property through the withdrawal of
this alleged total of £9,000,000, and the consequent
saving of £550,000 or so .yearly in interest.
(2) It isn’t any shorter of cash through it—the State’s
stock of money, as shown by the coin reserves in its
banks, hasn't diminished at all through the repayment
of this £9,000,000 of debt.
(3) Money is really more plentiful instead of less, as
evidenced by the fact that Victoria can now afford to re-
fuse with scorn to pay the high rate of interest demanded
by the British money-lender.
(4) The State’s manufactures have‘grown’very largely
since the income-tax was first imposed in 1896 and
capital began in consequence to leave the country, the
number of hands employed having increased by over
16,000. The population has increased. The people’s in-
comes have increased so greatly that the income-tax,
which yielded £170,000 in 1896, returned over £194,000 in
1901—despite the drought. The banks, which were little
more than wrecks in 1896, are mostly paying respectable
dividends in 1901.
(5) And though Victoria is said, since the accursed
income-tax came in force in 1896, to have exported
enough to pay off £9,000,000 of its private debts, still its
exports and imports almost exactly balance each other
just as they did before.
The Bulletin begs to move that, as things are
much better in Victoria since the foreign
capitalist (so it is alleged) began to get scared and
withdraw his money ; and seeing that Victoria is
alleged to have got rid of £9,000,000 of foreign
debt, and to be consequently saving £550,000 or so
per annum in interest, and yet has as much
money (as shown by the banking returns)
and more income (as shown by the income-
tax returns) than it had before ; and as
these things are alleged to arise from
Socialistic legislation—in view of all the circum-
stances it begs to move that Socialistic legis-
lation is obviously a very good thing indeed. It
quite understands that the two big firms of
solicitors above-mentioned are full of unreasoning
anger at the State paying off some of its foreign
private debt, seeing that they made a commission
on collecting the interest on that debt; and it
quite understands their endeavors to scare the
people into getting further into debt again and
threatening them with dire calamities if they don t.
They can’t be expected, by reason of the com-
mission, to take a patriotic or a common sense
view of the matter The Bulletin can take that
view. It makes no commission out of Australia’s
embarrassments.

Buying 1 up a Parliament.
Melbourne Age has, for a dozen years or more,
been demanding vaguely that “ something ”
should be done to the Victorian House of Land-
lords. This (Feb. 5) is its latest :
Possessing an entrenched position, which it well under-
stands, and wielding a power of absolute veto without
the smallest responsibility, there is no reason why the
Council should profess any coyness about its procedure.
It can do as it likes, and there is no one to call it to
account. Having, therefore, as a matter of form passed
the second reading of the Reform Bill, after indicating
that the measure is bad in every part, it now proceeds to
tear it up and cast the pieces in the face of the Assembly.
It will have no effective reduction of Council members ;
no appeal to the people ; no dissolution ; no joint
sitting ; no woman suffrage, and no effectual reduction
in the qualifications of members. All this has been made
tolerably clear during the course of the second-reading
speeches. It was made clearer last night. If there is to
be any reduction of Parliament, and any reform, it must
be of such a pattern as will decrease the weight of the
Assembly and give the Council a proportionate increase
in the balance of things. ... It now becomes the duty
of Ministers to determine what course they will pursue
in these circumstances. Is the country to be nothing,
and the Council everything ? Mr. Irvine gained his
position on his Reform policy. How is he going to keep
it ?
And if the Age can’t tell Irvine how to keep it,
then why pay a penny for that effete organ ?
In an ideal Commonwealth, whenever a paper has
talked three times about what shouldn’t be done
in a certain case, without once suggesting what
should be done, the responsible person will
be fined £lOO and costs for being a useless and
unresourceful blatherskite. The cheapest kind of
journalism in this world is the kind which says
that something is wrong and somebody should do
something unspecified to put it right. It is a
mean dodging of responsibility, for it leaves it
open to the paper to denounce every attempt that
is made to put the evil right on the ground that it
is the wrong kind of attempt, and to conceal
behind a veil of haughty reticence the fact that it
hasn’t the least idea itself what should or could
be done under the circumstances. There are
four courses open with the Victorian House
of Landlords: To turn its flank by systematically
transferring all possible authority to the Common-
wealth Parliament, which is a slow process at
best; to abolish it by revolution, which is
gory, noisy, destructive and illegal—and besides
it would be necessary to import a practical revolu-
tionist from South America to conduct the busi-
ness ; for the Assembly to go on strike and stop
the whole machine of State till the Council passes
the Bill for its own abolition, which would be
expensive but pretty certain ; and to tack on to
the Bill for the complete abolition of the Council
thfdltanfft eac h ML C ; who held a seat at
the date of the said abolition should receive a
hfe-penaon of £lo°o a year, as a mark of the
worth f gratltude * or hls serv ices. It would be
worth far more than ten times £48,000 a year
to get rid of the Council, and the device
