The Return of^thTowe’Sullivan.
There is a streak of real pathos about the return
ot the Prodigal Owe’Sullivan from Westralia.
VVhen he left the East to go and help to turn on
, be water at Coolgardie, the great financial balloon
was beautifully globular, and looked, to all out-
ward seemmg, as good as ever. He returned to
tind it burst and almost fiat. In the few weeks
ot his absence the last straw was put on the
camels back, and the long-enduring animal sagged
down wretchedly in the middle till its stomlch
trailed on the ground. In short, the loan money
gave out, and the great N.S.W. Public Works
boom, if itisnt dead, is at least half-dead, and
the date of its recovery can’t be foretold. The con-
dition of the N.S.W. finances at the end of January
was shocking m the extreme. The income for the
seven months of the financial year which were
completed on Jan. 31, was £927,588 less than ex-
penditure, or including sums paid but not yet
I*l rtlfLi 0 the accounts, the shortage was over
£1,000.000. All the trust funds (about £5,900,000)
have been borrowed, or overdrafts established
against them. The State has still a large sum to
pay for the Sydney land-resumptions. It has bor-
rowed back again at 4 per cent, a large amount of
the money already paid for resumed properties.
It is borrowing locally as much money as it can
get at 3£ per cent., but all it gets is only a drop
in the bucket. At the end of January it found
that the revenue was dropping heavily, and
that this month’s expenditure was £220,000
more than the income, and that it hadn’t
two coppers of its own to jingle on the tombstone
of its deadest ancestor. It was then that the
camel sagged down in the middle, and the balloon
burst, and the light went out, and the house that
was built on the sands began to totter horribly.
The See Government started to cut down loan-
expenditure desperately ; it announced a policy of
violent retrenchment; it, to all appearance, post-
poned the “finest rail way-station in the world ”
indefinitely ; it began to reduce the railway staff
whose wages it raised so gaily a little while ago ;
and it even dropped hints of disrespect at the 7s.
per day wage. Minister Owe’Sullivan was
in the very act of telling the Westralians
how much he admired them because, in pro-
portion to population, they “ blewed ” loan money
even more freely than he did, when these things
happened, and he left for the East with his ears
streaming behind him to see what had gone
wrong. It would take a four or five million loan
to set the boom really going again; and if N.S.W.
asked for any such amount just at present, either
the interest offered at this end would be very
high, or else the language used at the other end
would be very bad. So at present there is gloom,
and .John See’s under-jaw sticks out like the fire-
box of a locomotive, and Owe’Sullivan hasn’t
promised a single new railway since he came back,
and the department-trotting member drags his
dejected tail behind him so that it leaves a furrow
in the dry road along which he returns to his con-
stituents to tell them that the new culvert is un-
obtainable. And N.S.W., which rather likes its
Owe’Sullivan, as a cheerful public lunatic and an
optimist of the wildest description, feels sorry,
after a fashion. Also it feels glad after a fashion’
for the last chance that that shameless cadger,
Law, M.L.A., might get £75 for the Balmain
Choral Union is now dust.
The Want of Sufficient Cursing’.
At last the cause of the Victorian railway deficit
has been discovered, for the Irvine Government
has put a long pale cold finger on the spot and
identified it. It is want of sufficient cursing,
yelling and sacking on the part of the bosses that
makes things go wrong. At present a railway
officer has certain rights of appeal, and can’t be
bounced out instantaneously, and that unfortunate
fact strikes Premier Irvine thus (Age report):—
The Government were now brought face to face with
the great problem of railway management. He insisted
on the necessity of studying every phase of the subject
in order that the railways might be managed on true
business principles. Whilst they should under proper
management return large profits they now constituted a
serious drain on the resources of the State. He attributed
the mismanagement to the fact that it was a case of
servants without a master. He maintained that until the
person or persons in charge of the railways had dis-
cretionary power to dispense with the services of an
incompetent person instead of having to prove charges
asminst him, the railways would be non-paying for all
time.
Premier Irvine, having discovered why the Vic-
torian railways don’t pay working expenses and
full interest, proposes to put things completely
right by giving to the various bosses of the service
large powers to curse and sack the rank and file at
the shortest notice. The old circumlocutory
methods are to vanish. When anything goes
wrong the new variety of railway boss will just
rush out of his office, seize the supposed offender
by the collar, and say: “ Look here, d you
all the way to and back again, you d
idiot, take your dashed hat and get out of this.”
Then the offender, if he is large enough, will hit
the boss hard in the eye and go. There will be
no inquiry, no worry, no appeal—only a departing
employee, and a boss with raw steak on his eye
screaming for the police. And when things are
fixed in this fashion all is to be well. The cause of
the deficit, it appears, doesn’t lie in the fact that
many of the railways were built in regions where
there is hardly any population ; that they were
built with money borrowed at absurdly high
interest ; that they were loaded with a ridiculous
amount of debt through paying fraudulent land-
owners three times the fair value of their land
when it was resumed ; that they have perforce to
carry the burden of a very heavy charitable ex-
penditure in the shape of utterly unpayable
“ starving stock rates ” instituted for the benefit
of distressed farmers. No country Qutside Aus-
tralia, which has such a fearful amount of railway
for so few people, makes its rai 1 ways pay even so
well as Victoria does, though most countries in a
similar position have propped up their railway
systems with stupendous land-grants, while
the Victorian lines have to work entirely
**on their own,” but even that doesn’t
matter according to the Irvine theory. With
sufficient cursing and sacking, Irvine and co.
hold that all difficulties can be overcome —the old
rotten engines will cease to break down, and the
old rusted rails will no longer spread, and things
m general will go smoothly. When the hopeless
old locomotive sticks up its train on a steep bank,
Bent will just run behind, and blast vigorously,
and threaten the engine with the immediate loss
of its billet, and it will make a special effort and
get up. Plainly the Irvine-Bent Government
hasn’t realised yet that a railway isn’t exactly the
same thing as a working bullock —still in one sense
it is wise in its generation. It recognises its own
limitations. It recognises also the thing it is best
fitted for. It has no constructive ability, and
little capacity for administration, but at cursing
its subordinates and running them off the premises
it is unequalled, and it has decided to stick to the
thing it is best fitted for and let other things go.
Some Regrets and a Consolation.
N.S.W. State Labor Party Conference, which
has just brought its discussions to a close, covered
an extraordinary amount of ground in debate. It
is impossible to imagine a reform idea (with the
exception of honest finance) which was not in some
way canvassed. This wide diffusion of energy
was a mistake. A political party should be first
and foremost a practical organisation. Its business
is to lay down a course of action for the immediate
future and to let the possibilities of next decade
and next century look after themselves. Queens-
land Labor party for many years did grave
harm to its prospects by looking too far ahead
and pinning itself down to a declaration in
favor of “Socialism in our time.” N.S.W.
Labor Party Conference was not so foolish as
that; and is to be credited with showing sound
common-sense and sturdy devotion to the radical
cause in most of its resolutions. Still, the mis-
take of grasping at more than seems immediately
tangible was made; and The Bulletin— -hoping,
sometimes a little despairingly, that the Labor
party would shape itself to be the genuine reform
party in N.S.W. State politics—regretfully
notices the fact. A more serious mistake was the
rejection of a sound proposal from the Darlington
League that M’s.P. not entering the Assembly
under the Labor party’s banner, but willing to
subscribe to its principles and pledges, should be
allowed to join the caucus. It is not possible to
see that any harm could come of this ; the change
would probably lead to a more rapid growth of
the Labor party by converting constituencies
through their sitting members; and in cer-
tain easily-imagined circumstances it might
prove to be of the utmost value to
the Labor cause. The Owe’Seeulliv an power is
already crumbling ; in this or the next Parliament
it will probably break up. Then a successor will
be found either in a Labor Government of the
Ballance type, or in a Tory anti-Labor coalition.
If the Labor party in Parliament at such a crisis
could draw into its fold all unattached Radicals
and Democrats in the Assembly, very possibly it
would be able to secure the reins of power; other-
wise it could not do so. The Labor Conference
threw cold water on a suggestion which was of
great practical value. The dregs of the qld anti-
Federal spirit, which, by practical experience, has
been purged entirely from the Federal Labor
party, still cloud the mind of the N.S.
Wales Labor party —as shown by a reso-
lution of the Conference to strike out of the
Federal platform a demand for an amendment of
the Constitution to allow of Federal control of
labor legislation. It is a matter of absolute cer-
tainty that the only way by which several indus-
trial reforms, now pressingly needed, can be
secured, and others retained, is through Federal
action. It is possible that in N.S.W. the Labor
party will continue to have a dominant voice in
the Legislature, but the other States are already
given up to Tory reaction, and N.S.W. will not
be able to preserve liberal industrial legislation,
under inter-State Freetrade, if her neighbors
are under the thumb of the sweater. The
N.S.W. worker cannot, therefore, even if he
wishes to do so, safely take the selfish
view that the other States can be left to
their fate. If he has not enough of the broad
Australian spirit to wish for his fellow-Australians
an arbitration law, old-age pensions, a minimum-
wage regulation, and so on, he can, looking at the
matter from the merely narrow provincial point
of view, make certain that if, in Victoria, the
sweater gets a free run, N.S.W. won’t be long
able to continue to make concessions to the
workers involving an increase in the cost of pro-
duction. Uniform Federal legislation on labor
matters—such legislation as the liberal tone of the
Federal Parliament promises—is the only hope of
the social reformer in Australia just now, and
N.S.W. Labor Conference was most misguided in
declaring against that principle. Yet another dis-
appointing fact was the failure of the Conference to
deal adequately with the financial problem created
by reckless borrowing. The Labor party in N.S.W.
is credited, rightly or wrongly, with a full sympa-
thy with the policy of reckless borrowing. But
that is a policy which is even now drifting on the
rocks, and which a party wishing for power
should speedily dissociate itself from. All the
fiery radicalism in the world won’t serve a cent’s
worth as political capital on the fast-approaching
day when Uncle Bull insists on a stoppage of
borrowing, and when a party of strict economy
must, perforce, take the reins. That party could
be the Labor party. But if the Labor party
doesn’t choose to be the honest-finance party,
then it will be an anti-Labor party. In
Maoriland the Labor members associated
themselves with Ballance and honesty,
and they prospered. In N.S.W. if they
bind themselves to the car of the prodigal
Owe’Sullivan they must go down to the shades
with him ; and they are, in the public repute,
bound to that car, until they show otherwise very
clearly. However, in all political disappointments
—and some of the acts of commission and
omission of the N.S.W. Labor Conference are very
serious disappointments—there is this consolation,
that a way out of it all exists : the abolition of all
State Parliaments and parties and the absorption
by the Federal Legislature of the whole business
of governing Australia.

The back-number, Brisbane Courier, has lately
been once more heaping scorn on the auti-Jingo
party in England under the heading “The Little
Englander Again.” After all, only time will show
whether the Little Englander or the Bloated Eng-
lander is right. There was probably a Little
Macedonian once who objected to his country
wasting its energy in the insane attempt to con-
?uer Western Asia and Northern Africa and
ndia ; and a Little Swede who had doubts about
the boundless scheme of wiping out Russia,
Poland, and Saxony ; and a Little Spaniard who
thought the scattering of his country’s energies in
conquering South, Central and half North
America was too vast an undertaking ; and a
Little Paraguayan who fancied Carlos Solano
J'OJ’EZ a brilliant madman when he at-
tacked Brazil and Argentina single-handed,
ihere may even have been a Little French-
man who thought Napoleon I was taking
on too large an order; and a Little Epirot
who considered that Pyrrhus, when he went for
Kome, was biting off more than he could chew;
and there were Little Englanders in the back-
a £ es who fancied that the annexation
of r ranee was too large a spadeful to dig up all at
once. Possibly there were Little Persians 2000
years ago, who fancied that the great Darius was
reaching out for too much of the globe at once
when he led his army on its fatal tramp through
all southern Russia. All these little characters
proved to be right in the end, and even the Little
Englander may be vindicated some day.

The Bulletin is very tired of the correspondence
it receives about military affairs. The average
mtter starts by saying we should imitate the
Boers and if every able-bodied Australian is
willing to be enrolled for military service, and to
keep his gun ready, and Australia could go out
and fight the Zulus for 40 years by way of getting
its hand in, that would be possible. The rest of
the theory appears to be that there should be no
general and no discipline, that everybody should
write to the papers, and that the country should
produce a military genius when he is wanted.
The Bulletin’s opinion is that there is no de-
finite recipe for saving the country; but the best
it can propose is that; we should have a good large
citizen soldiery that can shoot well and march
well, our own arms and ammunition factory (well
inland), a cheap uniform, no rooster’s tail in the
hat, some kind of a general to struggle along with
when the war comes till the military genius
evolves, and that we should keep a civil tongue in
our head and not say “ blanky ” any more than is
absolutely necessary. Generally, a good deal of
damage is done before the military genius turns
up, but that can’t be helped ; a long war usually
seems to begin with a fool in command, and if the
country has luck it grows a better man after-
wards. There is no visible remedy for this. Two
cwt. of military correspondence is now dropped
into the basket.
Premier Irvine dropped a hint when discussing
the Victorian Railway Bill which shows that the
ugly scheme for selling or leasing the lines isn’t
dead yet. These are his words, as reported by
the Argus :
We are still endeavoring to achieve something which
has been tried and abandoned by almost all other coun-
tries, namely, the carrying on of a huge railway system
under national or State control.
The principal countries that own State railways
are Germany, Russia, Austria, Portugal, Sweden,
Norway, Denmark, Holland, Belgium, Switzer-
land (which has been buying out its private com-
panies), the Danubian States, India, Egypt, Cape
Colony, Natal, Queensland, N.S.W., victoria,
South Australia, Westralia, Tasmania, and
Maoriland—a pretty long list. There are several
in the category that have abandoned private
for State ownership, but very, very few that have
reversed the process, and if Irvine really made
the statement that the Argus debits him with it
was just a lie —and a very mean specimen at that.
Mr. Ezra Tomlinson, authorised delegate from
the Maoriland Drapery Prohibition League to
Australia, has arrived at Sydney to inaugurate
in the Commonwealth a crusade against the
horrors of the drapery trade. In an interview, he
corrects the wrong impression existing that the
league objects to female clothing of all descrip-
tions. Whilst resolute to abolish all millinery
and decorative drapery, the league is prepared to
allow a covering garment to women. A Bill for
an Act to Abolish the Manufacture and Importa-
tion of Drapery has been drafted by the league,
and in the schedules thereto the nature and sub-
stance of the covering allowed to women is
defined as follows :
Schedule A (referred to in clause 14 of the Act) : “One
appropriate garment” shall mean a single gown, covering
from the neck to the heels, plainly spun from cotton or
wool, with two full-length sleeves, hemmed where neces-
sary, but not adorned in any way with insertions, ribbons,
laces, feather-stitchings, tuckings or frillings, and to be
of the one color throughout.
This, Mr. Ezra Tomlinson points out, meets all
reasonable requirements. By strict administra-
tion of the Act it would be possible to guard
against surreptitious attempts at adorning the
female garment, or efforts to secure a fashion-
able cut. If necessary the garments could
be made in a gaol factory as an addi-
tional precaution against coquetry. The pro-
posals of the League, Mr. Tomlinson says,
may seem extreme, but in view of the desolation
of happy homes, the suicides, the fostering of vice,
the ruin of young children caused by the intoxica-
tion of the female mind with drapery and
millinery, sternly repressive measures are justified.
Mr. Tomlinson, who is a gentleman of a markedly
thoughtful type, with thin blue eyes and a faint
halo of pale hair, takes pride in the fact that he
has never assisted to debauch the mind of a woman
with so much as the present of a ribbon. His
mission in the Y.M. and W.C.A. buildings com-
mences shortly.

Some Prohibition facts cited by Bishop Potter at
a recent American diocesan conference :
In one of the farming districts of the Middle West,
where local sentiment has pronounced strongly against
any traffic in fermented liquors, it was noticed that
drunkenness of a particularly violent and insanity-pro-
ducing kind was largely on the increase among the farm
laborers. Ultimately the explanation was found in the
fact that the craving forstimulants had led to the tapping
from the silos of the vegetable juices of ensilage, which
were in a condition of alcoholic ferment, and gave a drink
not much less efficacious than fusel oil in maddening
stimulation.
In Vermont, a Prohibition State, Dr. A. P. Grinnel, of
Burlington, made a critical investigation of the consump-
tion of stimulants in that State, chiefly the narcotic
drugs, and of so-called medicines into which such drugs
enter as components of chief efficacy, which is extremely
instructive. Only part of the dealers responded to the
inquiry ; from those that did it was learned that they
dispense every month, of morphine, paregoric, or
laudanum, what is equivalent to 3,300,000 doses of opium
—the standard for a dose being one-eighth grain of
morphine, one-half ounce of paregoric, and twenty drops
of laudanum. The amount thus reported, which by no
means covers the total sales, would give a full dose of
opium daily for half the population of the State.
Moral: prohibit alcohol and you make the people
drink silo juice and eat opium.

“The Geisha” represents the half-way
house between comic opera and the alleged
“musical comedies” of “The Messenger Boy ”
and “Chinese Honeymoon” type—not altogether
decayed, good fruit enough for those whose taste
is for medlars, with some story and a great
amount of melody, the original sources of
which it would not be difficult to find. At
its original production in Sydnev, “The Geisha”
had a warm welcome, and the present revi-
val at the Palace—in some respects showing
distinct improvement—is probably just as attrac-
tive, in spite of the marked unsuitableness of
Miss Florence Young for the chief part. This
season of the Royal Comic Opera Co., which
will draw to a close with the run of “ The Geisha,”
lias been marked by excellent stage management
and great finish in the detail work; but, on the
whole, has shown some failures in regard to the
principals. There is a big gap between Thorley
and Roberts of to-day and Ryley and Bracy of
old times; and Miss Carrie Moore more often
fails than not nowadays. But Miss Florence
Young has been conspicuously successful in every
role until O Mimosa San, for which she is in
figure and voice and manner too robust. Even
her clear, strong vocalisation is, in a way, a dis-
advantage for the role: there should be something
of the languor of drowsy Asia in a Geisha’s
singing.
• • • *
George Lauri’s Wun-hi in “The Geisha”
is one of his best comedy roles, and his broadly
humorous characterisation of the bland and sinful
Chinaman was, perhaps, the most popular feature
of the play on Saturday. Miss Carrie Moore
does very well indeed as Molly Seamore,
singing and dancing brightly. Mr. Hugh
Ward is, however, not very well placed as
Marquis Imari, and Miss Ghiloni fails to
give any special distinction to the part of
Lady Constance Wynne. As Reginald Fairfax,
Mr. Thorley plays and sings with a bright breezi-
ness which very much helps the play to success.
“The Geisha ” will be played until the transfer of
the co. to Melbourne, when two new pieces—
“ The Emerald Isle ” and ‘ ‘ The Country Girl ”
will be produced.
** • *
Most important musical event of these
dog-star days in Sydney has been the re-entree of
Amy Castles, bracketed with the debut of her sister,
Dolly. Dolly proves to be another lyric soprano,
with resonant brilliant upper notes, and an attrac-
tive platform presence. The lucky sisters were
supported by tenor Charles Rose, and baritone
Needham. Latter always secures an affectionate
hearing, by reason of his Scotch songs. Mr. Rose
has a good style and a figure that shows off even-
ing dress to advantage. Miss Freda Miller, a
Kellerman pupil, made a successful debut as
pianist.
*# * #
The Fisk Jubilee Singers are giving Satur-
day Pops at Sydney Town Hall, and, judging by
the results achieved so far, the speculation is a
good one. The Town Hall is intensely respect-
able, and serious families who shun the theatre
feel that they can go there and not have the hem
of their propriety sullied or the tail of their
morality smudged. And at that big building one
can pose as a patron of musical art for Is., which
couldn’t be done in a theatre except up in the
gallery, where Bill has his eyrie and Ginger con-
gregates along with ’Lizer. Nobody can prove
that one wasn’t dragged to the shilling establish-
ment by an intense love of music, instead of by a
sordid desire to save the other 4s. And the Pops
are good value for the money; in fact, they would
be good value for more than that —as to exactly
how much more the writer is undecided, but in
mentioning eighteenpence be feels he is on per-
fectly safe ground.
** * *
“ The Fortune Teller ” is getting near the
end of its season at Sydney Royal, and the in-
visible Austrian army will shortly be bashed by
the invisible Hungarian army for the last time,
this result being attained through the gallantry of
Fedor, who doesn’t appear at all, but is only
spoken about. The advt. still preserves a dignified
reticence as to what will happen next at the
Royal.
** * *
Bland Holt’s “ Breaking of the Drought ”
shows signs of breaking up at last, the “Cotton
King ” being announced as its successor at Sydney
Lyceum. The drama of the dead and scorched
horse and the burnt-up selection will, accordingly,
soon give place to some newer form of distress.
** * *
“The Lady Barmaid” has not proved a
startling success at the Criterion, so on Saturday
Sheridan revives “Fun on the Bristol,” which is
alleged to have made Edward and Alexandra
laugh. _ “The Lady Barmaid ” boasted a nice girl
beer-slinger, and another not so nice; also a
dreadful American lady journalist who earned
elaborate frocking by travelling about and writing
nothing; and other lady journalists, who travel
about and write a great deal, and earn much
cheaper frocks thereby, viewed her with envy,
disfavor, and uncharitableness. The “Bristol”
brand of humor, ancient as it is, will probably be a
change for the better.
* * * 3*
Sydney Tivoli still relies on Jacques Inaudi
as its star. G. W. Hunter, a London comedian,
makes a welcome reappearance, and nobody is
tired of Mr. Maccann’s concertina-playing yet.
A new attraction is being prepared for this
theatre a game of “ Push-ball,” played
by girls. With a view, possibly, to economy,
the game will not start until the weather is a
little cooler. Girls going in for violent athletics
these dog days would probably, like the lamb in
the menagerie “happy family,” have to be re-
newed occasionally. A ball, of such vast dimen-
sions that if it was planted on the pavement The
Bulletin’s business-manager could just about see
over it, is used in the game.
This is the third week of “Zaza ” at Mel-
bourne Princess’s, and the house is pretty well
crowded every night in all parts except the d.c.
There has been some talk of dropping the feeble,
drab-colored fifth act from the crimson body of the
“ Zaza ” narrative, but this managerial idea would
be too rough on the refreshment-bars. The fourth
act, wherein the angry heroine complains that
Bernard doesn’t love her because he grows
savage when he suspects her of making
ructions in his domestic circle, is the hottest
and most thirsty act of the whole drama, and
the succeeding interval provides an earnest
rush for drinks. Besides, “Zaza” has been
shortened sufficiently to admit of time for a vir-
tuous fifth act. _ It could be curtailed a little
more by the excision of an antique lachrymose
duke, who proposes to purchase Zaza’s endear-
ments on liberal terms when he hears of her
trouble with Bernard, True, Zaza’s indignant
dismissal of the dook is much approved by the
amphitheatre. Nevertheless, it is a paltry bid for
applause. Considering that the sight of the dreary
nobleman always makes Zaza’s blood run cold, her
attitude of high morality when she orders him off
the premises does not call for special praise. On
the contrary, it tends to weaken the effect of her
subsequent similar attitude towards the other
man who had made her blood run 110 degrees in
the shade.
* * * *
Her Majesty’s is closed for three weeks,
to recover from the shock of the unlamented
panto. On Feb. 28th, the No. 1 Comic Opera Co.
returns to Melbourne, with intentions which seem
to be wrapped in a thin veil of unnecessary
mystery.
* * * *
Anderson’s telephone wire and Indian
gridiron drama is nearing its latter end. On
Saturday (14th) a drama entitled “In the Shadow
of Night ” will have a first Australian production
at Melbourne Royal, if the advt. speaks truthfully.
Present scribe regrets to notice that advts. as a
rule do not tell the truth when they announce
anything “ for the first time in Australia.” The
managerial habit nowadays is to try the effect of
a new show upon the dog in some place which
isn’t Sydney or Melbourne. Having pleased the
dog more or less, the show is qualified to make a
long series of first Australian appearances.
* * * *
Miss Gracie Emmett, and an imported
comedy company of four people in all, came to
Melb. Opera House last Saturday and played a
brief variation on the old reliable theme of
Widow O’Brien. Miss Emmett’s cheerful little
farce presents the same old Irish lady as a bona
fide woman instead of a Johnny Sheridan in
disguise. Also the Irish lady’s name was Murphy
(which is one of J. S.’s female names) before she
married a wrecky dyspeptic of comparatively
tender years. Miss Emmett plays the cen-
tral character cleverly, whilst the three other
people amount to an ample support. Fred.
Poplar and his unsober effigy from the tailor’s
shop are back at the Opera House. And
John Coleman is doing what he did of yore, with
the only difference that he does it rather better.
This country will seemingly have to wait a long
time for a new comedian who can approach Cole-
man in the art of being unaggressively comic.

as a junior “Princess of Song ” in the advt.) will
be joined together in two more Castles concerts at
Melb. Town Hall. The concerts are dated Feb.£
14th and 18th, and numerous depots for sale o
day tickets have been opened in sundry places-

At Adelaide Tivoli the show has been
strengthened by six Montroses from Austria, who
do athletic work, without apparatus, on the same
lines as the Cragg family, and may fairly be com-
pared with them. Seeley and West are taking in
Broken Hill before Adelaide, but the lady net-
ball players, after several weeks’ hard practice on
the stage, have at last been pronounced fit and
well for public performance; the fixture is S.A.
v. Victoria. At the Royal the Dampiers are con-
cluding their long and (except when it encoun-
tered a heat-wave at the end of Jan.) successful
season. Recent productions have included “ East
Lynne (with “apotheosis”), a detective drama,
with Alfred D. in three parts, and a gloomy hash-
up of events, entitled “ The Stranglers of Paris.”
Wirth’s circus began its country tour badly with a
performance in which the staging twice collapsed,
whereby the Naicest people of Moonta (or, at any
rate, the most expensively-seated) were deposited
on the ground to the number of 500 in all.
A married man, named Bennah (it is Bernard in
the French),
Whose artless wife had left him unprotected for
a while,
Was led into temptation by a tricky, stagey
wench.
But he battled like a hero ’gainst her fascinating
smile.
The subtle spells of Zazzacouldn’t conquer him at
first—
There never was a Bennah half so adamant as
this.
Then, at last, his virtue faltered, and his resolu-
tion burst
When she taught him all the meaning of the
Zazza-Zazza Kiss.
What ho ! the Kiss of Zazza (duly mentioned in
the ad.,
A special line inserted in the Argus and the
Age)
It warmed the soul of Bennah, and it drove him
fairly mad ;
It beat all osculations on the lurid lyric stage.
The victim took his nectar with a married, manly
zest,
In the matter of responses he was never found
remiss j
With the siren’s arms around him, and her head
upon his breast,
He revelled in the sweetness of the Zazza-Zazza
Kiss.
The pair were very happy for a half-a-year or so,
And then the hot enchantress got on Madame
Bennah’s track,
And the good old guilty passion brought its
punishment of Woe,
For that jealous ardent actress had the monkey
on her back.
So the raging lovers parted on the summit of a
“ scene,”
Amidst the scattered fragments of their transcen-
dental bliss;
The woman fed her conscience with regrets for
what had been,
Whilst the man retained a craving for the
Zazza-Zazza Kiss.
Time sped, and then he sought her in her prima-
donna pride.
She chilled her former lover with a meek, decep-
tive gaze
(Had Bennah boldly hugged her he would not
have been denied,
But common-sense is vetoed in these sentimental
plays).
She told him she must leave him, and, ye gods !
he let her leave ;
The serpent writhing near them hissed a disap-
pointed hiss.
Here the poet drops exhausted by his efforts to
conceive
The sultry hidden meaning of that Zazza-Zazza
Kiss.

New South Wales grows more and more conspicuous as
the State with the great boss deficit of Australasia. And
the above isn’t the whole deficit; if sums paid but not yet
brought to account are reckoned, the seven months’
shortage is over £1,000,000. The land and income tax
receipts are yet to come in, which is something;
but, on the other hand, the railway revenue is
beginning to drop heavily. Also, it seems inevit-
able that the loan expenditure must be cut
down from sheer want of money ; and, if so, the
general revenue, which has been inflated through the
State being in a condition of artificial prosperity caused
bv the lavish scattering of loan money, must almost cer-
tainly droop also. As a case in point : The loan expendi-
ture for January was cut down so far that it was £308,000
less than in January, 1902. And the general revenue
promptly responded by showing a decline of £83,411 as
compared with January, 1902. It looks as if only hard
and bitter economy could make ends meet when the
financial year closes on 30th June—and whoever heard of
such a thing from the O’See Ministry.

The New Zealand Loan and Mercantile
Agency 00. (head otbce, London), did a little better for
the year ended June, 1902, than in the previous one, but
still it is only able to pay interest on its first and second
debentures and an infinitesimal sum on account of the
interest on the third debentures. The only year in which
the third debenture-holders received their full interest
was 1900, and when the amount paid then is compared
with the amount paid in other years it will be seen how
badly the third-debenture people have come off in these
other years:

The increase of profit isn’t much, and the position can’t
be said to have materially improved. If it could have
been arranged that the reserve should be separately in-
vested and allowed to accumulate at compound interest,
apart from the general business, a fund would have been
growing which might some day cover all deficits and put
the Loan and Mercantile on its feet again. Unfortunately
no such arrangement exists.
The New Zealand Loan and Mercantile Agency
00. looks about as hopeless as any reconstructed insti-
tution could do while still remaining afloat. It has
written off almost all its capital (considerably over
£1,000,000) and all its old reserves, and (nominally) nearly
a million and a half of its debentures. But, as some com-
pensation to the debenture-holders, they received £lO,OOO
worth of preference shares which carry a claim (when it can
be met) to UK) per cent, dividends and other privileges.
If the profits increase sufficiently to pay the interest on
all the debentures (which would require about £14,500
over and above present earnings), and to show a steady
margin in addition, the preference shares are entitled
to a dividend of £4 per share (£40,000). They are also
entitled to 60 per cent, of any further profits. There-
fore, even if the earnings went up to £200,000 a year and
remained there, there would be less than £lB,OOO avail-
able (so far as it is possible to decipher so complicated an
arrangement) for dividend to ordinary shareholders, for
building up a reserve, for the repurchase and extinction
of nearly £3,000,000 worth of debentures, and to provide
against further depreciation. There is nominally nearly
£1,800,000 of capital still uncalled, but the shareholders
are so very largely dead and gone that this large
amount is really an unknown quantity. The Maoriland
Government, which helped the Bank of New Zealand out
of its difficulties, hasn't attempted to do likewise by the
Loan and Mercantile. Perhaps the situation is too
complicated for it. Still, it must be remembered that
a lot of the co.’s liabilities could be extinguished very
cheaply if a cash offer were made for them, and if the great
mass of contingent liabilities could be bought up for a
very little in the £, then it would be possible to make
calls on the shareholders and get in some money. There
is a great difference between paying calls into an almost
hopeless concern, and paying them into one which
has a fair chance of coming to vigorous life again. It
might be possible for the State, with profit to itself, to
put the Loan and Mercantile on its feet, and make it a
prosperous Maoriland (not a London) institution again.
And Premier Seddon used, at one time, to like tackling
big difficulties.

«••••*
By dint of making heavy calls the capital has
been again built up to £286,824. But the co. has only
about £5OOO in cash, which will soon disappear if the losses
continue at the present rate ; and there is very little
more capital (only about £2680) to call up. The appar-
ently incurable trouble is that there is £500,000 owing on
41 per cent, debentures, which swallow up £22,500 a year
in interest. If the co was free from debt it would show
a regular profit. But there is no visible prospect of free-
ing it, and unless the price of beer rises the debenture-
holders seem likely to own the concern in time. The
concern started in 1899. It only paid four dividends in
all, and the first one (? per cent, in 1889) was partly
made up by a contribution from the persons who sold the
unlucky concern to the present owners, and who
guaranteed 8 per cent, for the first few years. There
has been only one dividend (5i per cent.) since the
guarantee ran out. <

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, of Melbourne, reports
that there has been a great improvement in the general
outlook of the engineering business since the new year
started, and that his pipe making works in Melbourne
and Perth are working full-handed. Amongst the orders
now being executed at Footscray works is one for 7 miles
10 chains of 18in. diameter light steel spiral-riveted pipes
for the Victorian water supply ; and at Perth works a
■contract is in progress for over 6 miles of 10pn. diameter
spiral-riveted pipes, flanged joints, for the Greenbushes
Tin-Mining Syndicate. About two miles of these pipes
are to stand a test pressure of 8001 b. to the square inch.*

The field of concentration after stamps or
amalgamation is an old and recognised one, yet this
treatment is now improved upon in certain instances by
■concentrating direct from the stamps, as it is found (hat
gold heavy enough to sink through the film of water
moving rapidly down the copper plate is heavy enough
to be successfully settled upon the deck of the Wilfley
'table, and thus concentrated. On certain refactory ores,,
a common practice is now to stamp and amalgamate on
plates and to pass the tailings over Wilfley tables,
extracting the troublesome sulphides and then retreating
the tailii gs by pan amalgamation, cyanide or any of
the special lixiviation processes that may be demanded
by the peculiar characteristics of ihe ore. Illustrated
catalogues with valuable notes on ore dressing can be
■obtained from the the Sole Manufacturers in Australia of
the Wilfley Concentrating Table, the Austral Oris
'Company, South Melbourne.*

Investor (almost everybody who writes to
this column for information seems to take that pen-
name, and it leads to confusion): Don’t care for them.
If you could sell out without loss, and buy the preferred
inscribed stock of the E.S. and A. Bank, you would have
as good a return and a safer investment. Your present
investment may prove all right, but it is a gamble.

Premier o’3ee, of N.S.W., denies, with need-
less emphasis, the rumor that his Government proposes
to float a £1,000,000 loan locally. This is the explanation
given in the daily paper : r
“ ItwiU be remembered that the State Treasurer re-
cently denied that there was any truth in the rumor that
the Government proposed to place locally a million
pounds worth of Treasury bills. The Premier also em-
phatically asserts that the rumor is without foundation.
It was easier, he said, to obtain money in London where
our obligations were, than to get it here, and send it home
to meet payments for rai'way material and other things
Besides, the Government did not wish to take money
from industrial circulation, and use it for Government
purposes.”
Couldn’t Mr. See, as a carrot-merchant of large
discourse, looking before and after, see far enough
ahead to recognise that, if he floats his million loan
in London, he will take £40,000 annually in the shape
of interest out of “industrial circulation” in N.S.W.
while, if he floats it locally, he will keep that £40,000 a’
year in local “industrial circulation?” At the
end of 30 years the floating of that loan in
London will take more money out of local circulation
than would be taken out if it were floated in Sydney • and
a statesman should be able to see 30 years ahead—at all
events, he should see further ahead than the end of his
own fleeting, temporary nose Besides, the excuse is a
silly delusion on the face of it. The See Government is
offering every day to take all the money it can get out of
local circulation at 3J per cent.; it is only when it becomes
a case of borrowing at 4 per cent, that 'its scruples drive
it to London. The N.S.W. people who pay John See’s
screw aren’t worthy of 4 per cent. ; that blessing must be
reserved for the superior Britisher. And there is no need
to send to England for railway material in any case. The
iron is here, and plenty of men are here, and N.S.W. can
make ,ts own railway material—if the helpless borrowing
John will only be true to his alleged Protectionist
principles and try.

* * • •
N.S.W. land and income tax officials are not so
slow as some people would like to imagine. The Act
provides for a re-valuation of land every five years,
and many land-owners in town and country are now
finding out that in this, the revaluation year, the Dept,
has its eyes thoroughly skinned. The Income Tax branch
also is alive to every opportunity for ingathering the
shekels. Any unexpected windfalls to private individuals
mentioned in the daily papers such as court verdicts,
Tattersall’s Rweeps, &c., are carefully noted. The Probate
and Stamp Duties offices also afford a deal of information
to the taxation department. Despite every precaution,
the Dept, is annually defrauded of many thousands. The
individual who does a cash trade and keeps no books is a
thorn in the flesh of the officials.

Mount Boppy gold mine (near Cobar, N.S.W.):
Directors have issued a special report on the property by
J. H. Cornish, mining engineer, dated 30th Oct. last.
1 he report states that the average yield from the ore sent
i°t p i n, ? n , th ! wa s HJdwt. per ton, of a value
°l f'A 1, ??' The loc * e has been developed for a length
of 1100 ft. at the 100 ft. level, and for 600 ft. at the 200 ft
level; and Mr. C. estimates that, at the time of his
inspection, there were 100,000 tons ore in sight. In a
winze 58ft. below the 200 ft. level the lode is 64ft. in width
and the mean average of nine samples was 3oz. 19dwt.
18gr, per ton. This is the deepest and richest working in
the mine. The report indicates that there is an abundant
supply of water in the mine for milling purposes.
Prospects generally look hopeful in the extreme. On
general showing it is the biggest, if not the best, gold
mine in N.S.W.—which isn’t saying very much.
* * • *
The construction account of a co. like the B.H.
Prop, shows what a constant struggle the silver-miner
has. From first to last the co. has spent £1,249,963 in
plant, and of this £835,110 has been written off- that is
to say, the co. takes credit now for only £414,853, and it
is hardly likely that the plant is worth more The
smelting furnaces have been remodelled at least half-a-
dozen times; they have been practically rebuilt three
times. One after another the oxidised-ore concentrators
and the amalgamating, chloridising and leaching-plants
have become obsolete. The amalgamating plant had less
than a two-years’ life. Again, the briquetting plant, put
up at Pt. Pirie less than three years ago, has just now
been superseded. In the last six years £525,674 has been
spent, and £343,959 has been wiped off. The most
expensive alterations resulted from the changes in the
ore-body as the co. w orked down ; latterly they have been
due chiefly to the things that have been learned in the
groping after a more perfect treatment of the sulphides
* * * •
B.H. Prop, authorities are confident that the
Delprat process for the treatment of zinc ores will be
successful on a commercial scale, but whatever may be
the merits of the process the applications for patents are
not to be allowed to go unchallenged. A Melb. man
named Potter claims that it is an infringement of a pro-
cess for which he holds rights.
♦ * • *
“North Central, buyer Id., seller Ud.” This
has long been one of the curiosities of Sydney Stock Ex-
change list, but it now disappears, the alleged mine
having been sold—presumably to the Sulphide Corpora-
tion, which will make use of the area as a dumpfor tailings.
The price paid was £2530, which includes all machinery.
This is the last of the Sydney co.’s connected with
Broken Hill. The pertinacity with which the holders
have persevered with this worse-than-hopeless proposi-
tion has been amazing. There was never a ghost of a
chance of any payable lode being found in the lease, and
its only value is for the purpose to which it is now to be
devoted. Readers of Sydney newspaper mine reports
will miss the weekly announcement that a certain calcite
head is making larger or smaller, as the case might be ;
that “more water is dripping from the face,” and other
such alluring inducements to call-payers to part.
* * • *
Great Fingall (W.A.) is developing so satisfac-
torily at lower levels that the management has practically
decided to double the mill-power, and this will shortly be
put in hand. The new makes of stone in the south drive
at the seventh level have added largely to ore reserves, and
driving is now’ going on in the upper levels to intersect
this body of ore. The opening of the ninth level will be
started immediately so as to keep ore reserves well ahead
of battery. Total costs of the mine, including develop-
ment, come to about 28s. 6d. a ton, so that profit, when
the additional stamps get going, ought to be ’about
£50,000 a month on present-grade ore. On present out-
look the shares look extremely cheap; they should
eventually go much higher. 00. is now paying ss. div.
quarterly, so that another div. will be due at the end of
March in addition to the one temporarily suspended
owing to the forgery of scrip.
Island Eureka (W. A.) still maintains its output,
last return being 5280 z. from 380 tons. Most of the visible
pay ore is now worked out and, despite official and other
denials, it is an open secret that the mine seems to have
nearly reached the end of its tether. The lode underlays
to the west, as previously stated, while the gold chute
*P*t° the north and, in both cases, Golconda co. gets
the benefit. Latter co. was floated in London in ’96 and,
after treating 6895 tons for 14,8520 z., worth £53,000, and
distributing £6750 in divs., went bung and was recon-
structed last Aug. Including value of gold won, £91,000
has been spent on the property, and there is little to show
for it. On resuming work after reconstruction the new
manager, suspecting that Island Eureka co. was encroach-
ing on his ground, sunk a shaft near the boundary to
ascertain if such were the case. The reef was cut at a
depth of 256 ft., and assays averaged 30dwt. for 2ft. wide.
Two test crushings have practically confirmed the assays.
Golconda reconstruction provided only £9OOO, which
doesn t give the manager much of a show.
• • • •
Associated Northern, Kalgoorlie (W.A.), con-
tinues to open out marvellously. At 550 ft. level, on the
Oroya boundary, the lode is 100 ft. wide and carries rich ore
for the whole width. In a rise above this level an enormous
Dody of rich ore is exposed, and all stone is being bagged
as broken, for shipment to Dapto smelters. The levels
are only 50ft. apart vertically, but nearly double that
distance on the underlay, as the lode is very flat. Main
shaft is down 750 ft. and is being sunk about 70ft. a
month, so should reach the rich ore located per diamond
drill by the beginning of April. To date, mine has
treated 19,460 tons for 101,8820 z. gold, worth £407,500,
divs - Original working capital of
*lOO,OOO is still intact, all costs of mining and treatment
having been taken from gold won. A wonderful achieve-
reflecting great credit on Capt. Rodda, who, in
1899, took charge of what, up to his advent, was looked
upon as a cat of the wildest habits. Go. is about to
erect a large reduction plant.
* • • •
Great Boulder No. 1 (W.A.) seems to have no
luck. This “pup” of the Great Boulder has been in
existence for six years without producing gold. In Nov.,
01, co. was reconstructed with a 4s. assessment to
acquire a new property. Mr. Hamilton selected the
ii.iieen at Davyhurst as a likely venture, judging from the
large amount of one-ounce ore treated by the prospectors.
After a six months’ test, Hamilton concluded that the
prospectors knew as much about mining as he did, and
allowed them to keep the property. Some months ago
one i-oocleriough, at Menzies, was securedon asix months’
working option. From this mine the prospectors, for a
considerable time, had regular crushings going 3£oz. to
the ton, which looked good enough for anything. Since
Hamilton took over the property assays have quite failed
to confirm the battery, the ore, apparently, having been
judiciously picked. From present appearances the option
w '! l be abandoned, and Boulder No. 1 again become
childless.

• • • •
Co -> Kalpini (W.A.) has started its
IU-nead mill, but, owing to scarcity of water, is running
only two shifts daily. No stoping has been done so far.
the stone going through all coming from developments.
As all ore has been dumped, a high average is not ex-
pected. Co. has one of the most automatic and up-to-
date plants on the fields, the ore going direct from faces
to rock-breaker, and then right on to the cyanide vats
without handling. The cost of working wiil be much
lower than anything yet known on the field. The mine
is in charge of A. W. Irvine, well known in Queensland,
and one of the early, but unlucky, pioneers of W.A.
* • • •
Chaffers (W.A.), although getting patches of
fairly good ore occasionally, near the Horseshoe boun-
dary, is a hopeless proposition on present indications.
Co. is in 500,000 shares, which, at present price of Bs.,
makes the little 12-acre block worth £200,000. Scrippers,
who gaily buy this worthless paper, seem to quite ignore
u* shares on market, and the eagerness with
which those in the know will pass on the baby for them
to hold.
* • • •
Hannan’s Star, Kalgoorlie (W.A.), has cut a
lode at 675 ft. level, which gives good assays, but has been
insufficiently exploited for its value to be properly
gauged. Mine is situated outside the proved pay-belt
and although rich dabs of ore are occasionally met with*
they don t make up for the blanks. In an active market
the scrip may be quoted, but should be left severely
alone. J
* • • •
Bay ley’s, Coolgardie (W.A.), is again being gal-
vanised into life on the strength of alleged developments
at 1000 ft. level. The reefs being worked only carry gold
in patches for short distances, the greater part of the
stone being barren, in addition to there beino- long;
blanks. The mine looks an absolutely hopeless proposi-
tion, and, like the Londonderry and Wealth of Nations
nearly all the gold seems to have come to the surface!
It seems sheer waste of money going on with such a
show, but, so long as directors’ fees are available, the
farce will be continued.
* * * *
Lake View Consols (W.A.) is getting some very’
rich ore in driving north at 1000 ft. level in the Consolg
and Perseverance lodes. Both lodes for a width of Bft
carry values averaging 2oz. per ton, and have very ricli
patches of telluride in places. This development is quite
as favorable for the Perseverance as for the Consols if
not more so, as the drives are within 150 ft. from t’be
boundary. As the chutes are dipping south, the L V
Consols should get more of them at depth. As the
Central Boulder cut rich ore at a depth of 1536 ft. close to
the north-east boundary of the Consols south block—a
part of the property so far unprospected—the outlook for
the Consols is particularly rosy.
* * * •
Last month’s Westralian gold yield was
210,4500 z., the highest ever recorded in the history of
the State. In October, 1899, when the boom was at its
height, and one or two Kalgoorlie mines were sending
away to the smelting works in the eastern States large
quantities of fabulously rich ore, the output touched
205,1860 z. ; but since Oct., 1899, the monthly total has
never reached 200,0000 z. until now.

A new way of salvation has been found for
Coppertop, the far north-west of S.A. show, which for
years has been going to do great things for its Melb.
shareholders. The local nigger is to do the trick now
it is explained that he is numerous and very cheap Also
as the Coppertops will find out, he is very unreliable • and
the chances are that he will suddenly want his walkabout-
just when the furnaces are charged and when neglect
JY dl I nean destruction. No doubt the prospectors in the
White Range and other wild parts of Centralia have used
him already. The popular “system” of mining there is
to go with the gold ” ; and as windlasses can’t therefore
be used the nigger is bribed to hump the dirt up to the
surface. That is about as far as he has got as a mine**
laborer, and he isn’t likely to get much farther.
The latest development of State aid to prospect-
ing in Weslraliais the lending of camels. The S.A. Govt,,
working on different lines, is sending out a camel-party,
to explore the Centralian ranges ; L. A. Wells, who ha*
done much of this work, will lead.
• * • •
An example of economical and successful
dredging. The Perseverance Dredging Co., Upper Buck-
land (Vic.), was formed 12 months ago with a capital of
£4500. A very large dredge was ordered, and three
months ago, when operations commenced, the co. was in
debt £l4OO. This has been wiped out, and a div. of Is.
per share declared. The average yield is l|gr. per cubic-
yard ; and out of the return co. is making a profit equal
to Is. 6d. per share per month.

Lord Nelson, St. Arnaud (Vic.): “Pyrites’’
emits sulphurous fumes as a reply to a Bulletin par.
(31 l/’OS) as to “ the fly in the ointment” at this mine.
He asks : Are the shareholders aware—l. That the whole
crushing and concentrating plant is obsolete and in the
last stage of decay, and that much valuable pyrites
(worth from £l3 to £l7 per ton) is going away to the
tailings dump? 2. That the cyanide plant, which should
return a fair profit, is a losing concern ? 3. (A
doubly-distilled libel.) 4. That no stores are
kept on the mine, and much valuable time
is wasted by employees going to and fro the local
store for a shovel or a few bolts at a time? 5. That the
chief qualifications of some of the men on the mine are-
well, anything but mining knowledge ? 6. That several
other things—but the above ought to be enough to make
shareholders consider themselves lucky to get their Is.
div. per month instead of expecting 2s.

Sufferers from the drought in N.S.W. can
thank the “ fake ” daily press for the poor
response hitherto made to appeals on their behalf.
When Parliament was sitting, and there was a
chance of making anti-Federal capital out of the
sufferings of the man on the land, “oppressed by
drought and the fodder duties,” the clamorous
shrieks of the daily press filled the air. But the
shrieking was not done with any discretion, and
only caused suspicion. Then, when there was no
longer any political end to be gained, the Sydney
Freetrade press forgot all about the drought and
the distress in the West. Now the public reads,
with its tongue in its cheek, accounts which are
true enough, but which are viewed with suspicion
simply because they appear in the “ fake press.”
* m m *
The steam yacht Victoria, that Mr. Cope-
land or Mr. Henniker Heaton or somebody per-
suaded N.S.W. Govt, to purchase, is now out of
commission and being used to grow barnacles on
in Neutral Bay. The insurance premium alone on
this costly toy is nearly .£lOOO a year, and other
expenses of maintenance are heavy, even though
the vessel is kept out of use. Why not sell the
gorgeous affair ? The money can be put into
revenue account (the ship was paid for out of
loans) and provide further evidence of N.S.W.
prosperity under Premier O’See’s rule.
• • • •
A recentN.S. W. citizen, with a family of six
sons and a tendency to drink, had “the horrors”
occasionally. Instead of his family keeping him
at home to be cured and paying for a medical
man, he was always taken to gaol, brought up on
a charge of lunacy, and remanded from week to
week for medical treatment until he became well
again. And for all this care never a cent was
paid, although the patient was very well-to-do.
He finally died in gaol.
A FAMILY DIFFICULTY.
From Napier (M.L.), where it is a serious offence to
serve children under the age of 13 with liquor.
KIDDY (to barmaid): “ Please, I want a shillin’s
worth o’ brandy."
BARMAID: “ What age are you, sonny?"
KIDDY : “ Twelve."
BARMAID: “ You must tell your mother to send
someone older."
KIDDY : "’Tain’t fer me mother; it’s fer me
father, an’ ’e’s a teetotaller, an' don't want
mother to know."
“ Deeby ” writes :
I was lately a patient in the Sydney Prince Alfred
Hospital, and was simply astonished at the number of
people in good circumstances occupying beds supposed to
be provided for the benefit of those who eannoc afford
the expense incidental to an operation in a private
hospital. One of this class of patients was a gilded
farmer ; another had a respectable share in station
property ; another was a well-to-do storekeeper. One
and all boasted (when in the convalescent stage) of the
money they had saved by entering the P. A. Hospital
at £1 a week with (in cases) a 30-guineas operation
thrown in. Some system should be devised by the
hospital authorities to check this scandalous practice of
some of the greedy well-to-do.
[Sydney Hospital has tried to do so by abolishing
all payments by patients. You must enter that
hospital as a confessedly poor or dishonest
patient.—Ed. B.]
• * • •
The accounts of the Colney Hatch (Eng.)
burning disaster state that the suicidal patients
were the most difficult to get out of the building.
Now, in the case of the ’93 flood mishap at
Goodna Asylum (Q.) the case was different. It
was evening when it was seen that the flood-
waters must submerge the dormitories in the low-
lying portion of the ground, and it was decided to
wait for daylight before removing the patients in
the boats. During the night the patients most
difficult to keep quiet were the suicidal ones.
“We don’t want to be drowned like rats in a
trap,” was their constant cry throughout the
night. The only patient lost was a well-known
city man who had been driven to the asylum by
land-boom and domestic worries. He jumped out
of the boat, and made a determined swim for it
till the force of the current turning the bend beat
the life out of him.
• m • *
This is the time of year when the weary
S. Australian, halting between two opinions,
finally makes up his mind that there isn’t enough
to stay here for. There is hardly a year in which
the summer suicides don’t outnumber those of
winter by 3to 1; the Adelaide aggregates for
three years are 37 to 13.
• • * *
Adelaide has a crematorium all ready for
use, and all that is needed to inaugurate it is some
enthusiastic believer in incineration ; but no one
feels inclined to volunteer for that purpose.
Premier Jenkins laid the foundation stone of the
furnace, but he shows no disposition to test its
efficacy now. A goat, enclosed in “a suitable
coffin,” was put through a few weeks ago with
satisfactory results. Meanwhile the adjoining
cemetery, which holds 50,000 corpses, is being ex-
tended across the parklands to within 80yds. of
the houses on West Terrace.

What an insufferable bore is the greasy
cableman:—
REGAL SYMPATHY WITH BEREAVED.
London, Tuesday.—The King has telegraphed an ex-
pression of sympathy with the relatives of the men lost
in the torpedo-destoyer Orwell disaster.
Of course the King has cabled his sympathy—or,
rather, his man Friday has. That is the indis-
pensable thing for the man Friday of anyone in
the King business to do. It may be always taken
as read. Why doesn’t the cableman let us take
it as read ?

A Bulletin correspondent :
An interesting item to passengers travelling on the Vic.
railways. Now that the wages staff are on five days a
week, the men on the lines, repairing bridges, etc., often
leave their work more or less unfinished to take their day
off. As there are no flag-men on duty to warn trains to
go slow over the partially-dismantled bridges and
culverts, the plan adopted is to stick up a green flag (the
signal to go slowly) to warn drivers of approaching
trains. The wind may blow the flag away or roll it round

A complaint directed to Victoria’s
violently-retrenching Minister Bent recently ran
thus :
Our station-master here (Panmure) went on duty at
5.30 a.m. Wednesday. He is still on duty, and likely to
be for some hours. It is now 9.50 p.m. Thursday. Forty
hours 1
The only consolation is that the Ministerial
career of the coarse and deafening market-
gardener will be short. He has got things into
such a state that for a Victorian train to arrive
less than an hour late is becoming a rarity. Busi-
ness men may not care what becomes of the rail-
way employes, but they miss so many profitable
swindles through the utter disorganisation of
things that they begin to have an unholy desire
to fry Bent.

He the molasses cure for cancer, said to
have proved efficacious at Mackay (Q.). Dr. Hen-
zell, the local hospital medico, says that two of the
cases were true cancerous growths. Now comes the
statement of a Nundah (Q.) man that, after three
doctors had diagnosed his case as throat cancer,
he practically cured himself by taking a mouthful
of molasses a few times each day.
* • • *
Re cancer-cures. Henry Morris, the Eng-
lish specialist, in British Medical Journal :
Until we know the real cause and nature of cancer the
emplo3'ment of all methods must necessarily be empirical.
Some have been tried upon one theory, some upon
another —in fact, there are nearly as many theories as
there are so-called “cures"; but none of them inspire
confidence, because up to the present moment we are as
ignorant as ever as to what it is we are endeavoring to
cure. Some of the remedies have been credited with
having effected cures in several instances, but how they
have acted, what character of disease they can be relied
upon to influence, why they should ever act beneficially
upon some form of cancer, and not upon others, upon
cancer in some organ or part and not in others, nothing
is known ; all is simply empiricism.

The Owe’Sullivan loan boom having col-
lapsed in N.S.W., there is not much hope now of
the construction of the long-promised Sydney Law
Courts, and the ramshackle iron and wood building

removed from King-street, for that spoilt a Beauti-
ful Warning. Entering the old building, a couple
of years ago, you came upon the Equity Court;
on the next floor was Bankruptcy and Lunacy ;
on the next Probate.

White mice in cages may be again seen
exposed for sale in Sydney. Since these little
creatures bore part of the suspicion of having
brought bubonic plague to Australia, the Board
of Health might take notice.
The offices of Adelaide Central Board of
Health were cleaned of dirt and dust this month
by an incursion of painters and paperhangers.
This was the first time the accumulation of a
quarter-century had been disturbed, although the
department is most severe on householders who
neglect to keep their premises fresh and sweet.

to clinch the oath
of a Chinese wit-

• • • •
Mild wonder followed the discovery, the
other day, in pulling down some old church
buildings at Darlinghurst (Sydney), that the
foundation-stone laid by Bishop Broughton could
not be found. The foundation-stone of an
adjoining wing, laid later, was found, but on
removal it was discovered that the coins cus-
tomarily deposited had been carried off. Robbing
foundation-stones was a peculiar weakness of early
colonists. Gov. Macquarie laid the foundation-
stone of St. Mathew’s at Windsor in 1817, but a
few nights later the stone was lifted and the coins
stolen. He laid the stone again, but again in a
few nights the money was gone.

The site of the first place of worship near
Sydney Custom House, where a commemorative
service was performed a few Sundays ago, brought
up several stories about Rev. Richard Johnson and
his times, but the most curious of the early inci-
dents were omitted. The church built on that
site was paid for by Johnson, according to the
following certificated items: —
20£gal. spirits, 116 cwt. flour, 1811 b. pork,
3511 b. beef. 51b. tobacco, 31b. tea.
First price, £7 14s. Selling price .. £l4 a 9£
Paid in dollars 59 18 0
Price of building £74 7 9)
That bill Johnson forwarded to the Governor,
who derisively repudiated payment. He next
sent it to the Secretary of State, who similarly
rejected it. But on money matters Johnson was
hard to shake off. He wrote to the Archbishop
of Canterbury and the Duke of Wellington. He
kept on writing for four years, and was finally
paid.

How do these mysterious big Sydney fires
occur ? Mostly, no doubt, through defective
electric-wiring. Anybody is allowed to do elec-
tric-wiring work in Sydney. If you want to in-
stal gas, you must employ a licensed qualified
plumber ; but any and every quack can do electric-
wiring work without let or hindrance. To such a
pass have things come that no decent firm with a
reputation to lose desires wiring- work unless in
conjunction with an order for machinery, for it
is idle to attempt competition with people who

* * • *
The amended story of ho wold Richard Ince,
from Ballarat, came to die on his wedding ni<dit
m Melbourne, is a ghastly satire on the law which
permits old men, boys, and “drunks” to get
married almost as easily as they can buy a cigar.
Septuagenarian Ince, according to the amended
story, met a young Westralian widow in
Melbourne when she was on her way to
catch the German steamer for Fremantle. Pre-
sumably the old chap had previously made
the widow’s acquaintance, but the amended
story throws no light on the point. It
merely states that the female had no intention
of getting married when she met Ince. He
being an irresistible Adonis of 72, persuaded
the artless young thing to go right away to a
Wesleyan church in Lonsdale-st. and claim him
for her own. They put up at a coffee palace, and
within half an hour old man Ince died of heart
disease. So the lady who is now a double-
barrelled widow will take a third of his property.
* * « *
Brisbane undertakers have just decided to
discontinue holding funerals on Sunday. The
Brisbane summer climate renders interment
within 24 hours of death a necessity. The under-
takers’ resolution is all very well if people will
abstain from dying on Saturday.

A Melbourne medico, prescribing “moder-
ation ”in meat-eating, says “I do not think
that the average middle-aged person, following a
sedentary occupation, should eat beef or mutton
for breakfast at all. He will do better work and
keep a clearer brain by breakfasting lightly on
porridge, a bit of broiled bacon, and bread or
toast and butter. A light meal of a cutlet or
chop in the middle of the day may be taken, and
a moderate dinner of soup, fish and joint in the
evening.” Sandow wouldn’t prescribe any heavier
feeding than that for a person engaged in violent
muscular development.

Alderman in a Northern town re the local
dog nuisance: “ I have a great respect for canines,
but I do not think the seat of a man’s pants is the
place for a dog.” He had been badly bitten a few
days before, and moved, amid great enthusiasm,
that a certain old town identity should be ap-
pointed to shoot dogs. It was, however, pointed
out that he had been previously detailed for this
duty, but, being defective of vision, had by mis-
take shot an alderman. Then the motion lapsed,
and the meeting dissolved.

Jos. Symes, in
the Liberator :
There has been a great
fuss in the crawling-
papers over a “royal
scandal ” in Germany !
Bah ! royalty is nothing
else but a scandal, and
the most disgusting one
at that. There are no
marriages in royalty, ex-
cept such marriages as
prevail among prize dogs
and stud cattle and
sheep. The marvel is
that the animals put
up with it! They would
not if they had a spark
of manliness or woman-
liness in then). If this
sort of political selection
and breeding led to any
good results, we might
encourage it; but the
animal is never selected,
bought or hired for per-
sonal qualities; these are
never so much as con-
sidered. The whole ques-
tion is where and from
what was the creature
born ? If the pedigree
is all right, nothing else
is required ; physical or
mental weakness or im-
becihty doesnt count, and viciousness is too trifling to
be thought about in such a case. And this kennel-bred
Stuff is “ the fountain of honour,” be it remembered, and
fools, rogues and flunkeys crawl to it for decorations.
titles, &c., and the clergy crawl to it for preferment and
place and pay.

THE WORST KIND OF POVERTY.
HIM: “I believe your husband was a very rich
man?"
HER: “ Oh, yes, an exceedingly rich man, but a
very poor husband."
A curious case under the Children’s Pro-
tection Act was before the present sittings of
Sydney Quarter Sessions. The female defen-
dant’s motive seems to have been a “craze” to
collect children as some individuals do stamps
and china. When arrested her premises (she only
rented a room) contained some half-dozen infants,
whose very names she could or would not tell,
most of them looking decidedly well, and for
whom she seemed to have a strong affection. She
is an elderly woman with practically no means.
The Judge who tried the case was apparently
satisfied that she had no really wicked motives,
as he let her off, on her pleading guilty, with a
nominal sentence. 9

A number of well-to-do Charlton (Vic.),
farmers are stated to be selling their farms and
packing up their household gods to go and dwell
in Zion City under the wing of John “ Elijah ”
Dowie. This paper reckons there is a considerable
shock in front of those converts. As an expounder,
financier, and wearer of big purple wings, Dowie
has few equals, but when these farmers set out to
persuade him to irrigate their farms, or to lend
them 250d015. worth of seed-wheat, all the satis-
faction they are likely to get is a tract on the vir-
tues of prayer.
• • • •
The foolishness of the average man when
he gets a firearm in hi 3 hand was well illustrated
in Sydney, t’other day. A man picked up a
revolver, loaded in four chambers, at Redfern
railway station, and, after turning it over and
examining it thoroughly, pointed it at last at a
crowded street, and pulled the trigger, to see if it
was in working order. It was. A cabby going
by had a narrow escape ; the ball just missed him,
and killed his horse. One Sydney paper referred
to the incident as a “ comedy ”; it must have been
very funny for the cabman.
• • • •
Sydney D.T.’s religious column con-
tains a eulogy of the Salvarmy’s success at
street-cleaning, which it has taken in hand
in several U.S.A. cities. The success, of
course, consists in making a profit out of
the work. Any corporation could do the
same if it were allowed to do the work with
slave labor maintained at a cost of only
just sufficient food to keep the wretched
helot alive, and received the bulk of its
food, or its cost, as the result of persistent
cadging. The Salvarmy should be run in
for obtaining money and goods by false
pretences. It is a really money-spinning
concern which makes a handsome profit
out of beggary, through representing
falsely that it begs for the purposes of
charity.
* » * *
M. Nicolas Krukoff, who is just
now travelling around for the Russian
Ministry of Agriculture, seems quite cap-
able of intelligent remarks concerning the
need for better agricultural instruction in
this country, and the absurdity of trying to
grow imitation European wines for Euro-
pean palates instead of striving to create
an intelligent Australian demand for purely
Australian liquor, leaving the public to
discover what sorts of brands they like
best. Asked whether there was a large
wine production in Russia and why the
outside world hears nothing about it,
Krukoff said, “Our wine is made for Russians.
It suits us."

A Sydney firm of undertakers circulates in
its correspondence (presumably not in all of it)
this hilarious invitation :
THAT WEDDING OF YOUR’S.
When it comes off, do it in style. It’s a red letter day
—one to be remembered, especially if you make the
happy journey in a landau supplied by' us.
The Bulletin wonders if ever that cheerful invi-
tation drops by accident into the envelope which
encloses the bill, or the receipt for Mr. John
Smith’s funeral; and, if so, whether Mrs. John
Smith is pleased or otherwise at this glowing anti-
cipation of the future. Every widow is at heart a
bride ; but every widow may not be prepared to
think over her next wedding the day after her
late husband’s death. There are some splendid
possibilities latent in the Sydney system of run-
ning corpse-burying and wedding-carriage-pro-
viding from the one shop.
Few sects have ever
cultivated a district more
carefully than the Metho-
dists have the Lower
North of S. Australia.
Every four or five miles
on the dusty
plains you
come upon a
little gabled
Zoar or Ebe-
nezer, with a
dilapidated
post-and-wire
fence in front
and a patch of
graves at the
back. One
under - paid
parson over-
sees a dozen
or so of these;
for the rest, the
local blacksmith
does the best he can
—for some m; sterious
reason the local blacksmith
is the most consistent local
preacher, just as the local shoemaker is
the most consistent Radical and shocking example
generally. For the whole State, the “Church of
England ” runs the Methodists a hard race, and
just wins ; but, for this Lower North, the Metho-
dies win by a good deal more than 2to 1; and
the Scotchbyterians, who actually preponderate
in the better-favored S.E., don’t in the dry North
count one for every Methodist 10.

“ Sarah Jones ” wants to know ;
Is the man who is at present victimising’ Sydney ladies’
schools by means of bogus cheques and a desire to
educate his daughters, any relation to the man who, three
years ago, took down nearly every professional teacher in
Sydney and suburbs for half-a-crown apiece. His method
was to call at the house, when the man was usually out,
and tell the small, flurried wife that he wished to engage
Mr. So-and-So for his college as professor of music,
elocution, or painting, or to swish the pupils, or some-
thing, at a large salary. Then he intimated that the
fact of the husband’s gigantic intellect having been added
to the teaching staff had to be immediately advertised,
and it was customary for the new professor to pay 2s. 6d.
for that purpose. It was a thin device, but when it was
tried on me I didn’t know whether I stood on my head or
my heels for sheer joy, and I paid the 2s. 6d. gladly.
Before I got right end up again he was gone with the
money. When my husband returned home, instead of
dancing the Highland fling at his good fortune, he used
terrible language. He had paid the half-crown himself
the week previous. On comparing notes with our friends,
we reckoned that the little man with the brown beard
and boots slightly down-at-heel had roped in 137 half-
crowns. Six months afterwards he was “doing” Mel-
bourne the same way.
♦ • * •
A Norwegian sailor, named Forssell, con-
victed at Melb. Criminal Court of killing an old
woman in mistake for a younger woman, he being
too drunk to know the difference between them,
was sentenced last week to six years’ gaol. Had he
killed two old women the murderous alien would
probably have got eight years. There’s a reduction
in the price if you take more than one life.

The distinctive dress of the larrikin does
not seem so apparent now as a few years ago,
when tailors and bootmakers found it a lucrative
business to provide the hoodlum with the peculiar
outfit of his rank. One famous old-time larrikin
paid £5 for his boots, upon the toe-caps of each of
which were let in the portraits of his noble self
and his donah.

“Benjie”: Charity has been preached
for 1900 years, and “ the time is not ripe ” for its
practice. I suggest, as a man who has conscien-
tiously avoided church for 20 years, a church
charity-tax. There are 640,000 electors in N.S. YV.;
so surely at least 240,000 attend church. A penny
apiece Would give £lOOO a week, with which
much could be done. And if my suggestion wero
adopted, the number of church-goers would at
once increase.

When Dr. Temple, the late Archbishop of
Canterbury, was a little boy, he used to earn a
few coppers by scaring rooks off the freshly-sown
wheat. To the end of his long life he kept the
determination which, in the beginning, helped
him to overcome the obstacles in his path, and he
also retained the appearance of a scarecrow.
The W.C.T.U. is usually “down” on
osculation and violently averse to gambling. But
this paragraph appeared in its Adelaide organ
(the Alliance News) for the current month :
“ I’m so certain that I’m right,” she said, “ that I’ll
bet you a kiss.” “Against what?” he asked. “Against
another kiss, of course,” she answered. He was thought-
ful for a moment. “ That settles it,” he said at last.
“ I’ve found a game that permits both parties to win.
Let’s double the stakes.”
