A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, February 10, 1903.
Mr Dear Arini, —
Exodus of money and society to Maoriland is
unprecedented this year. Last Monday, Miss
Rawson, N.S.W. Governor’s daughter, left for
Auckland in the Sonoma. Mrs. Burdekin and
daughter, and Miss Irene Marks, a well-known
Sydney belle, have also gone thither. I have
been looking at published temperatures of N.S.W.
cool spots and summer resorts: Bombala, Moss
>nA> 00m ba, Blackheath, all up amongst the
90 s. ihe only place that is really delightful and
bracing is Kiandra, and that is inacessible, save
by long coach journey. I hope Mr. O’Sullivan
will soon borrow money enough to buy scrap-iron,
or whatever he wants, for a railway, and so fulfil
ms promise to bring us within reasonable distance
of our finest mountain resort and keep Society’s
money in the State.
Castles girls, Dolly and Amy, have made
musical the Town Hall for large, moist audiences
not of the regular concert-going type. At first
concert last week there were enough R.C. priests
present to make a Sacred College. They didn’t
go out in the interval, and, outside the pro-
gramme, nothing interested them till Mr. Dan
i Sl onnor Made a late entry, ushering in two
ladies. The legislator seemed awfully proud of
his charges, and the front-seaters as well as the
soggarths showed lively interest as they followed
with their eyes Demosthenes’ triumphant march to
his seats.
Amy Castles’ reception was as loud and long as
any artist could wish, and her voice seemed to me
as good as it was some months ago, although somo
authorities said that they noticed a change in it.
It must be a bit of a nuisance to have ears so keen
that they hear the grass grow. Her first number
was .Je suis Titania, ” and as encores she fed the
ravening audience with “The Old Folks at Home”
un j . 9 ne Ever Marries Me.” Amy as a
ballad singer is very sweet.
Enter Dolly Castles, debutante. Another lyric
soprano, very flaxen and fair and pink, short-
nocked, modest, and smiling. Her voice is very
much what her sister’s was before her study
abroad. She has some wonderful upper notes.
Ocean, Thou Mighty Monster ! ” from Weber’s
Oberon, was the very large order which the
winsome Dolly undertook to deliver to an astonished
audience, and, quite naturally, not being a finished
choral swimmer, she rather floundered in the
magnificent breakers of the descriptive declama-
tor/ ri S i° nf v Bllt with “ Sweet Vale of Avoca”
and lostis “Good-bye” she did wonders for a
debutante. Miss Freda Miller made her debut as
a piamste of skill. Her self-possession is phe-
nomenal. But it is out of place with short
skirts and the Shetland-pony mane that falls over
hei eyes and face, and gives her such trouble
when she makes her frequent bobs to the
audience.
There was more smoke than fire in the outbreak
at Hotel Australia, just after midnight, last
Saturday. Two firemen went round knocking at
guests and servants’ doors. One gentleman
rushed downstairs with his wife’s silk underskirt
over his pyjamas. All brought their jewellery,
even when they didn’t wait to dress. One dis-
tinguished guest, a military man, gallantlv im-
peded the firemen by his efforts to help them save
the hotel. Fortunately the hose which he was
handling burst and “knocked him out.” Em-
ployees next day presented him with a huge card-
board V.C. “for valor.” He gravely wore it to
the theatre. When this “ gent ” goes to the play
alone he is said to take two seats—one for himself
one for his hat. ’
The tree planted by Melba in King’s Park,
Perth (W.A.), was an already-well-grown Queens-
land flame-tree. She took the shovel from
Minister Forrest and briskly threw in the earth,
then the superintendent of the Gardens
stepped forward and began to trample the soil
over the roots. “ I had hoped to see Madame
Melbi do that,” was King Forrest’s remark, in a
sad voice. “ Then I will,” replied Melba, frankly.
Raising her skirts, and revealing a nice pair of
feet and a ravishing silk and lace under-robe, she
trod a measure on the loose soil around what will
be known in future as “ Melba’s Tree.” Madame
f °rrest snapshotted her the while, and as the
hour was the sunset.one the party did not look
inartistically hot.
A correspondent, the nap of whose mind has
been brushed the wrong way
1 am sure that the par. in last week’s Melbourne letter.
a J,’ ol ‘ b , *; h ® , hl s u h i oned Toorakienne never patronising
tvFl r t klS |i aths > was written by a man - a horrid one
at that! If he asked one of the Sydney T.B. attendants
it the smartest girls were regular customers she would, if
she knew the truth and spoke it, reply : “ They don’t
come here at all, because they have the means to bathe
rurkishly in their own homes.” I have, and most of my
*?ave. , You may buy the canvas cubicle (and
stove), that does duty for a hot room, for 255., and
lathering, rubbing and polishing may be very well done
by yourself if you have not got a maid.—a lady’s maid, l
mean. The smartest society women here and in Mel-
bourne have ladies’-maids. Last week I went to one of
our local T.B. s (the tired feeling was so overwhelming
*T at ? c , ( ? ul ? f do my own rubbing); Its accommoda-
tion is limited, and it is open to women only on certain
days in the week Although I was early, all the dressing
boxes were already occupied; and' the attendant told mo
to leave my clothes on a chair.”’ Several elderly 13-
Stoners were already leaving their garments on chairs,
grumbling the while. I, being, fastidious, as perhaps are
the Toorakiennes,.fled. F
And she is right. I have been doing some 1
Sydney investigation, and I find that, as a matter
ot fact, 80 per cent, of the-women who are regular
customers of the Crescent and the Star are trying
to get rid of fat or to. prevent its increase. After
the bath, they weigh so anxiously—weigh wrapped
in their cooling-down sheets and straw slippers.
One extra-anxious young woman, who turned the
scale at list. 21b., exclaimed in horror, “O, I’ve
Eut on a pound and a quarter since last week ! ”
he pondered over the misery whilst dressing,
rushed out of her box half-clad, and bade the
attendant weigh the sheet and slippers. They
accounted for the extra weight, and, in the relief
and delight, the 11-stoner asked one who was a
stranger to h*r to come and have lunch. The
stranger stared at her for half-a-minute, and then
replied, “I don’t mind!” which, with some
people, is the equivalent for “many thanks.”
They did their hair, and eagerly discussed the
possibilities of Turkish baths as a means of “ keep-
ing the figure,” and finally agreed that there was
nothing in it, and that “it really hurt you, if
your heart was weak.”
All the recumbent figures in the cooling room
were intensely interested in the loud-voiced dis-
cussion, and one lady, who was reading Mrs.
Craigie’s “Love and the Soul Hunters,” while
she drank tea and ate scones, broke in, in
a voice like concussion of the brain : “ About what
you said about ‘keeping the figure,”’and pro-
ceeded to tell of a woman in “this book’’who
lived only for that.
“She was 45 and ” (reading the passage) “her
hair, which used to be a nut-brown, was now of
ebony blackness. Her fresh complexion was now
artificially white—a very smooth, waxen white.
She had shining grey eyes and vivid red lips, the
upper of which had an elaborate Cupid’s bow
well defined by a brush. Her slim, supple figure
was that of an unmarried girl (there was a 22-
year-old daughter in the background). She wore
a white lace gown, one row of superb black pearls
around her throat, pearls in her ears, and a bunch
of heliotrope in her belt.”
Eleven-stoner sighed deeply, and said: “A lot
of us could be like that if we could live in London
and spend £4OO a year on beauty-doctors.” A
girl holding a tortoise-shell comb in her mouth
and a limp, wet magazine on her knee (she had
been reading it in the hot room) removed her
mouthpiece to remark that “France would be
better. ’ And she read her contribution from the
wet magazine (Cosmopolitan, I think) about
Madame Lecompte, who died some months ago in
Paris, at the age of 84, killed, it is believed, by
one of her jealous young suitors. This lady (a
niece of the General Frossard, honored by Napo-
leon III.) and her story are well authenticated.
She lost her second husband when she was 70.
She was rich, and immediately set about renewing
her youth and making fresh conquests. She
made-up to look like thirty, practised a quick,
light step, and when her skillfully-padded and
rounded figure, arrayed in the most delicate and
artistic gown-creations, moved springingly along
the street, she was mistaken for a quite young
woman. Madame was a marvel of what spirit
and nerve may accomplish, for at 84 she was still
engaged in meeting susceptible young men, and
had on hand an endless number of love affairs.
Huge packages of love letters, beribboned, dated
and labelled, were found in her desk.
This contribution had a most extraordinary
effect on a lady of about 60, with very bunioned
feet and about a hundred long grev hairs. She
jumped like a kangaroo when Madame L.’s age
and the love-letters were mentioned, and said the
Frenchwoman was a “hussy.” But her eyes
glittered, and she went over and took the name
and date of the magazine. Awful thing if her
visit to the Turkish baths were to be the cause of
sending her frisking about in a short muslin frock
and white frilled hat and ankle-straps, in search
of young men and adventures.
Away back of Glenore (Otago, Maoriland) flows
the Fountain of the Perfect Complexion. Unfor-
tunately the odour of the spring is perceptible
some miles away, and an appalling and fearsome
odouritis. Even the railway keeps some miles from
that part of Glenore ; yet the railway is a hardy
thing. The man who wants to drink of Glenore
approaches it with a silk handkerchief tied over
his nose and mouth, and a bottle of eau de
Cologne. He drags behind him an unwilling
horse, also with a handkerchief tied over its nose,
and so they cross the mud flats to the Spring.
When the patient.has taken his drink he mounts
the horse immediately, and that noble animal
starts for a sweeter atmosphere at top speed.
The beautifying %vater is delicious to the taste—
a perfect Spa water in fact—clear and effervescing,
but its stench is like nothing else this side the
Pit of Acheron. Its medicinal qualities were
discovered by a railway ganger, who first drank
of it while suffering from the d.t.’s, and made a
wonderfully quick recovery. He got drunk four
times in one week and sobered up on it each time.
At the end of the week he began to shed his skin.
It dried up and peeled off, and that which was
beneath was clear and bright and spotless and un-
wrinkled. Something like twenty years had
■dropped off that inebriated ganger's appearance.
He took home some bottles of the fearful fluid
and poured it down his daughters’ throats by
sheer strength.. The girls became famous for the
beauty of their complexions, and were mostly
married to doctors.
A most plausible swindler has taken down
several school ma’ams in and near Sydney, and, so
far as I know, is still pursuing his nefarious
schemes, as they say in melodrama. He is well-
dressed, and calls on the principal of Dictation
College, and explains that he is a widower with
two little girls whom he would like to place
under Madame’s roof and eye, whilst he goes
to take up a responsible post in China. Re-
port speaks so Highly of Madame, etc. He
insists that Gwen and Flora shall have swim-
ming-lessons twice a week and shall keep a
pony. Terms are agreed upon, £163 per year.
He will bring the children next day. Next morn-
ing he calls again to say that the children will
come in charge of a friend, but he must leave at
once by Melbourne express, and to save incon-
venience he will pay her the first year’s sum in
advance, as his Chinese post is far in the interior
and the Boxers . He produces a cheque for
£173. Madame points out the mistake, £lO in
excess, and the widower acts worried-like, as he
has only that one cheque with him. If Madame
has the money in the house she gives him the
change—sometimes. In any case she never sees
him again.
Doctor Graoe Russell, a Maorilander, is the
first woman doctor to settle in College-st., Sydney.
She studied and took her degrees in London and
has been abroad nine years.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The Sargood mansion, ‘ Rippon Lea, ’is to be
sold immediately, and Mrs. Sargood and her only
unmarried daughter leave for England at once.
The Rippon Lea garden is said to be the finest
private landscape garden in Australia. The late
owner made a success of it, as he did of most
things, but it always seemed a pity that, to start
with, he had not chosen a site of more natural
beauty. It is a very few feet above sea level, and
20 years ago was a barren sandhill. Now, it is as
fair a spot as artificial hills, lakes, and islands
can make it.
“Justice Brummel Williams and family also
sell off house and props, and make towards a
permanent home in the Big Smoke. The woolly
George Chirnsides ditto. Two wealthy house-
holders out Armadale way also prepare to put up
the auction-sale bills, and take their accumulated
bawbees to a busier hub. Melbourne already
gapes in places, and the flash lady burglar’s
occupation will soon be gone.
“ Melba’s reception was a well-managed affair.
A good business-head always serves its owner in
good stead, be the matter in hand a life’s career
or an afternoon-party. The diva welcomed her
guests on the lawn, and looked as fine, big and
showy a figure as the best of ’em, in her Frenchily-
built muslin, trailed from top to toe with wistaria.
Her big hat of lilac chiffon, with plumes to match,
shaded a face glowing softly with a convincing
bloom of youth. Melba’s face, as a matter of
fact, is plump and round in its contour, so that it
can carry a hint of complexion powder, or such,
without appearing garish or theatrical. Her
chum, little Miss Clarke, the State Governor’s
daughter, also wore lilac-tints, and beside the
glowing Melba seemed a pale periwinkle, nest ing
near a glory of purple convolvuli. Gov. Clarke’s
only chick felt the recent spell of heat dread-
fully, and is ordered by her health oracle
to keep as quiet as she can. In trying
to observe her rest regimen, the patient
falls out of the pan into the fire. She keeps at a
safe distance from gay, tireless Somebodies, only
to fall into the clutches of dull, tiring Nobodies,
who, at the ‘ Myoora ’ affair, apparently almost
worshipped the convalescent to death.
“ Though all the men Melba meets are under-
stood to be sighing and moaning hopelessly on her
account, not very many of the smitten sex faced
the music at her party. Lord Dick Nevill, straw-
hatted and draped about the neck in a dark, up-
holstered sort of tie, was garnered in, early in the
proceedings, Lady Hutton, who wanted to
talk charitable fete over her strawberries. Lord
Dick, of course, is an expert in suggesting
‘draws’ for compassionate capers, but is not always
allowed a free hand. He has a neat knack of in-
ducing prominent theatricals to roll the chariot
along, but unless said stars are certified as models
of propriety from their youth up, charitable dames
label them ‘so very awkward, you know!’ and,
as soon as the Nevill hears this danger signal, he
changes the subject to performing dogs and a
fortune-teller. Old Mr. Buckley, Gov. Brassey’s
sometime crony, was another of the ‘ Myoora *
beaux, also natty Byron Moore, elocutionist
Lupton, who taught Melba of old, and Herr
Hiramer, another friend of her youth.
“ Maybe, the most expensive gown at ‘ Myoora ’
was worn by Mrs. Everard Browne, whose magni-
ficent lace over silk was white, like most of the
best toilettes on hand. Premieress Irvine had
medallions of bright-green inserted into her blue-
silk muslin. Mrs. Robert Harper, looking fagged-
out after her misadventures in Canada and recent
anxieties as to her husband’s health, roamed over
her own ‘ Myoora,’ at present tenanted by Melba,
and, like everyone else, noted the coon song on
the piano, and Marchesi’s portrait with the auto-
graph sentence, ‘lf I love anyone, it’s you ! ’
Kitchener found nothing more novel to inscribe
on his than ‘From your sincere admirer,’while
the Hopetouns contented themselves with names
and dates.
“ ’Tis said that the Myoora garden party—
Melba’s only large entertainment so far—was
arranged partly with a view to putting the
hostess’ old school-friends and similar human
reminiscences (‘rummy nuisances’ they are called
in some quarters) out of their agony. It was
getting on their nerves, so one hears, that ‘Nellie
did not love ’em as she used to do, or she would
not have neglected to welcome them to her marble
halls.’ Now there’s nothing to prevent the faithful
souls singingtheir ‘ Nunc Dimittis ’ whenever it
suits them.
“One of Melba’s male relations-in-law goes
back to the Big Smoke with her as manager at
£lOOO a year.
In an ‘ Aunt Sally ’ square a bona-fide coster
lent London color to the scene. There life moved
at its fastest. Prizes, worth a struggle, shone on
a table within sight of the competitors. Gloves
were split, sleeves gave way in feminine efforts to
dislodge the grinning effigies. Then Melba came
along and had her fling, ripping off her gloves for
the fray. Her six shots went wide of the mark,
and making a grimace she fell back to the side of
Lord Dick Nevill. There was zest and interest
in her eye over this Sally contest. She backed
her fancy against Mrs. Everard Browne’s.
“There was a sudden mustering of the smart
clans at Saturday’s Caulfield meeting. Toorak
had skipped the word along from ’phone to ’phone
that Melba was to be there. She was, in the
most glorious apparel she has yet flashed on the
out-door world. Divers patterns of lace were
dovetailed in a knotted-silk fabric. The sleeves
ended at the elbow in cascades of rare lace.
Aide Nevill played adjutant to her. When not
pacing the terrace by her side, he was backing her
august fancy with the books, or sitting in her
shadow on the Stand relating confidential things
in a low tone. Occasionally the shaking of
the Diva’s shoulders told of enjoyment of
some contretemps related. That always makes
Melba laugh heartily. The world, so anxious
to do the advantageous and proper thing in her
eyes, amuses her when it flounders and bruises
itself. A camera man cleared for his life when he
found himself in Melba’s line of vision. The day
before, at * Myoora,’ some of his brethren had set
up their machines to ‘snap’ her as she took a
turn at the ‘ Aunt Sally,’ when she made a
quick flank movement and bashed three of the
balls right in the camera’s eye. ‘ I owe it to the
brutes,’ she told the crowd, who were in con-
vulsions.
“ Mr. Robert Harper, who was lately said to be
looking ‘shockingly ill,’ is beginning to feel quite
different to that. Stories about the returned
voyageur’s prostration and his collapse after his
welcome home were largely imaginative, and his
appearance never justified them. The doctor is
quite satisfied with Mr. Harper’s present state of
convalescence; he doesn't advise the patient to
take a further holiday, or tell him not to stand
again for Parliament at next election. Mr. H.
expects to be in his usual fighting form long before
next election comes.
“ The widow of Senator Sargood has issue 1
mourning cards (they must run into several thou-
sands), expressing thanks for ‘ the great kindness,
help, and sympathy shown ’ in the time of
bereavement.
“Melba’s present pet is Mrs. Robert Power.
She told this favorite that she ‘ would like to do
something to please her.’ Her friend suggested
‘ a song or two.’ The Diva not only consented,
but begged her chum to invite as many friends as
ever she chose, to share the treat. Then she sent
along a van with a grand piano and a harp, and,
on the appointed evening, arrived with her
harpiste, and sang song after song. She has just
had a miniature of herself painted as a gift to her
pristine pet, Miss Clarke, the State Governor’s
daughter.
“ The most striking feature of the cycling sports
at the Exhibition Oval on Saturday was the
liberal display of shapely openwork hose by a
dainty little damsel in the reserve. The young
lady sat in the front row nursing her knee and
keeping the gaze of a couple of thousand spectators
riveted on the well-turned ankle, bounded at one
end by a neat shoe and at the other by a deep line
of lace. It seemed to be generally considered that
this spectacle was in itself quite worth the
shilling charged for admission.
“ A Melbourne girl, who was married the
other day, did a smart thing, I hear. She was
one of the G.P.O. clerks whose claims to increased
pay were to be decided by exam. Saying nothing
of her intended nuptials, and keeping her mind at
a nice ballast (most unusual in a bride-elect), she
joined the candidates and did the prescribed pass-
papers. Then she married, having attained three
months leave of absence on increased pay. A
wise woman that.
“ There was an awkward pause in the law courts
recently, when a giddy young lady, whose ways of
ordering luxuries were seemingly not justified by her
means, mentioned the name of a large politician
from a neighboring State, but of Federal interest
to all, as a person likely to offer bail ‘ for the sake
of old times.’ He didn’t. It’s doubtful whether
he was even approached seriously on the matter.
As the busy member in question boasts of never
reading the Melbourne papers, the touching com-
pliment of the young female may never have
reached him.
“Lady Chermside—wife of the Bananaland
Governor—went up to Gracedale House on
Friday, and Melba followed suit on Saturday
evening after the races. Gracedale, by the way,
heads the list of Victorian haunts for its romantic
records.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The wife of Capt. F. C. A. Lyon, of the
P. and O. Co’s flagship in Australian waters, is a
daughter of the late Geo. Higinbotham, Chief
Justice of Victoria.
Editor Willoughby, of Melbourne Argus,
has gone to Queenscliffe for a health holiday. His
house at Toorak (St. Ives) has been taken for a
year by Postmaster-General Drake.
Only three weeks since the late Senator
Sargood was buried in Melbourne, and already
the recollection of his military funeral is a theme
for ridicule—when anyone calls it to mind.
According to the last census there isn’t
one solitary Jew in the N. Territory. In fact,
outside the little metropolitan district there were’
all told, only 23 in the whole of 8. Australia !
Digby alias “Fatty” Grand, arrested on
suspicion of being concerned in the shooting of
Constable Long, is said to come of a big English
family, and to be not far distant from a title.
The yarn is mostly on his own authority.
Inspector Foelsche, who, after being for
28 years in charge of the local police, is said to
know more of the Northern Territory than any
other living man, is putting in his last summer at)
Port Darwin—he comes South shortly on a pen-
sion.
G. H. Reid finds that Austin Chapman
resembles Sancho Panza. That was really one of
the nicknames which the late Parkes kept for
George Reid. One time Parkes called him Sancho,
and Reid went around members asking who was
Sancho.
Senator Cincinnatus Playford, who does
pretty well for S.A. in Parliament, does even
better for it outside. Lately, in his own market
garden, a few miles outside of Adelaide, he has
teen showing the State how to grow 32 tons of
potatoes to the acre.
They aren’t strong on names in Tasmania.
Launceston Examiner regularly alludes to the
Victorian Treasurer as “Shields,” and on 26th
Jan. it described the Commonwealth Premier as
“Sir Edward Baker” —apparently a curious hash
of Richard Baker and Edward Braddon.

with a seat next the Speaker; and he sat there in
loan magnificence.

When he carried a hod he acquired a quarter-acre
allotment, which he turned into a splendid
—den. Having to leave, he let the place to an
old couple at one penny per week rent Labor
members, who can afford it, need have no fear of
becoming landlords on those terms,

“ Where would N.S. Wales be,” says See,
“without the Bills I passed into Acts?” Where
would N.S.W. be had See not been born? Ex-
Premier Salisbury used to say about his Bills, “ I
do not look on them as Acts of Parliament; I re-
fard them in the interest of England as Acts of
’rovidence,” But Salisbury had imagination.
People have, it seems, been cutting up
the bear’s skin without first catching the bear.
As to the speculation concerning Police Inspector-
General Fosbery’s successor, it transpires that
Mr. Fosbery has no present idea of resigning, and
that Premier See hopes he will long retain office.
Sydney Daily Telegraph printed this
from its Melbourne correspondent the other day:
Looking tired and depressed, Mr. O’Sullivan, accom-
panied by Larry Foley, left by the Melbourne express
this afternoon for Sydney.
If the Owe’Sullivan does choose strange company
on State jaunts he must endure the consequences.
Nanson, editor of Perth Morning Herald,
is also leader of the Westralian Opposition, and
he has found the dual office so trying that he has
had to go on the sick-list. He is the only journa-
list in Australia who has doubled those functions,
although Barrow, editor and proprietor of Ade-
laide Advertiser, held the post of Treasurer of
S.A. , and, afterwards, his partner, Thomas King,
from whom editor Bonython acquired the paper,
was Minister of Education.
B. R. Wise has arrived in London, and
put in a good night’s work last Friday (1) defend-
ing and explaining N.S.W. industrial legislation ;
(2) complaining that Australia and Canada hadn’t
been consulted by Britain before embarking on
this Venezuela business, which threatens to be so
disastrous. A popular “gag” the latter before
an English audience. Britain, judging by her
newspapers, bitterly hates the Anglo-German
alliance against America, into which the German
sympathies of the Royal Family have betrayed
her.
Federal Commandant Hutton, though his
own person ally-prepared documents are anything
but perfect examples of English, is mightily par-
ticular about the manner in which his pronounce-
ments go forth to the world. It is his custom to
round up reporters from all the pa pers on these
occasions, seat them in his office, and dictate: —
“ The Department of Defence —capital “ D ” and
comma —having to consider the possibilities of in-
vasion—comma —must be keenly alive to Imperial
interests—full stop. Nothing definite has been
done in the past—semi-colon—something should
be done in the future—full stop.” And so on.
“Melb.", Klingender has just gone, a
sort of TJltimus Romano-rum of the yore-time
attorneys. The squatting, mining, banking
and commercial litigation seems quite to have
passed away, the kind of thing in which attorneys
like Klingender, Muttlebury, Malleson, Duerdin,
Nutt and Moule kept merrily going, and such
barristers as Ireland, Michie, Dawson, Fellows,
Sewell, Wood and Aspinall, in the days when
Stawell, Barry, Williams and Molesworth still
held the Bench. Klingender was a remarkably
cool hand, and conducted great affairs with the
invariable temper of the ice-chest.

Veteran Henry Norman, ex-Governor of
Queensland, talked wildly of as a possible Gov.-
General of Australia, is hardly likely, in his 77th
year, to be offered or to accept such a post.
Besides, General Norman is almost more Aus-
tralian than English in some respects, and disposed
to be sarcastic at the expense of Imperial Federa-
tion —which would prevent the Balfour-Chamber-
lain Government even thinking of him for Aus-
tralia.
Quite the most picturesque (or grotesque,
whichever way you look at it) figure at Melb.
A.N.A. banquet was the Right Rev. the Modera-
tor of the Presbyterian Assembly, who was
attired in a cocked hat, knee-breeches, black silk
stockings, lace ruffles, and a nose that blushed at
his temerity. All religions seem to run to chiffon
and knee-breeches ultimately. Booth will take to
them next. He has got as far as the belltopper. -
Mrs. Humphreys, a 93-year-old. colonist,
has lived, at Watson’s Bay, Sydney,
in the same house for 73 years—a
remarkable instance of constancy in
so shifty a country. She is blind,
but has a wonderful memory. The
mind is specially clear on the long
past. Thus: “I saw old D’Arcy Went-
worth’s funeral going down George-
street to the cemetery behind the
brickfields. Four men carried the
coffin, four more walked behind to
relieve the bearers. In front walked
four mutes, with staffs and crape
streamers ; whilst all the men in
Sydney of any note followed, with
‘ weepers ’ in their hats and sideboard
collars on. It seems more natural-like
for men to carry one another to their
graves than for horses and hearses to
do the work. In the Market-square
sat that day a woman in the stocks, the like
of which I never thought to see in Sydney.”
Will Irvine appoint himself Vic. Supreme
Court Judge on the occurrence of the forthcoming
Williams vacancy? This question agitates the
other Vic. eligibles. There are two notable Vic.
precedents. Attorneys-General Stawell and Ker-
ferd appointed themselves Judges, the former
giving satisfaction; the latter, however, was
very much criticised. With the exception of
Ministerial offices, the only Crown billets open to
sitting M’s.P. are Judgeships and the Agency-
General.
When Larry Foley, chaperoning the Owe’
Sullivan, appeared for half-an-liour t’other night
in Vic. Assembly, it was amusing to note the bee-
line that Treasurer Shiels made for him. Shiels
confesses to a devout admiration of athletics, and is
at all times eager to recount his own early exploits;
no doubt he entertained the old-time pug. cham-
pion with much discourse on that favorite subject.
The Raven Treasurer was once telling a dis-
respectful pressman how his jaw had been dis-
located by a misdirected cricket-ball. “ Was it a
cricket-ball or a metaphor?” asked the long-
suffering gallery-man.
Dr. Abraham, dying at 88 in England,
takes away a patriarch of the Anglican Church of
Australasia. He went as Bishop to Wellington
(M.L.), soon after Dr. Barker arrived in Sydney.
Like most of the imported he made his way back
to England where he has vegetated for the last 30
years. Bishop Barker classed him in early life
among the ripest classical scholars in the church.
The C.E. bishops who conferred in Sydney,
lately, were, on the whole, a well-made body of
men. Queensland, Victoria and N.S.W. were
represented, and the Queenslanders and ex-
Queenslanders were especially presentable from a
physical point of view. In Australian churches,
the bookman and the praying-man are (and a good
job, too) being succeeded by the men of open-air
life and little midnight oil-burning.
W. P. Reeves, whom the Anglo-Australian
press extols as the Hon. Pember Reeves, has faith
in the old idea of a guiding daemon, and acknow-
ledges the visible finger of Providence in his
destiny. Some of these days he may throw him-
self into Federal politics—“a fine field for a
man”—but, so far, his daemon hasn’t warned him
to quit the pleasant neighborhood of St. James’
Park, London. Reeves is really the only Agent-
General who counts apart from his office; he
works hard, and is liked and respected. His new
book, summarising for English readers the
of Australian democratic legislation—“ State
Experiments in Australia and New Zealand”
was written chiefly in the ends of his business
days, before breakfast in the morning and before
bed at night.
Rev. E. Tremayne Dunstan, of Sydney, is
founding, or trying to found, an ‘‘lndependent
Church,” based on “ Apostolic doctrine, Apostolic
fellowship and Apostolic piety.” In connection
with it there is to be a college for training young
men for evangelistic work. The hollow humbug
of modern clericalism is never better shown than
when a priest or parson has a fall. If Mr. Dun-
stan had been a schoolmaster, a club secretary, or
even a racecourse steward, he would probably not
have ventured, or been allowed to venture, to
resume his old employment after such a financial
dibdole as that which preceded his recent hurried
departure from N.S.W. But for a parson’s
billet, for the spiritual guidance of young and old
—well, it seems anybody is good enough for a
parson’s billet.
Hobart (writes a correspondent) was on
the tip-toe of excitement, a couple of weeks ago,
when news was published in the local papers that
N.S.W. Works Minister Owe’Sullivan was about
to take “a well-earned holiday” in his native
town. The oldest inhabitants began to tell stories
about the Owe’Sullivan when he lived in a cottage
in Hampden Road, and the late John Donnellan
Balfe, the Irish M.P., dwelt in Wellington
Crescent hard by and took the O.W.E. as a pro-
tege and gave him a taste for public life. _ And
Foreman Rex, of the Mercury, was garnering all
his recollections of the time when the Owe’S. was
on his wages book; and how he used to praise the
future Works Minister for giving his wages to his
mother, and held him up as an example to all the
dirty-faced youths in the job-room and the machine
place. Then news came that the Owe’Sullivan
wasn’t coming after all.
A RAPID FERTILISER.
MR. MUMPER (rushing in excitedly from the
garden): “My dear, all those lovely Tsar
violets are up.”
MRS. MUMPER: “ Already? Oh, how heavenly!
It must be the effect of this sunny weather.”
MR. MUMPER : “ No, Medusa, it’s the effect of
Mr. Smith’s fowls.”
John Ross, who led the survey-paicy that
went before the transcontinental telegraph con-
structors, died in Adelaide last week. The fine
old bushman was banqueted in the town hall on
coming back from that expedition, with the S. A.
Governor of those days in the chair; but a new
generation has arisen, and the veteran was down
on his luck. A Register paragraph on Tuesday
resulted in an informal meeting, at which over
£5O was put up for him in a few minutes. But on
Thursday he was dead, the end being hastened by
an accident. Ross was born in the far north of
Scotland, in 1817.

Aboriginalities.
Tk> Contributors.— ln the ease of ethnological and
natural history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience ; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific
value.
A Riverine squatter, who is carrying out
extensive irrigation works, is besieged with ap-
plicants anxious to work their teams of horses and
ploughs for the munificent wage of one shilling
per day and tucker. The area for each man and
team must not be less than three acres per day ; a
certain amount of fodder is allowed for each horse,
and, to save their stock from absolute starvation,
the owners are willing to give their services and
plant for practically nothing ! After this, we
can’t afford to speak slightingly of the low wages
of European countries.

THE ARCHAEOLOGY OF THE DROUGHT-
As swagtnen begin to venture again on
’ong drought-closed tracks, “Found Dead,” and
“Perished from Thirst” again become common
news-heads. A week or two back two mates
(Mark Glee and Billy Perry) were found dead
near Kalandra woolshed, out Hughenden (Q.)
way. One was in a half-standing position, leaning
against a fence, a hundred yards away from where
the other was lying; their swags were found a
mile away under a tree. Three more “ perishes ”
were reported in N.S.W. during the last week in
January. These horrors could be considerably
lessened by the regular posting of information at
wayside pubs, post-offices, mail-changes, &c., as
to state Of roads, where to get water and probable
time it will last on “long stretches.” Finger-
hoards ghould be placed at all branch roads where
a t rave ll e r would be liable to error, giving distance
to squattage or town, and to intermediate water
(“no water” oould be tacked on when necessary).
This could be done by police, stock-inspectors,
rabbit-inspectors, and others who have com para-
tively easy times in backblock towns. Our
3S£
s ini ness , might give a little attention to the
necessities of those who are very often facing
Sheol.
*
‘ 00100 : English brown trout are being
caught in M'Callum’s Creek, near Talbot (Vic.),
by the old English tickling trick—it was ancient in
Shakespeare’s time. You look in the quiet pools
till you find a trout drowsing through the hot
hours and meditating the vanity of existence.
Then, with precaution against noise and shadows,
you bend over the bank, introduce your hand
beneath the water without a ripple, and gradually
bring it to the trout’s plump belly. Then you
gently “tickle,” and while the trout’s dreams
grow golden your hand slides up to the gills,
clutches, lifts—and the beauty is landed ! There
is a good description of the trick in Cutcliffe
Hyne’s new book, “Thompson’s Progress,” p. 99.
Shakespeare refers to it in “Twelfth Night” (ii,
5, 26)—“Here comes the trought that must be
caught with tickling !”
Menindie Lower Darling has had its first
ia
sight of a river steamer for 14 months,
“1.W.” tells a story of
chivalry:
From Gloucester to Taree by coachi
is a weary journey. We were near-
ing Taree, about 7.39 p. m. The moon
lit up the road, and suddenly the
monotony was broken by the sight,
of a female figure some 300yds. ahead.
There was a young man in a sulky on
the road, between our coach and the
fair one. The coach-driver whipped
up his team to pass the sulky, in the
hope that the lonely pedestrian would
deign to grace the box seat of his
coach and thus make the entry to
Taree a more triumphal one. The
man in the sulky, however, whipped
up too, and, after a short race, drew
up smartly alongside the adorable
one. We got there just in time to
see him politely raise his hat and
hear him gently inquiring if the lady
would accept a “ lift ” into town.
She murmured a thankful assent,
and stepped towards the sulky. But
then the moonlight fell on her face,
and the gallant whipped up his horse
and fled. She was the ugliest old
black gin on the Manning !

“ E.L.N.” re the hardiness
of dogs:
Travelling with horses once from
Hergott Springs (S.A.) to Wilcannia,
had a dog in our camp called
“ Sandy,” who, being slightly deaf,
got spread out about once a week by
a draught horse, but always rallied.
One night, when camped at Calla-
banna station (deserted then, save by
a stockman and wife), the dog wan-
dered off to inspect the sheep-yard,
where the few remaining “killers,”
were housed for the night. The
stockman, hearing the commotion,
appeared on the scene in his shirt,
carrying a gun, and taking Sandy for
a dingo lodged the contents in his
hind-quarters; then, rushing up
with a 3ft. waddy, he completed the
slaughter by bashing his head till
the blood ran out of his nose and
ears. After it was all over, the
stockman struck a match, and
found it was our dog, and a few
minutes later informed us of the
fact. Next morning, we decided
to bury him, and proceeded to
the spot with a spade, but Sandy
had gone. On returning to camp we
found him curled up in the front
of the waggonette. A few days after
he was “ heeling ” the horses as well
as ever. Some weeks later he at-
tempted suicide by picking up a bait
on Yancannia run ; we dosed him
with salt and salad oil, and turned
into bunk, leaving him for dead.
Next day he was all right.

A tough item from “ Proo-
eban ”:
Re strong bush types. Jim Parkes,
a Tumbarumba (N.S.W.) native,
better known as “ Sparkes,” was
the strongest specimen of bushman
I ever met. Here are three per-
formances : (1) Forgot a bag of
flour, returned from dray mile and
a-quarter into town, and carried
the flour out without a spell. (2)
Carried a two-horse plough on his
shoulder a quarter of a mile from a
selector’s to his dray, met a man and
yarned some minutes but did not
drop the plough. (3) Entered Ram-
say Bros, yard at Tumba’ to bring
out an engine-pipe weighing 9cwt.
His mates inserted a long pole in
their end before essaying the lift,
but Jim reached down and, taking
his end with one hand, sauntered
out to the team in the street. Have
seen him take a log, impeding his
dray, by one end ai.a walk round
with it, thus clearing the track—a
log heavy enough for three men.
Moles and Crimean shirt were all
he wanted on the bitterest days of
winter when ranging the snow
hills; but pneumonia eventually
laid him out. He stood 6ft. 7in.
Two station hands, after spending Christ-
mas in Sydney, left Byrock (N.S.W.) on 30th
December on their journey back to Boorooma to
resume work, and perished on the road for want of
water. One was lying under a tree; the other
had struggled on towards a Govt, tank, and died
within half-a-mile of it. His dog perished with
him. Another man, named James Brown, who
had been working on a border station, left Narine
for a spell in Mungindi on Jan. 2. His body was
found 10 miles out, and three-q’uarters of a mile
from the bore drain, with a water-bag and billy
beside it, the swag having been dropped a con-
siderable distance back; i>ls was found in his
pockets—and he had lain there for more than a
week.

“F.C.” ; “Shaker" (B. 10/l/’O3) says
he’s going to pass the milk-jug when the milking-
machine comes in. Well, I have had much
experience of the cow-thumper generally, and,
though he doesn’t waste too much hot water or
soda on his buckets, he doesn’t neglect his
separator. Have inspected hundreds (literally) in
Victoria, and repaired dozens, and never have I
seen one that a fly could get a meal off. While
the patents last, the farmer has to pay for his
separator —as he’ll have to pay for his machine-
milker—and the agent takes care to impress upon
him that cleanliness is absolutely necessary to
efficiency and durability. No farmer will want
to buy a new set of mechanical-milkers every year.
HOW IT AFFECTS SOME.
BLINKER (just out from “a sixer"): “ Yairs,
the tucker's gettin ’ worse and worse. All
through this ere retrenchment .”
Writes “ A.8.T.” :
Your correspondent, “Frank D.” (B. 31/1/’OS), is
(assuming sincerity), upon his own showing, a rank
tenderfoot where rabbiting is concerned. I live in S.A.,
180 miles up, in a place which is not “ literally alive with
rabbits,” but there are some, and can report thus :
Bought a small Antwerp rifle and 50 cartridges. Result:
40 dead rabbits in two afternoons from 4to 6 p.m. Kept
this average up for a fortnight, then wearied of a too-
easy game. You need to walk about a bit, judge your
distance, know bunny’s habits, when he is likely to
squat, how far he is from his burrow, when it is safe t@
stalk him for 100yds. or so, &c., &c. In 9 cases out of 10,
if he decides to “ squat,” he squats till shot or chased
away. Out of curiosity, have shot 50 bullets all round
a squatting rabbit, and then chased him away.
“ 2000 shots for 22 rabbits ” ! Frank, get thee to a gun-
nery ! I sometimes watch boys go cautiously towards a
watercourse to which rabbits have gone late in the after-
noon to drink—their burrows being, say, 50yds. from the
bank. The urchins crawl up noiselessly to the burrows,
plug them up with earth, then rush to the creek, yelling.
Bunny wildly scuttles for his burrow, and bumps into
the plug, after which the proverbial “ Silly as a rabbit”
materialises. He has no second idea, no resource what-
ever. A couple of dogs and a few willing hands and legs
in this way often bag dozens. Give bunny a 200yds.
sprint—he has no notion of “saving” himself,
and afterwards you might, his burrow blocked,
catch him on crutches; his lungs are so weak
and his heart so faint. Hard pressed, he often
gives a little squeak, then you know his heart
has “ broken.” But “ Frank D.” says that he dug at one
end whilst the rabbit ran out at the other, which proves
that, silly as bunny is, he can give “Frank D.” points for
shrewdness. When “Frank D.” goes hunting he should
not publish results. Rabbits are trapped round here in
thousands, and mostly destroyed. Does “Frank D.”
know that 20,000,000 were caught in Australia last year
and put, in some shape, upon the market 9 And then the
countless millions destroyed as pests! Talking about
rabbits reminds me that it is hard to keep a cat in these
parts. They take to creeks and sit, fat as mud, at the
burrows awaiting the unwary young bunnies.

“ E.” : Re “Frank D.” on rabbit-trapping
(8., 31/l/’03):
He is evidently one of those useless, helpless, poor
devils who don’t know enough to come in out of the wet,
so far as making a living in the bush is concerned. Only
rank new-chums would waste time fiddling about with
the so-called rabbit-traps sold by ironmongers to-day.
Squatters condemned them years ago, and there are
thousands of them stocked and hung-up in sheds on
N.W. stations, while the managers go at bunny with
strips of wire-netting. Bunny is also one of the easiest
things in the bush to shoot, and the man who scores only
22 out of 2000 shots is not a reliable-enough person to
have charge of a gun. Why didn’t he get the billabong
kids to kill some for him with a stick? Said kids could
have told him that the great majority of phosphorus-
poisoned rabbits die in the burrows. And who but an
out-and-out fool would start to dig out rabbits before
blocking the exits 1

“E.C.” : Is the N.S.W. Birds Protection
Acta dead letter? In any of the Sydney bird-
shops may be seen galahs, butcher-birds, magpies,
parrakeets and finches, all of which, if my memory
serves aright, are protected by the Act. This
trade, in birds is mostly callous greed on the
one side and vulgarity on the other, but it occasion-
ally involves downright cruelty, as witness the
recent postponement of the Yass Gun Club’s
shoot, on account of 70 sparrows for the slaughter
dying prematurely through exposure to the sun.
* * * *
“ A.8.T.” : Syd. Kidman, the cattleman,
discoursed, the other day, to a S. A. newspaper-
man on dingoes as mesmerists. He says that,
when a pack of them goes for big game, they sur-
round that victim, gaze, snap and yell at it till,
thoroughly stupefied, it is incapable of effective
resistance. Then it’s dinner-time. 1 was myself
once, surrounded by a pack of about 25. Had
nothing but a waddy, and dared not precipitate
matters. Simply stood stock-still, whilst the c.urs
snapped, snarled, grinned and leered all round me.
At the end of an hour—it seemed a century—they
sneaked away. I then shook off the lethargy that
lay heavy on my brain. At the end of the first
half-hour, had I wanted to use the waddy, I don’t
think I could have mustered enough energy to do
so. The eyes of the dingoes seemed to be seeing
into my brain.
• * » •
“ Drover ” : In N.S.W., it matters not
whether the roads are “ closed ” or not—the
squatters use them just the same—not only the
roads but the “ routes,” which are supposed to be
set aside for the sole use of travelling stock.
Heaven help the drover who loiters on one of
these reserves to get a bit of grass for his starving
sheep. The neighboring squatter is promptly
down on him with the threat of “proceedings,”
though all the time his own sheep are worrying
it. The roads and reserves are the squatters’, with
the fulness thereof.

“ Towrai ” : The giat of a
special by “Ares,” in the World’s
News (24/1/ 03), is that when specu-
lators first began to acquire land
m Fiji surveying-chains were not to
be had in Australia, but, crinolines
having gone out of fashion about
the same time, Robertson and
Moffat, of Melbourne, had on their
hands four or five tons of crinoline
steel, the whole of which M'Ewan
and. Co. bought up and made into
surveyors’ chains to meet the de-
mand. “Ares’” weird conception
of the form of a chain is thus in-
dicated : “ The steel cut into con-
venient lengths and joined together
with links/ Now a steel tape,
l-16in. wide, weighs about 21b. to
the chain. Crinoline steel, four
times as wide and twice as thick,
would therefore weigh 161 b. to the
chain, and four tons would contain
5600 chains, ample equipment in
those days of one-chain tapes for
1866 survey parties. Surveyors
must have been as plentiful there
as bubonic rats were a year ago.
Fact is, Fijian chiefs soon con-
cluded that measuring meant
robbery and would not let a
chain be dragged over the land
openly, so that many surveys had
to be made by measuring a base
either secretly or outside the for-
bidden area, and then triangulating
with the instrument alone.

“Jarrah”: The covered-in
tank is a much-neglected con-
venience in the drought areas. A
line of forks up the centre, fairly
strong beams and rafters, and
stripped palings laid on closely,
with a fence round the whole, are
all the essentials. A tank thus
roofed-in will hold water to within a foot
of the surface (the soakage limit) indefinitely,
and is equivalent, through precluding evapora-
tion, to an open tank twice the size. Know
a Mallee man whose protected supply hasn’t
failed him once during present seven-years’
drought, though he has sold thousands of gallons,
» * * «
“D.H.R.”: “Chass” remarks (B. 31/1/03)
that he lately pulled some ears of wheat, one side
of which contained good grain whilst the other
side contained only smut-balls. Had “Chass”
left the ears growing a little longer, he would
have found them all smut. The smut hadn’t had
time to do its work. The side in which “Chass ”
found the smut was probably the western side,
which remained longest without sun in the morn-
ing, and consequently retaining moisture longer,
bred smut.

loose at Mitchell. If they did it’s safe to say they

cattle-duffers. Some men, now being tried, are

What is alleged to be the oldest living
cow in N.S.W. was recently exhibited at Dapto
show. She has had 21 calves, and is still milking
and in good condition.
* • • «
The mutton-bird, which, according to Mr.
French, the Yic. Govt, entomologist, is cruelly
treated by being ripped open or trodden under-
foot for the sake of its egg, supplies yolks to many
Melbourne cake-makers. Right along the south
Victorian coast, from Sorrento to Cape Liptrap,
mutton-bird nesting goes on from the last week
in October. Two years ago, when in
Flinders, writer saw a man with a can
full of broken mutton-bird eggs, which
he was sending to Melbourne. He
said that the pastry-cooks flavored
the “mush” with vanilla, etc.,
and it was made into
pastry, etc. It was
much cheaper than
hen eggs.

MISS ETHEL MOLLISON, who plays "lead" in a
forthcoming Williamson Comedy.
Photo, by Windeatt, Chicago.
Land-owners on the plains south of Ararat
(Vic.), who, years ago, started letting out land
for wheat-growing on the share system, are now
sowing down the stubble with lucerne. In some
paddocks, sown last spring, the lucerne is now 9in.
high, and would carry five sheep to the acre. This
is the land that was offered to the Peacock Govt,
for 50s. an acre for closer settlement, but was re-
fused because it was assessed for taxation as “ 3rd
class.”

From Manning Times ;
Recently Mr. Moylan, of the VVoolla, who was at the
Old Bar, caught a flathead, and another fish which
measured about 4in. in length, with a head like a frog,
having a silvery belly and a spotted back. The fish had
no scales. When taken home a suggestion was made to
cook this fish as well as the flathead, but it was decided
to give the cats a feed. The fish was cut into three
pieces and given to three cats, and within Ismin. they
were all dead.

“ Bobrid ” : Re Australian farming, which
is mostly wheatgrowing: I know a farmer near
Tumberumba (N.S.W.), who sowed Hungarian
millet alongside wheat, and when the millet was
3ft. high the wheat barely measured 7in., and poor
stuff at that. The millet was stacked for fodder,
and in the present month (Jan.), a self-sown crop
of millet promises to be better than the first one.
Until the “cocky” learns to adapt himself to his
country and his crops he’ll be ever looking for
seed-wheat!

“C.H.R.”: Re weight-lifting. Many
years ago, on the Launceston (Tas.) wharf, a con-
test for supremacy took place between Tom
Hortle, son of the then Super, of Longford police,
and “Jock” M‘Cracken, who was afterwards
drowned at Ross, crossing with the mail. The
terms of the contest were: the cleanest lift of a
lOcwt. “monkey ” into the tail of
a dray from the ground with the
hands alone. Hortle lifted first,
but half - way up the weight
slipped and fell to the ground.
M'Cracken then lifted it clean
into the dray. Hortle afterwards
succeeded, but lost on the first
try. M‘Cracken’s son, now in
Tas. Parliament, may verify this
story. Tom Hortle, who died
some years ago in Warrnambool
(Vic.), where he was well known,
could, on the authority of men
still living, take a bajg of wheat
by the ears and sling it clean to
the top of a full dray-load. When
the first gold-rush broke out in
N.S.W., among the Tasmanian con-
tingent were the four brothers Hortle,
Thomas, William, John and Charles. An
old ‘ ‘ collector ” was accustomed to drive across
their washdirt every morning in a chaise-cart.
Unfortunately writer has forgotten his name,
but, for the information of any old digger who
may still survive, he was a very fat, heavy
man, and wore goggles. Tom Hortle warned
him twice. On the third occasion, he grasped
the spokes of one wheel and upset the whole show,
horse and all, off the claim.
Probably some Bulletin readers have seen or
heard of “’Possum Hill Jack,” of ’Possum Hill,
Berlin (Vic.). He was in evidence there 25 years
ago. His great feat was to run alongside a cart,
travelling at a good pace, and completely stop it
by holding one of the wheels with his hands.
There is still living near Deloraine (Tas.) an old
man, named Mick Maginty, one of the heroes of
the Crimea, who once carried a 2001 b. bag of flour
five miles, mostly up hill, with one spell on the
road. His height was sft. 7in., and his weight
9J;si.; and he was A 1 at single-stick, as writer too
well knows.

Whip: The Bulletin would be glad—more
than glad—to see 14,000 square miles of the S.E. corner of
N.S.W. constituted a Federal Territory, the freehold to
be the perpetual and inalienable property of the Com-
monwealth. It only suggested 6000 sq. miles because it
thought there was very little chance of obtaining more.
In fact, it will take a bitter fight to secure that much....

“Merton”: Be road-sweeping for gold.
Some time ago Maryborough (Vic.) Council
allowed two men to sweep a couple of miles of
road, on condition that metal (10 loads to the
chain) was to be placed thereon after the sweep-
ing, and the whole top-dressed. The contract
was faithfully carried out and the men declared
that it paid them handsomely. Roads paved
with gold 1
The Saxony royal scandal, according to
recent European papers, began with the
Grown Prince being an unmitigated brute ; pro-
gressed by the Grown Princess taking to reading
Zola and riding a bicycle (the King and the
Crown Prince were inexpressibly shocked by this
last “ immodest proceeding ”); then there were
free fights:—
Her husband affected to be as much horrified as the
King at her ways, and quarrels were frequent and violent.
At times, it is said, her husband beat her, when she would
return the blows with vigor, and a veritable rough-and-
tumble fight would result.
The lady, it is alleged, claimed the same right to
be revolting as her husband, and matched his mis-
tresses with her lovers. Finally—■
the Crown Prince took the extreme step of turning the
Princess out of the palace. There was a violent scjyiej
in which recriminations were made by each against the
other. The Prince dismissed both the Princess and the
French tutor, and they left the palace simultaneously.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial 'par
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance
is solicited.
England is certainly the paradise of the
greasy alien. All the people concerned in the
recent Bank of England forgeries were Polish or
German Jews, racial relatives of the Jewhannes-
burg boodlers.

N.S.W. Taxpayers’ Union programme in a
nutshell:—
Owing to extravagant expenditure and borrowing, the
financial position of the State is bad ; therefore let us
reduce taxation and make the financial position worse.
N.S.W. Labor Conference condoned the
action of Fred Flowers, Labor M.L.C., who
voted against woman suffrage, one of the planks
of the Labor platform in the Upper House. The
'dea seemed to be that, after all, it didn’t matter
nuch what a man did in the Upper House.
Lubricating oil pays a duty of 3d. a gallon
under the Federal tariff, of which, by theßraddon
ilause, 2|d. goes back to the importing State as
revenue. N.S.W.’s so-called “ Protectionist ”
Government passes over local industry and buys
its lubricants from the foreigner, so as to swell
ts revenue by the amount of the repaid duty !
The Federal tariff at first proposed an
adequate duty (3d. a lb,) on blue. One big
English firm thereupon prepared to build a factory
in Sydney to manufacture the article locally and
employ 600 hands. The duty was lowered to a
nominal figure by the efforts of the unpatriotic
Foreign-traders, and the factory project was
promptly abandoned.
Now that the Brisbane Newspaper Co. has
fallen out with Philp and Co., the Q. public is
being treated to some truths that, if published
before last State election, would possibly have
made a big hole in Philp’s majority. Now, it is a
day after the fair ; but the Courier is built that
way. Probably before next State election they
will kiss and be friends.

“ N.S. W. Taxpayers’ Union ” is getting on
nicely. According to the Sunday Kitchen
Wench, it is going to work in harmony with
“The People’s Reform League,” a political
myth seemingly unknown outside the Kitchen
Wench office. At present the “Taxpayers’
Union,” according to its alleged secretary, num-
bers 2500, and is going to hurl John See from
power, keep Joey Carruthers out of office unless he
agrees to its platform, and stamp the hated Labor
party utterly flat. The Tax Dodgers’ Union has
big ideas—pity its stomach is so small.
Peter Airey, M.L.A. lias been asking the
Philp Government a pertinent question. He
wanted to know what was the use of spending
£13,000 a year in assisting more people—mostly
the very poor—to come to Queensland, when the
Government couldn’t find employment for a very
large number of people who are there already.
And for all the answer he got Airey might as well
have put his question to the leper-station or to
Dunwich Asylum.
Last Monday, in an article entitled “ Vic-
toria and the Food Duties,” Sydney D.T.
lamented greatly about the iniquity of levying
Customs duties on produce just now, when the
local supply is temporarily short. Yet the D.T.
advocates a revenue-duty on tea, of which the
local supply is always short—in fact, there is none
at all. Indeed all the things on which it advo-
cates duties are things which are either always in
short supply locally or are in no supply at all,
As showing the tremendous difference
between the Federal and the State franchise and
the progressive, democratic legislation of the
former as contrasted with the stagnation of the
latter, the returns of electors in the Royal Park
district of North Melbourne electorate are inter-
esting. The State Assembly roll includes only
667 voters —and of these but 470 are resident, the
rest being plural-voting property-owners. The
Federal roll shows 1811 persons, all qualified by
residence.
Vanity Fair (London) on the annual re-
union of the Cobden Club —that “great’’Free-
trade organisation which consists of 233 Britishers
and 242 foreigners who sool Britain along the road
that pays the foreigner best: —
So long as a single ox is fattened or a single ear of
wheat is grown in these islands there will always be
occasion for melancholy gatherings, such as that which
met last week to munch its foreign bread and foreign
meat to the greater glory of the foreigner. A dinner of
the Cobden Club resembles nothing so much as a horde
of sick vultures sitting round a well-picked skeleton.
Cabled that Chamberlain, speaking to the
Kimberley diamond-Cohens, t’other day, pre-
dicted that if Cape Colony did not mend its ways
it would become “ the Ishmael of South Africa.”
He did not mean to be complimentary. But see
Genesis xxi., verses 17, 18, and 20 :
And God heard the voice of the lad (Ishmael), and the
angel of God called to Hagar out of heaven and said unto
her : “ What aileth thee, Hagar ? Fear not, for God hath
heard the voice of the lad where he is. Arise, lift up the
lad, and hold him in thine hand ; for I will make him a
great nation.” . And God was with the lad and he
grew.
Sydney Evening News stigmatises as
“preposterous” a request made by labor repre-
sentatives to Premier Barton—
That the crews of all ships coming from abroad, and
discharging or taking in fresh cargo in Australian ports,
should be paid Australian wages.
Yet, what is more just than that Australian work
should be kept for Australian workers at Aus-
tralian wages ? As things are now, the Australian
shipping interest is unfairly exposed to compe-
tition from tramp steamers whose ill-paid crews do
the work of unloading. The Australian shipowner
has to pay his sailor much higher wages and is
forced to give to the wharf-laborer a shilling an
hour. Why should he suffer from the competition
of people who don’t pay their workers even three-
pence an hour ?
In Queensland members of the Defence
Force used to travel free by rail while on duty,
but now that the Force is a Federal affair Minis-
ter Leahy demands that fares shall be paid.
This is good enough, but Minister Leahy’s
explanation is far otherwise. He says
Queensland will gain nothing, because
the fares paid will be deducted from
its share of the surplus, but he wants
to prevent the Commonwealth
Government taking undue credit for
reducing expenditure. Which, it
would seem, really means that he
wants to be able to compare the Com-
monwealth’s Defence expenditure
(railway fares included) with the old
State expenditure (railway fares not
included), and build up against the
Barton Government a bogus charge
of increasing expenditure.
N.S.W. Political Labor Confer-
ence is being soundly trounced by
the daily papers for its resolution
favoring the abolition of the Im-
perial veto as regards legislation
passed twice by the Commonwealth
Parliament. Possibly it would have
been better tactics to avoid raising
such an issue until the need arose
through the rejection of some Aus-
tralian Act by the British Crown ;
but it is absurd for the Sydney
D.T. to argue, as it does, that—
The right of veto is part of the British
Constitution, and it applies to the legis-
lation of the House of Commons, as well
as to that of a colonial Parliament.
The right of the King to veto an
Act of the British Parliament exists in theory
only ; it is never exercised, and never can be
- exercised. It is only colonial legislation that
is at the mercy of an irresponsible veto.
Some tariff facts ; Under the Parkes scale
of duties a flourishing oil-industry was being built
up in N.S.W. (Joadja Creek alone gave employ-
ment to 1100 men). The Reid tariff practically
destroyed the industry, and the Federal tariff con-
tinues the crime. A return to the Parkes scale of
duties on kerosene oils would build up another
great industry in N.S.W.
At a recent meeting of Brisbane Chamber
of Commerce, one infuriated foreign importer laid
a proposal before the other importers, that the
whole gang of foreign importers should treat
Minister Kingston with cold contempt when he
visited their city. The trouble is that Kingston
is a very big man, who could lift up two average
importers at once and tear them up and throw them
away, and unless the importers stand on a chair
to treat him with contempt, he may not see them
at all.
Before the inquiry into the alleged
dummying and other malpractices of Victorian
Lands Minister M’Kenzie was half through, the
Irvine Goverment gave his portfolio to Taverner,
and shut the door firmly in the old squatter’s face.
It might not be kind, but it was certainly wise.
Sometimes when Jonah goes overboard a feebly-
dishonest pretence is made of hoping that he will
be washed back again, or that he will be found
safe on the seashore of Assyria later on, and his
bunk is kept dry for him for a day or two ; but,
after all, no one wants him back, and no one is
deceived.
Still not a word in N.S.W. Labor party’s
generally excellent “fighting platform” about
the debt-slavery into which the State is drifting !
Free education, water conservation, the stoppage
of Crown land sales, and other things to reduce
revenue or increase expense; but not a syllable
about filling up the gap in the accounts. Free
education is good; but if, after abolishing fees, we
simply go that much further into debt, the effect
is merely to make the children pay for their own
education —we pass the bill on to them. And then
we feel good because we have given them “ free
education ” ! The hypocrisy of it!
Alterations in the polling strength of
N.S.W. constituencies, brought about by female
suffrage, will make a re-distribu-
tion of seats necessary. Mr.
“Baldy” Davis, who votes on
the See side, represents 2242
Bourke electors, while Mr. Geo.
Howarth, who votes on the other
side, is the mouthpiece of 10,421
residents of Willoughby. The
preponderance of female voters
in the suburbs will make the
question of metropolitan represen-
tation a more burning one than
ever. The 87 country constitu-
encies have now, in round num-
bers, 340,000 electors, or, say, 3900
each, while the 38 city and sub-
urban electorates have 240,000, or
nearly 6300 each. There are other
curious discrepancies. Sydney
Lang is 2585 strong, Sydney Fitz-
roy, 6000. Woollahra has 2647 men
and 4265 women votors. The
Barwon has 1613 men and only
734 women ; Bourke, 1576 and 666.
Most of the suburban members
must be most respectful to the
ladies, Mr. “Tomato” Jessop
especially ; in his electorate they
are in a majority of 1250.
Donald Macdonald, M.L.A., and Presi-
dent A.W.U., at N.S.W. Labor Conference :
The labor unionists of N.S.W. had been sold like bul-
locks at Smithfield by the Attorney-General, Mr. Wise,
in regard to the Arbitration Act.
Which silly nonsense went without a protest.
N.S.W. Arbitration Act gives trades-unions more
legal protection and wider powers than they
possess anywhere else in the world. Of course,
it does not guarantee a labor union a favorable
verdict whenever it goes to law; and, short of
that, evidently, no Act would satisfy some people.
Some hifalutin’ Joe Chamberlainisms from
J ohannesburg : —•
I hold that no Briton should appeal in a just cause to
the Motherland and go away dissatisfied.
Certainly he shouldn’t—if the Chamberlain crowd
can make a dirty, sordid profit for itself by not
sending him away. But nearly 40 millions of
Britons are appealing in dumb misery, year after
year, against the hideous system of land-monopoly
which makes 1| per cent, of the population of the
United Kingdom proprietors of 80 per cent, of its
wealth. And they go away unsatisfied—if they
are too unsatisfied they go away to gaol.
The paralysing of strike tactics by Supreme
Court injunctions, although only recently used to
any extent in England, has been such a regular
weapon of industrial warfare in America that, in
the early nineties, the phrase ‘ ‘ Government by
injunction ” was in common use by unionists
when denouncing the Federal Government as a
cat’s-paw of the capitalists. The alteration of the
well-known “ Save your money and buy a gun ”
to “ Save your money and buy a judge ” arose out
of one of the numerous injunction cases, where it
was thought the legal armory was placed a little
too freely at the disposal of the trusts in labor
matters.
Victorian Toryism is now enjoying its
first uninterrupted innings since the pre-Berry
days; and, judging by its re-adjustment of old
burdens of taxation, and addition of new burdens
to the shoulders of the landless classes, its election
of a Conservative Senator to represent a radical
people, its successful deprivation of the voting
rights of one-eighth of the citizens, its con-
templated onslaught on free education, and its
scarcely-veiled purpose of reducing old age pen-
sions to a grudged charity, doled out by its wives
in “ Ladies Benevolent Societies ” —it is certainly
making up for lost time, and doing more, with
brains (for it has some), in a year or so, than the
parti-colored tribes of Liberalism have done for
their side in over 20 years of office.
The Vic. Iceberg Govt., said to be the
first formed during the past 20 years without
King Syme’s personal supervision, is showing a
wanton disregard of that magnate’s instructions.
Since R. Speight’s time, David’s care has been the
Railway Dept., and anything he said in connection
therewith was supposed to “go.” But David has
for some time been barracking for the appointment
of a single Railway Commissioner with a board of
five “ directors,” and so certain did he feel that
Irvine and Bent would bow to his dictation,
that the Age actually announced the Govt.’s
“decision” that the management was to be on
these lines. Imagine Syme’s surprise when he
learned a day or two later that the Icebergers had
adopted precisely the same scheme as Peacock’s,
and intended appointing three Railway Commis-
sioneerson the N.S.W. lines. The Age potentate
was misled by two or three people who have been
billet-hunting through the medium of his paper,
and with whom the wish was father to the alleged
“ news.”
A voice from the Vic. House of 48 Rich
Landlords:
Mr. Sternberg regretted that the Government had
found it necessary to introduce new taxation. He would
rather have seen the deficiency funded, if necessary, and
added to the public debt than cast new burdens upon an
already struggling people.
Sternberg, like Micawber, thinks a bill is paid
when an lOU is given for the amount; he has
no idea that debt is a burden on a struggling
people.
William M‘Millan, in an interview at
Adelaide, foaming at the mouth because the
Commonwealth Government refused to hush up
the petty Customs swindles of the importers, said
that “two years of mal-administration have abso-
lutely blasted all the hopes” entertained by
“true Federalists.” Also he raged with special
venom about Federal postal management, and
said it was “thelaughing-stockof practical people.”
Perhaps it is—yet, come to think of it, when
M'Millan was helping to run N.S.W., it cost
to send a Sydney paper about 2000 miles to the
far end of Australia ; it cost nothing to send it 500
or 600 miles to the most inaccessible corner of
N.S.W., and it cost Id. postage to send it 10
yards into the next street. A man who was
partly responsible for an idiotic system like that
should keep very quiet about postal matters.

Sydney D.T. (now willing to let a little of
the truth peep out as to S’Africa): —
The mine-owners and miners have never formed a
happy family. One of the causes of the ignominious
failure of the Jameson Raid was the flat refusal of the
white miners of the Rand to assist a movement intended
to upset the Boer Government and replace it provisionally
with one which, as they believed, would chiefly represent
mine-owning- and financial interests. The policy of the
powerful corporation which controls Rhodesia has been
to secure the fullest possible share of the benefits arising
from their vast monopoly, regardless of how hard the
conditions of those who must make terms with them may
become, and it was thought that the same policy would
be followed in the Transvaal to a greater extent than was
practicable under Boer government if that of the banks
and companies were substituted.
But the D.T. didn’t let theN.S.W. workers know
that when the cohentingenting fever was on and
President Carey aspired to a knighthood.
George Reicl has been holding forth much
in Westralia about the departure of population
from Victoria Any ordinary person would fancy
that would be a subject about which even George
Reid, as the man who started a pretty large
export of the population of N.S.W., would say as.
little as possible. There are some interesting
figures on this subject in Coghlan’s latest issue of
the “ Seven Colonies,” page 530. The Federal
tariff came in force towards the end of 1901, so that
year may be taken as the end of a period of some-
thing approaching Freetrade which Reid started
in Ist Jan., 1890. The results are these : Two-
years of extensive Protection under the Parkes
tariff and four years of more extensive Protection,
under the Dibbs tariff (1890-1895) saw N.S.W.
gain 34,944 people by excess of arrivals over
departures. Six years of something approaching
Freetrade under Reid’s tariff saw it lose 10,223
people by excess of departures over arrivals. As-
the Vic. departures decreased, those of N.S.W. in-
creased till at last it became the greater popula-
tion-exporting State of the two. George Reid
should get his Dry Dog to audit his statistics, or
else he should audit the dog’s statistics.
Barrister-squatter Mackinnon’s proposal,
made recently to Victoria’s staggered Assembly, to.
square the ledger by an all-round land-tax, sent a
cold shiver down the spine of all landowners-
Mackinnon’s plan is to use the municipal councils’
valuation as a basis, and to add the tax to the-
rates. By this means the tax would be fairly
enough distributed, while the cost of collecting
it would be a mere bagatelle. Mackinnon.
suggested an impost of 3d. in the £, which would
provide enough to meet the deficit without going
on with the unspeakably mean programme which
Irvine is bludgeoning through the Assembly. The-
callous cruelty of starving the charities, stealing a>
day’s pay a week from the railway employees, and:
docking the vote for painting and repairing State
schools, already begins to cause a revolt against;
the Deform Ministry. The astounding feature of
the case is that Mackinnon is a landowner advo-
cating a tax on his own property, and the-
language of his fellow landowners is quite unfit
for printing in an inoffensive rag like The Bulle-
tin. What they will say if Mackinnon ever gets-
into power is too deafening to contemplate. The
said brother squatters are, to a man, personal
acquaintances or friends—mostly the latter up to
date —of the revolutionary Mackinnon ; but it is
understood that they will shortly hold a meeting:
to reconsider their position.

Hemorrhages Conquered by
VITADATIO
Russell-atreet, Prospect, Adelaide,
November 2, 1900.
Mr. S. A. PALMER.
184 Pitt-street, Sydney.
Dear Sir,— It is with pleasure I give my testimony to
the curative powers of Vitadatio. Mv wife was a sufferer
for two years with Hemorrhages, at first intermittently,
and afterwards incessantly, throwing up alarming
quantities of blood, and falling away to a mere skeleton,
becoming also so weak, that she could not walk about.
The only consolation the doctors could give was, that if
the hemorrhage was stopped consumption would follow.
Various remedies were tried, but were of no use. I was
then advised to try Vitadatio for her, which I did,
although I was prejudiced against the remedy thinking
it was only another of those “ cure alls.” To my
surprise, however, before the fourth bottle was finished,
the hemorrhages had ceased, and from that time my
wife increased in weight, health, and strength. Those
who knew her condition, can scarcely believe their own
eyes, her condition now being so different. It is fully 15
months since the cure took place, and there has been no
return of the hemorrhages. You may use this as you
please for the information of others.
WM. CAMERON,
Storekeeper, late of Gawler.
Witness—Geo. R. Davis, Adelaide.
For further particulars ,
S. A. PALMER ,
184 ritt Street, Sydney .

Obit., the much-respected George M‘Credie,
ex-M.L.A. for Cumberland, first of the plague
cleansers in Sydney, and accidental cause of
the George Dean criminal trial convulsion. As
to the last, the son of M'Credie, foreman of the
Dean jury, died, and Justice Windeyer, to hurry
the case through and release the father, kept the
jury at it till the verdict was returned.
Peters, the “ hero ” of the crazy Chelten-
ham (Vic.) flogging outrage, faced the Supreme
Court the other day in a neatly-trimmed brown
beard, and looking the picture of health and
sanity. The contrast between his present and his
first appearance was a strong lesson on the efficacy
of prison diet and regular habits. The jury, on
the evidence of the gaol doctors, found him insane
at the time of his flogging exploit, but expressed
the hope he would be detained until “properly
well.” Sadism, a perversion of sexual desires,
from which he suffered, seems to be a combination
of religious mania and Deadwood Dick. In this
case, said the doctors, it took the form of finding
a pleasure in the sufferings of others. So many
persons have that kind of disease that it is hardly
worth calling it such. It is almost as common
as feet, and they aren’t generally called a disease.
Adelaide police court has decided that a
horse-trainer who severely walloped his apprentice
jockey (aged 18) was not in any way punishable
for assault, because a sort of general authority to
wallop was included in the indentures of appren-
ticeship. Presumably, therefore, if the indentures
authorised amputation, or allowed the apprentice,
under certain circumstances, to be beheaded, and
he was amputated or beheaded, the same painfully
wise court would decide that everything was
perfectly correct. A court that is so bloated out
with wisdom and information seems liable to
decide anything. About 20 years ago an English
police court arrived atawiser conclusion. A factory
owner had devised an ingenious way of promoting
velocity among his girl employees. They were
divided into groups of three and the time they
took to finish a certain amount of work was noted,
and the slowest girl received six strokes from
the forewoman’s cane. The “ rules of the estab-
lishment” were supposed to be sufficient to cover
this, but when one slow girl was caned three times
in one day she proceeded against the stipendiary
spanker for assault. An unusually sensible magis-
trate decided that even if the girls subscribed to
rules which authorised the mistress to flog or hang
or disembowel or scalp or otherwise assault them,
that fact didn’t alter the law under which such
proceedings were quite illegal; and he fined the
nigger-driver something respectable.
