IAYNE'S EXPECTORANT
CURES THE WORST COUGHS.
“ Spelonken ” writes from S’Africa on the
other side of a bitter controversy :—
Re Bushveldt Carbineers, have noted several articles
and paragraphs, also “ Bushman and Buccaneer.” Who-
ever was responsible for that yarn made very little effort
to divorce fact from fiction. It is mostly latter. “ A
representative of an English paper in Pretoria ” (Bulletin
Sept. 13, 1902) also lets himself go in a sort of anyhow
style as to facts. It is pure moonshine that Morant and
co. had “ instructions ” from Hunt or anyone else. The
evidence is all against it. Hunt had the opportunity to
“blot out” several bands of refugees before he was
killed, but he did not do it. Why did not Morant blot
out Field Cornet Kelly and his men, whom he got
subsequent to putting the others "out of sight,”
in accordance with “instructions?” If acting under
instructions, why were these “ blottings out” reported to
headquarters as “ engagements,” “no casualties on our
side.” Again, why should Morant report (after shooting
a wounded Boer lad per firing-party), “ I regret to say
that the Boer captured by us the previous evening died
of his wounds the following day”? That was Morant’s
first start, too. Six Boers had been shot months prior
to this, and Van Bureen (a B.V.C. trooper) also, and this
before Hunt was in charge. Major Thomas here, and
many of your contributors, lose sight of striking facts
which stick up as plain as kopjes on the karoo. Again, that
all the “ blotted out” were, of course, wearing kharki is
pure fiction. Hunt was said to be mutilated, and Morant
may have conscientiously thought so from the terrible
tales that troopers tell, but there was no mutilation
except in the imagination of those who never saw him.
About 20 B.V.C. men could speak definitely on the facts.
The ICO interviewed knew nothing except hearsay.
Thomas and co. are charging the wrong kopje. I never
saw any printed evidence, so cannot say whether
it is “ simply ridiculous” and “infamously mislead-
ing.” The men had a full, fair, and impartial trial.
Every chance was given them to bring evidence as to
“custom” and throughout S.A. it was not forthcoming.
I incline to the view of J.B.M. (B., Nov. 15) re Witton
petition that the verdict was according to tbe evidence
and the confession of the principals. By all means get
the truth out. Then “Booze and Bushveldt" might
make a good title for a future Deadwood Dick.
[The Bulletin has never countenanced any
effort to exculpate Morant: instructions or no
instructions, his were dastardly acts. But it does
think that there is more than sufficient evidence to
justify a full inquiry into the allegation that Boer-
murdering was “ customary ” in the out-districts
during the later stages of the recent war, and was
connived at, if not directly ordered, by high
authority. If that fact is not established by
inquiry (we don’t say that it will be, but that there
is reasonable ground for believing that it will) then
some scores of people have been “ lying with
circumstance ” and with great skill.]
Received, “ The Labor Movement in Both
ihmiiBpheres ” —a well-written, bright pamphlet,
by Tom Mann, pleading for the social-democratic
view m politics.
Melb. raspberry-vinegar makers who say
they tried in vain to make the public to take true
fruit - juice, have a precedent in the historic
jam purveyor whose career Barry Pain told in
verse. After amassing a fortune by the sale of
rotten tomatoes and marrows flavored to taste, he
was converted and issued orders to each of his
mighty factories that only genuine fruit was to be
used. From that day his business dwindled and
he died in the gutter.


A Groundless Pear.
A Women Afraid of Herself.
Walking along a country road in the dark, one
often imagines that every tree or post is some
human being bent on mischief. Sometimes one is
afraid to even look back, so fixed has the idea
become that someone is stealthily following them
with intent to do harm. The darkness of the
night and loneliness of the way is accountable for
such a state. But what can be imagined of a
woman who, during all the day, was afraid of even
the noise made by ner own footsteps ? Nervous,
irritable and depressed ; afraid to move, fearing to
see even those nearest and dearest to her. Such
was once the condition of Mrs. Lillie Johnston, of
14,0 Crown-street, Sydney, before she was restored
to perfect health by Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Bills. “My nerves were entirely run down,” she
says “ and everything I wished to do seemed a
trouble. In neither my home or surroundings
could I take any interest. Indigestion first brought
on my serious illness. From this rapidly
followed loss of appetite, accompanied by frequent
sick headaches. The slightest exertion comdetely
prostrated me. I became low spirited, depressed
and irritable, and my life was indeed a burden.
At seeing the most harmless things I would start
with fear, and oft I would be put into a complete
tremble for nothing at all. I attended a hospital
but their treatment was of no avail. A friend
suggested I should try Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Bills. Before I had taken many my condition
began to improve marvellously. With their con-
tinued use I became completely cured. lam now
a happy woman in the brightest spirits and the
best health. My work is a pleasure to me, and I
am only too pleased to testify to all the wonder-
ful good Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills have done

SPORTING NOTIONS,
A day before one of the recent big Cups, a few
enterprising punters backed an intended non-trier at
long odds, then wired the owner : “ Drew Lightning in
Tatt s sweep ; will give you half, if you get a place.
Jack smith, Newcastle.** The owner, on receiving the
message, wired to “Tattersall” to look after hisinte-
rests. Lady Blank tried, and came second, and the owner
was so delighted with his win that he immediately gave
the jockey £lO. On sending for his share of the prize, he
was informed that it wasn’t a person named Smith who
•j the horse. Though the punters lost, they
evidently had a good run for their money.
• • • *
Tattersall ” and the Tasmanian Govt, are
seemingly trying to kill the golden goose. Sleepy Hollow
has put a 5 per cent, tax on sweep totals, and investors
have to put up with al5 per cent, deduction. A penny
tax has also been put on each ss. ticket issued, and
Adams has cheerfully passed it on to clients, his 10 per
cent, commission not being able to stand it. This means
ds. 4d. charged on every £1 invested.
• • • .
The utter “deadness” of the A.J.C.’s stipen-
diary stewards beats even that of the horses they were
supposed to galvanise into something like live running.
As a matter of fact, the poor men seem to have abso-
lutely no power. Apparently they can only draw the
, j 11 °* supreme A.J.C. committee to things
that do not seem quite right, and as they have no guaran-
tee that their recommendations will be acted upon
(racing business is very mixed and shady in Sydney just
now), they are content to potter around with a few cases
of reversal of form on the part of horses not too influen-
tially owned. In all probability, if the stewards were to
wade in as they ought to do, they’d take the polish off
the toes of some of the patent-leathers of the A.J.C., and
be promptly snubbed, if not sacked. The wild anxiety
of that committee to keep all real power in its own hands
smells like last year's crayfish.
• • • •
That row over the Skein Dhu-Santoi protest
emphasises the necessity for enlarging the powers of the
stipendiary stewards—or getting stewards who will do
fearlesB 'y- They are on the spot, they are in
the best position to see and hear everything, and their
decision should be final and irrevocable. It might
occasionally result in injustice, but the great blundering
committee, going on hearsay evidence alone, is infinitely
more likely to do so.
• • • •
“Stiff” running continues as per past on Syd-
ney suburban courses, and it begins to look as if the
salaries of stipendiary stewards are simply thrown
away. There are so many ways of running a “ bye”—a
horse gets left at the post, gets “ pocketed,” makes his
run too early or too late. It is only the callow youth
who tries to pull the head off his mount in the straight
that is likely to come to grief. Then, as to the betting
market, half the “ books ” have interests in horses, and
bogus wagers are made galore to show, in the event of in-
quiry, that the horse was backed. The only' way' to pro-
tect the “ mug ” who backs horses would seem to be to
lock him up in Callan Park.

Talk of Sydney ponydom just now is the
remarkable performances of 14.2 J Minerva. She
appropriated a mile race at Kensington with 10.10 up,
and for following week’s racing, at same place and over
same distance, her weight was 11.2, and she again won
easing up. Minerva shaped well among horses in M.L.
and Q., but, since coming l to Sydney, has been running
with the unregistered lot and has improved wonderfully.
She was bred at Durham Court stud, by Metal (imp.)
from Georgina, by Moorethorpe from Georgina Frederick
(imp.). At moment of writing pony followers are curious
as to what she’ll be sked to hump this week.

• • * •
Fry, the late English bookmaker, ap-
peared to be at least a worthier man than either of the
two previous “ kings ”of the betting ring. The original
monarch was one Davis, who made an immense pile in
early days, when there were no telegraph wires and not
much railway travelling, and when “books” on the
popular events were opened one year and two years
in advance. Davis continued to make a £lOO,OOO book
on the Derby even when the “toffs" had discontinued
their old sporting habit of backingyearlings for the “ blue
ribbon.’ Consequently he suffered a huge annual loss on
000 y J° l L me ’ S n< *, w “ not L worth more than about
£70,000 when he retired from business, stricken by par-
alysis. Robbed of the use of his legs, the wretched Davis
shut himself in one room of his house at Brighton (Eng 1
where his wife waited on him “ hand and foot,” and when
at last the misanthrope died he was found to have be-
queathed his fortune to the Brighton Corporation ignor-
ing his wife’s claims. During the years of Davis’s existence
in his one room a hard-headed Yorkshireman named
Steel (partnered by one Peach) ranked as “ leader of the
ring.” The sceptre of wealthy Steel passed to Fry who
had been a big speculator and an indefatigable goer long
ere he took the actual lead. Fry, originally a linen
draper, lived a life of eternal gambling and alms-givin "
Off the racecourse, he was a card player, billiard player
public speaker, and general “sport”—for love of excite’
men t Like Davis aforesaid he was practically a teetotaller •
also he was a non-smoker, and a very sparing eater. He
did his best to keep himself fit for a career which would
have killed a more self-indulgent man long before Frv
lived to be 67, and he was on the racecourse almost ud to
the last, notwithstanding that his death was due in a
great measure to mental anxieties consequent on his
being taken down by the “Yankee jockey ring” For
the past season or two Fry had been compelled to reduce
the size of his book, and put a check on his charitable
disbursements of the boodle. But he never lost his
geniality. Had he troubled about a family tree Frv could
probably have traced his ancestry back to the Quakers
He had a Quaker name and Quaker virtues, and a host of
acquaintances, hard-up and otherwise, called him friend.

That correspondent who complained of the lack
of interest in motor-racing on the track overlooked the
possibility of an almighty and picturesque smash.
Australian tracks aren’t constructed for motor-racing,
and the man who sits stiffly, looking like a petrified
jockey, is really holding his life between his red hairy
hands. Such a smash nearly occurred recently on
Melbourne Exhibition track. There is a rule that pace
shall not exceed 18secs. per lap—which means, roughly,
40 miles per hour. But the rider, puffed out with sauer
kraut and Teutonic arrogance whizzed round at 17 3-6 secs.,
and finally streaked past at 17 2-ssecs., or a mile in
1.27 4-6. Then the machine couldn’t hold the track and
something happened. No blood was splashed around,
but there’s always a possibility of seeing the machine
turn turtle, whirl round wrong side up, and drag the
driver in long shreds round a few miles before it can be
stopped. That sort of possibility appeals to an Aus-
tralian audience.
• • ■ •
By turning out to compete in the recent Mel-
bourne bike fixtures Taylor ran considerable risk. The
doctor had said he should not ride. Taylor said he
would, and did ; but he didn’t ride respectably, and his
Quixotic idea of keeping faith with the public was quite
worthless. The public didn’t want to see a sick man
risking his life to make a third-rate show, and would
probably sooner not have seen him at all.
• * • •
“Ban”: “Plugger Bill” is at present as-
tonishing the M.L. population by riding with the front
forks of his jigger reversed, curving back towards the
rider. He explains that he adopted this plan to get
nearer to pacing machines, but he rides this way unpaced
too. It is a weird sight to watch Bill’s sprint nowadays,
with those forks springing and bending with every
down stroke. It must take quite a lot of management on
Bill’s part to keep his feet clear of the front wheel, but up
to date nothing has occurred to damp that hard faced
gentleman’s enthusiasm for his new idea. Martin’s
patriotism blossoms forth as a flower in a red, white and
blue sweater, with khaki sleeves. But he is not having
things all his own way in the Land of the Moa. In a £lOO
match at Christchurch, against Geo. Sutherland (M.L.
champion), Bill was beaten badly, though the odds
were all in his favor. In the first distance,
mile unpaced, Sutherland stole a march on the
veteran, winning anyhow with 50yds. to spare. The 20-
mile paced event brought out an 8-horse French motor
C3’cle, specially built for pacing, for Bilmartin, and an
ordinary motor cycle for Sutherland. In five miles
Martin had gained nearl.v half-a-mile, owing to superior
pacing, when his back tire punctured and the race was
stopped. After delay the event was proceeded with, the
men starting from their relative positions at time of
mishap. After a lap or two Bill’s big pacing engine burst
its back tire and came a cropper. Bill got clear of the
trouble, and, after riding unpaced for nearly a lap, got
behind a similar machine to his opponent’s pacer. A
change at once came o’er the spirit of the dream. Bill
didn’t relish the altered conditions, and lost his pace once
or twice. Sutherland rapidly overhauled and passed
him. Bill then gave up the chase, alleging a broken foot-
strap. He pleasantly replied to the cheers of the crowd
by placing his fingers to his nose.
* • • •
Whatever Major Taylor may be in competition
riding, he is utterly outclassed by Robl in paced contests.
The racing at Melb. Exhibition Oval on Saturday was
merely a repetition of the previous week, “only more
so." Taylor was announced to have recovered from his
illness, and though he afterwards asserted that he still
suffered, he was, anyhow, in better health than on the
previous Saturday. Yet his riding was much worse.
Robl simply made a hack of him in the two two-mile
events. Dickentman made his first appearance as a
competition rider by starting in the open handicap races,
but showed himself a very ordinary second-rater at this
style of cycling. During the day there were some fine
displays of crooked racing.

One of the sweet jokes of Sydney cycling circles
is the frequent announcement in the D.T. of some record-
breaking enterprise of Mr. Arthur Smyth (of the Tele-
graph aforesaid). Mr. Smyth is a (say 16st.) gentleman
of more than generous plumpness, and when it is gravely
related that he is going after another record on a machine
“ which only weighs 2til bp.” there is created a humorous
anticipation that something will surely happen this time.
• * * *
Monday morning, Sydney cyclist Smyth, pur-
suing his record-breaking mission, brought Australian
mile best (professional and amateur) down to 1.34 1-5.
Intermediate times were—quarter, 25sec. ; half, 48sec. ;
and three-quarter, 1.11. This achievement is 3-ssec. less
than the best mile (professional) ever done on the con-
tinent, and 3 4-ssec. under the British amateur mile
figures, but is 8 2-ssec. over the world’s amateur best, and
18 l-ssec. in excess of the world’s professional speediest
—Contenet’s.
* • • *
“ Aldon ”: Knew a young fellow (18) who rode
a second-hand hard-tyre machine 14 miles a day to and
from work. He was a cockie’s son, apprenticed to a
Mallee blacksmith, and did that unspeakable ride, besides
bumping waggon-axles with a 121 b. sledge, for 7s. 6d. a
week, not “found.” The track was chopped-up hope-
lessly, his pedals had string-halt, one tyre was all plough-
line, and half the balls were missing. The journey took
him two hours each way—and yet he bought the thing
(£3) “ for the sport! ”
* • * *
It is surprising the ignorance that is displayed
by many' cyclists, and especially country ones, as regards
the proper inflation of their Dunlop tyres. The majority
of wheelmen ride them too slack, thus giving a big rolling
strain to the tyre. The cover will not give nearly as
good wear when it is ridden in this condition, whilst the
risk of side slip and puncturing is increased. The proper
degree of hardness of the back tyre can be correctly
gauged by the rider sitting on the saddle, when there
should be only the slightest depression of the tyre at
point of contact with the ground.*
• • • •
“ L.W.” : That Scanlan scrap at Sydney
Nat. Sporting Club recalled the Fitzjames-Roderick
Dhu unpleasantness. The big hairy Cornstalk wood-
chopper ought to have spread the Yankee in
little bits around the walls. But he didn’t. The
woodman swung frightful blows and shoiered
them thick and fast, but Scanlan side-step ed,
ducked and dodged with effective but ungraceful
celerity. Still, he kept his front to the rusher, arid at the
slightest opportunity he landed heavily. Heckenber«-
only seemed to hit Scanlan once—it was across the kidneys
and made the ’Murkan see stars and realise the necessity
of avoiding any repetition of the dose. Those ridiculous
bull-rushes of Heckenberg’s were his undoing; Scanlan
managed to hook him on the mouth and chin repeatedly
and brought him to his knees. After getting on his feet
again Heckenberg once more waded in to finish it at close
quarters ; but tremendous blows that would have felled
bullocks went over Scanlan’s shoulders, round his neck or
missed him altogether, leaving Heckenberg quite unpro-
tected. As soon as he was sure he wasn’t dead Scanlan
brought a whirling right smash on the pinnacle of
Heckenberg s proboscis and laid him out. Heckenberg
declined to get up, and Scanlan went off looking very
thankful fordeliverance from great peril. Heckenberg has
the makings of a John L. Sullivan if only someone could
teach him to keep cool and use his tremendous reach.
At present his only stock in trade is steam-hammer-
power—not that he hits anything much except the atmo-
sphere.
* * • *
Recent bruise for bantam supremacy of Queens-
land ’tween 19-year-old Sydney boy Coster, pupil of
“ Snowy ” Sturgeon, and ex-Bananaland amateur Frank
Moore, went the full 20 rounds, and was frequently extra
exciting. Moore scored most in the initial round but
subsequently till the 11th Coster had things his wav
mainly through well-judged use of the left. Later Moore
brightened up some and owned the 18th round but
Coster had stored up too many points.
* * * *
Lightweight champion pug., Bob Turner, won’t
be 20 till May 6 next. His methods are not picturesque
but very effective ; his right remains firmly fixed over
the left point of the jaw as a fend, and the wisdom of
this practice is demonstrated by the succession of heavy
swings and “ crosses ”It stops. For offensive purposes
he has the left, and, as Its owner “times" well, that
weapon does considerable execution when opportunity
offers. The lad is also a marvel at taking punishment;
heavy punches on what ordinarily are weak spots appar-
ently trouble him no more than mosquito-bites.

“Jab”: Melbourne Sportsman, t’other day,
deplored the alleged absence of backers of pugilists, and
contrasted present state of affairs with that existing 12
years ago at Melbourne Athletio Olub, when “wealthy
patrons supported the noble art In princely fashion."
Writer remembers that period, and had much to do with
the pug. game then, but it’s any odds that there’s at
least as much money for boxers now—from novioe up,
they can all find backing, some to the tune of several
hundreds. A £2OO side bet on a fight is common these
times, and as much as £5OO has been found for each prin-
cipal in some battles of late years. They never did any-
thing better in the Melbourne Athletic Club’s time.
M’Call-Creedon fight, to take place in Sydney March 8,
is for £2OO aside, and before the men shape there'll be
at least £2OOO depending on the outcome.

Glove-fight ’tween “Hock” Keys and “Charlie*
Frost, at Sydney Gaiety A. 0., Monday night, didn’t draw
up to expectations. Frost (two weeks ago) filled thi
breach left by Melbourne “ Micko ” Walsh, who suddenlj
streaked home (allegedly ill) without explanation of anj
sort. Battle was hot and clever throughout. Keys (3t4
1 on) justified the odds in the first five rounds, for he had
t’other man down often, but Frost more than held his
own in the sixth, and was decidedly best in the next two.
It was then Keys’ time to be floored a few times, and then
they were spread-eagled alternately. Both tired to
nothing halfway through the stipulated 20 rounds. The
17th saw Keys falling through sheer weakness, whereupon
the referee came between and gave the big part of the
money to Frost. Twice Keys had the fight won, but
over-eagerness to finish marred the effectiveness of his
work. Great grit was shown on both sides.

“Uppercut” : The libeller of Vic. art students,
physique in a recent Bullktin must not forget George
Coates, winner of the travelling scholarship of four years
ago. Coates, a coach-painter by trade, stood sft. 10in.,
and was as fine-looking and well-built a fellow as ever
pulled on a sweater. He was as clever with the gloves as
the charcoal-stick, and could hit like a piston-rod ; also
he was extremely good-natured and gentle. Had he not
been an artist in paints, Coates might have made a better
living altering the color of human faces in the 16-foot
ring.

“Cul”: Kensington (Sydney) proprietary in-,
tends to give coursing a decent show on its ground, the
centre of the racecourse being well on towards com-
pletion as an ideal coursing-enclosure. “Doping” or
using the hypodermic syringe, a custom now common in
coursing circles, should be put down with a strong hand
by the new club. It is possibly allowable [No, it isn’t.—
Ed. B.] that an invigorator be given to a dog by means of
the syringe, hut it is on’y too often used to stop a dog
when he is required to run “dead.” A friend of writer’s
new to the game had a dog doing splendid work, and
backed him to win a course. He was easily beaten in his
first course and his owner sold him in disgust. He won
several courses in the country almost immediately, and it
came out afterwards that the trainer had dosed him with
the syringe and effectually stopped him. The syringe is a
dangerous factor in the hands of an unscrupulous trainer.
* • v •
“Via”: Re cribbage records (B. 17/l/’O3).
Here’s one. Years hack a Bowen man w'as summoned to
Brisbane on a big court case. Whilst waiting his turn,
he and a fellow boarder played crib every day and all
day, 3d. a game, tally-up at night. Both knew every
point of the game, but every day the N.Q. man “did in ”
a few shillings. After a fortnight the other man left,
and whilst playing a game of whist the cards panned out
two short. Examination proved that two “ threes ’’
were missing, and this was the percentage that gave the
other man the leverage. He rightly concluded that an
ace or king might be missed, but the modest three was
too insignificant. Moral—count your cards even when
playing a friendly game to pass away the time.
» * * •
The “going over” of George Read from the
amateur to the professionals is a much talked about event.
George has some business intellect. Alleged silver
pots, medals and the like, which render down into very
fair pewter, have no attraction for him, and he prefers
the lurid and filthy lucre. Years ago, in Sydney, it was not
uncommon to see a man receive sovereigns for one race
and sideboard furniture for the other, and in those good
old days the “ harbor rat ” competed and rubbed cuticles
with the gentleman swimmer from Govt. House ; and the
champions of those days were the best in all the popu-
lation, and not a limited section of it. However, Read
will not have much opportunity, as South Australia is
the only portion of this continent where money is now
raced for.

Hundred yards swimming premiership of Vic-
toria, decided Saturday, evidences wide disparity between
calibre of that State's swimmers and those of N.S.W.
First and second, Humfress and Cadden, are Sydney
boys over there for holidays. Humfress won by a foot in
1.10 1-5, quite his top time. Neither Humfress nor Cadden
could live among the first eight in a N.S.W. champion-
ship if all the best started, and it’s doubtful whether
they d finish with the first dozen. Humfress’ time Satur-
day is a latter-day record for Victoria, but 20 years ago
that State had a man (Walter M’lndoe) who got over the
distance in 1.4.

TASMANIA.
NORTH WEST COAST.
rpHE VAN DIEMEN’S LAND COMPANY is prepared to
receive tenders for the renting: of portions of their
estates situated at the Surrey Hills and to let parts of the
Hampshire Hills and farms near Burnie.
The lands consist of rich basaltic soil, well-grassed and
timbered, and abundantly watered bv numerous per-
manent rivers and streams.
The Surrey Hills Block, extending to 150,000 acres, has
lately been re-surveyed and divided into 11 Blocks vary-
ing in extent from :inoO to 24,000 acres, to suit the re-
quirements of those tendering.
The Hampshire Hills Block (10,000 acres) will also
shortly be surveyed, and will be dealt with in smaller
lots.
The lands are very favorably situated, and are served
by direct railway communication with the Port of Burnie
whence there is a regular Intercolonial Steam Service.
This railway passes through the full extent of the
lands to be let and provides ready means for the trans-
port of cattle and goods to Mount Bischoff, Zeehan, and
the Western Mineral Fields.
It can also be arranged to let, if desired, smaller cleared
farms near the Coast, or houses to suit requirements
would be built in or near Burnie.
TENDERS FOR LEASES OF THE
SURREY HILLS RUNS
for a term of seven years from the 31st March, 1903, will
be received on or before the 14th March.
Full particulars as to conditions of lease, etc. , may be
obtained at the offices of the VAN DIEMEN’S LAND
COMPANY, BURNIE, TASMANIA, where a plan of the
property may be seen.
Applicants may tender for all or any number of the
runs stipulating that only one or more of their tenders
may be accepted.
The highest or any tender may not necessarily _he
accepted.
By order of the Court of Directors,
A. K. McGAW, Manage)-.

From the “Sydney Morning Herald” :
GIANT ENERGY.
One of the most continuously successful strokes
of business made in Sydney during the last 10 or
15 years has proved to be Fisher and Co.’s
introduction of a specially pi-epared kola, under
the trade mark “ Mighty Alok.” As everybody
knows now, the word “alok” is kola reversed—
and it was necessary to do this for copyright
purposes, as scores of inferior so-called “kola”
preparations were on sale everywhere. Fisher and
Co. found that as the word “kola ” itself could not
be registered as a trade-mark, it was absolutely’
useless to attempt to build up a big trade on a line
that could every hour of the day be counterfeited.
Hence we have what all Australia now knows—
“ Mighty Alok”—a special preparation from the
kola nut, universally admitted to be a really
remarkable and permanent restorer of human
energy. By a secret scientific process every
particle of the inherent strength-giving properties
of the kola nut have been secured and put up in a
most delightful drink for use in the “usual” or
“extra bitter” form, both containing the same
wonderful and therapeutic virtues. Alok is drank
with cold soda water and makes a delicious morn-
ing drink, slightly bitter, and is a great appetiser.
The Mighty Alok is a splendid tonic, and that it
stands alone is proved by the fact that every year
sees a big increase in the demand everywhere.
All hotels, with soda water, 6d per glass. All
chemists, 2s. 6d. and 3s. 6d. per bottle. Manu-
facturing pharmacy and proprietors, Fisher and
Company, 337 George-street, nearly opposite G.P.
Office.

Brisbane Courier calls loudly for a new
party, but refrains carefully from specifying its
members. How would the Govt, discontents,
Messrs. Cowley and Cameron (most obstinate of
reactionaries) blend with the Labor Opposition ?
Would the Radical oil amalgamate with the Con-
servative vinegar?
Apparently Victoria’s 48 Rich Landlords
have been cowed by the exhibition of a very small
modicum of backbone—unless they are reserving
their energies for the Reform Bill, The Probate
Act Amendment Bill, which they laid aside but
ten days before, was thrown at them again—
with but a couple of trifling amendments—
and they rushed it through in the record time
(vide Argus) of minutes! Seemingly, the
former platitudes about the necessity of “calm
consideration” and “careful review” were just
so much bunkum.
Sydney Water Board advances a reason-
able. grievance when it complains of the Owe’
Sullivan Government undertaking works not
strictly necessary, interest on which has to come
out of the Board’s revenues. Many a country
town entrusting the central Government with the
construction of a local water-supply system has
been similarly burdened with unreasonable in-
debtedness.. The Railway Commissioners, also,
can complain that construction work is undertaken
for political rather than railway reasons, and the
interest bill debited to them. Such a system
makes economical and remunerative management
practically impossible.

At Newcastle Police Court, before Mr. Love, S.M.,
William Kurey was convicted of assaulting Thomas
Bland, and knocking out three of his teeth. No provoca-
tion was given.
Wyndham, in Kimberley district of Wes-
tralia, got under the shower-bath of a Timor Sea
monsoon the other day, and had 25 inches of rain
in five days ! If science could only teach how to
guide such noble rain-clouds into the interior !

Mr. George A. Lawson, the sculptor, has received a

Some of the Hindus, gathered at Delhi to
make up the Coronation circus, suffered terribly
from the cold of that quarter. A few died, among
them Muhammed Munawar Ali, chief Mahom-
medan of the Carnatic, who broke down halfway
thro’ the performance, and died as he was being
rushed back by train to warmer Madras.

“ F.R.G.S.” writes :
Mr. Moore’s idea (Bulletin 10/l/’03) of an Australian
transcontinental railway is remarkable, so is his idea of
the geography of Australia, judging by his map. On
this, Oodnadatta is shown in the Northern Territory,
whereas it is located about 130 miles south of the 26th
parallel (the boundary between S.A. and N.T.). Also, the
only hill or mountain he considers worthy of being shown
is Mt. Boothby, in the N.T. This hill is probably as large
as two or three white-ant hills rolled into one.
“Grass-tree ” writes :—
Cabled that, at the recent Delhi Durbar, the Duke of
Connaught appointed a squadron of the Ninth Lancers to
he his escort, and that “ the choice greatly pleased the Eng-
lish in India, as that regiment was unjustly lying under
disgrace.” The facts are these : A native cook was brutally
assaulted within 25yds. of the Lancers’ cantonments. He
crawled to the officers’ mess and, before he died, stated
that two Lancers had assaulted him because he refused to
get native women for them. An inquiry was held, a,t
which native witnesses only were examined. Their evi-
dence corroborated the statements of the murdered
man ; nevertheless, the finding of the Court was that it
was impossible to discover the guilty parties, as there
was only native evidence. The Lieutenant-General was
dissatisfied, and ordered a fresh inquiry. This Court
reported that it could not obtain sufficient evidence to
fix the guilt, as “ all clues had been lost.” Two days
afterwards another native was kicked brutally by a
soldier, and died from the results of the kick. The
soldier was tried, convicted, and fined 50 rupees. Another
who broke a native’s nose was also fined 50 rupees. No
distinction between the killing of a native and breaking
his nose. After these merry frolics a third Court was
ordered to hold an inquiry into the death of the first-
mentioned native, and, according to Lord George Hamil-
ton, it obtained sufficient evidence to show that the
murder had been committed by two men of the Ninth
Lancers. So Lord Curzon punished the regiment by the
calling in of all officers on leave, the stoppage of all leave
till June, 1903, and additional cantonment duty till the
same date. What an object lesson to the Hindoos is the
glorification of these Ninth Lancers by the Duke of
Connaught 1

KIDNEYS. LIVER.
The Cause of Rheumatism y Gout, Lumbago, Sciatica,
Neuralgia, Blood Disorders, Gravel, Bladder Dis-
orders, Indigestion, Biliousness, Sick Headache,
Anaemia.
HOW TO EFFECT A PERMANENT CURE.
The Kidneys. —Blood is conveyed from the heart to the kidneys through arteries, and it is the
function of the kidneys to filter the blood, and otherwise separate from it water and various impurities.
These impurities are urea, uric acid, and mineral matter. When the kidneys have done their work of
purification, the blood enters the veins and traverses the body, accumulating, in its course, a fresh
supply of effete matter produced by the wasting of the tissues. The heart again delivers the blood to
the kidneys, and the process is repeated. Night and day the work of the kidneys goes on without
ceasing.
When the kidneys, through weakness or disease, are unable to perform their duty efficiently, the
blood retains the uric acid and urea which would otherwise be removed, and becomes contaminated by
these urinary poisons. Nature’s method of letting us know that the kidneys are working indifferently
is to make us conscious of the presence of these poisons by causing us to suffer from Rheumatism,
Gout, Lumbago, Sciatica, Neuralgia, Blood Disorders, Gravel, Stone or Bladder
Troubles. The commonest first symptom is a
PAIN IN THE BACK.
But it is not necessary to wait for this acute signal. If some urine, passed the first thing in the
morning, is placed in a covered glass and allowed to stand for twenty-four hours and is then cloudy,
shows a sediment like brick dust, is an unnatural color, or has particles floating about in it, then the
kidneys are weak or diseased and immediate steps must be taken to restore them to health
and activity.
The Liver. —The health of the liver and of the kidneys is closely connected. It is almost
impossible for the kidneys to be affected and for the liver to remain healthy, or vice versa. The liver
is an automatic chemical laboratory. In the liver various substances are actually made from the
blood. Two or three pounds of bile are thus made by the liver every day. The liver takes sugar
from the blood, converts it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able to again supply it to
the blood, gradually, as the latter requires enrichment. The liver uric acid, which is in-
soluble, into urea, which is completely soluble, and the liver also deals with the blood corpuscles,
which have lived their life and are useful no longer.
When the liver is inactive or diseased, we suffer from Indigestion, Biliousness, Anaemia.
Sick Headache, and Blood Disorders, because the blood is laden with urinary and biliary
poisons to such an extent that it cannot absorb nutriment from the food we eat, red corpuscles are no
longer formed, and it is these red corpuscles which nourish the nerves. In other words, the blood is
vitiated and starved.
The importance of keeping the kidneys and liver in vigorous health will therefore be realised,
and an explanation is afforded why
Warner’s Safe Cure
possesses such wonderful power in curing so many seemingly different disorders as those mentioned.
Warner’s Safe Cure possesses the remarkable property of being able to restore the kidneys and liver
to health and activity. All urinary and biliary poisons are then duly removed by the simple process

“Why do you trouble about mo now?" the
woman asked.
“Oh, just a fancy, I suppose—got tired—began
to yearn for a respectable domestic fireplace and
slippers, and—er—all that sort of thing, so” (with
a sneer) “ I thought I would look you up. It will
be delightful to have legalised respectability once
more.”
The woman walked to the window—the man
indolently glanced around the room.
“ Pretty hard-up, eh ? ” he asked, after a pause.
The woman did not answer.
“ Well, with your good looks, you surely could
have done better for yourself than this .”
The woman faced him. “Fortunately my good
sense proved a better friend to me than my good
looks. The first has given me the love and protec-
tion of a good, honest man. My looks ?—well,
they gave me you ! ”
“Really! and does the good, honest man who
provides the love and protection and”—with a
contemptuous glance around the poor room—“and
—er —this —does he flatter himself that he’s pro-
viding it for a good, honest woman ? ”
“Yes,” she whispered.
“ I thought so! It looks poverty-stricken
enough to damn-well reek of honesty and virtue,
and er —all that sort of thing. Come, Bess, put
on your hat. I can make your honesty much
more luxurious. Come, kiss and make friends,
and I’ll forgive you for—er—deserting me.”

Oh ! why have you troubled about me now?

What could Ido ? It was not desertion. I
could not face the horrorß of a divorce-court. You
would not consent to a legal separation ; what
could 1 do but leave you ? ”
Women reach the apex of selfishess,” the man
observed meditatively. “You never considered

name ”
“ y ° vr name ! Listen to me, Mark. I left
your house penniless. I worked desperately hard
for a scanty return. I often wanted food, but
even that was happiness compared to my life with
\ou. I hen this man met me, and helped me with
his friendship ; he, too, was alone and poor ”
The man sat watching her.
“ Mark, you have been so cruel in the old days
be merciful now. He does not know—oh !
Mark, listen—l did not tell him I was a wife. I
married him ! Leave us, Mark, to live out our
lives. There are so many other women in yours ;
you cannot want me. We two are alone in the
world, save for each other. My sin is big, I know,
but shield me, Mark—shield me ; let me live out
my life with . My God !he is coming.
Mark ! Mark ! for pity’s sake ! ”
She clutched at him desperately.
The man rose languidly. “ Deuced awkward
where can I —er —hide?”
As she pushed him behind the screen the door
opened, and a man entered slowly. His pale,
nervous face was drawn, and he passed his hand
in a dazed way over his eyes.
The woman went forward quickly.
“No, my dearest. I’m all right—a little bit
tired, perhaps, ” and he tried to smile in her face ;
“just a little tired. I’ll lie down for a minute or
two . ”
The woman gasped for breath, then hastily put
a chair for him. “ Sit for a moment,” she urged.
He sank down.
“Is anything wrong?” she asked—and won-
dered if he could hear her heart beating.
“No, no, darling—nothing special, just the
same desperate luck. lam a coward to speak of
it, dearest, but somehow to-day I’ve been haunted
by a vague uneasiness. Everything has seemed
more sordid, everyone more heartless. If it were
not for your dear self I would have ended things
—but you are you, my love, my wife.”
She stroked his hair. “It will all come right
soon, dear, don’t worry.” Her voice was hard
and discordant—for her nerves were at desperate
tension. “ Are you too tired to go down for some
bread —I mean some tea, dear ? I haven’t had
any, and my head aches.”
“ Why, yes, darling, of course I will go,” said
the man struggling to his feet. “I’ll just”——
he moved toward the screen.
“No! no! go at once,” she cried excitedly;
but the men were looking at each other.
“Deuced awkward,” murmured Mark. “Really,
but—er — ”
The other man turned to his wife for the ex-
planation he was sure she could give, but her
tongue refused to speak, and she stood stammer-
ing. Then the dark blood crept over the man’s
face.
“ Who is this man ? ” he demanded.
She looked appealingly at Mark, who twirled
his moustache carefully, and was silent.
“ He is—he is ” she began hesitatingly.
“He is your lover,” broke from the man—“ My
God ! and you are the sweet, pure woman I have
prayed to as a god—for you I have humbled
myself in the dust to get money—for you I have
begged—yes, woman, for you begged, do you
hear ? Great God ! I could kill you—you false-
hood—you lie / ”
The woman held out her hands beseechingly.
Mark noted the prettiness of the attitude.
“Why don’t you plead to your lover, woman?
Are you ashamed to own him ? ”
“ He is not my lover,” faltered the woman ;
“ he is ”
“No, by God!” said Mark, stepping to her
side —“and I’ll damned well kill you if you dare
to insult her—she is my wife ! ”

In a dismal room in a cheap lodging-house the
woman sat long over a letter. At times she
paused, and her brow flushed. She saw again the
contemptuous face of the man she loved. Then
her pencil moved quickly and her pitiful story
went forth as her plea. But what mattered it to
the figure crouching at the foot of the bed in that
other cheap lodging-house ?
it is only while we live we suffer.

Adelaide Local Court has decided that it
cannot interfere with the Dental Board (ap-
pointed under last year’s Act), which refused to re-
gister Mr. Aubrey Davis although he has practised
continuously in Sydney for 20 years. He holds a
N.S.W. registration, but on going to Adelaide for
his wife’s health was rejected as “not possessing
any qualification.” Judge Boucaut said that he
himself was not a dental expert, and must assume
that the Board had exercised its discretion in
good faith, but he would like to hear the matter
again in the Supreme Court.

At Poverty Point.
“ Haresfoot” : Elton was as good a comedian as ever I
saw, in the parts that fitted him. He wanted a really
human part, something like his capital portrait of “ The
Guv’nor.” He was never so well at home as Lauri in the
comic-opera repertoire, despite his hits in "Dorothy”
and the “ Old Guard.” He and Lauri acted together in
“LaCigale.” Lauri lay very low. He seemed to have
nothing to do but hold a donkey in “La Cigale.” Elton
rode the high horse, and was astonished when William-
son accepted his resignation, after which came the public
surprises of Lauri’s Lurcher, Polydore Poupart, &c. He
had the advantage of Elton in singing and dancing.
Many years ago Elton made a success at London Gaiety,
as Caliban, in Burnand’s burlesque of the “Tempest,”
with Monkhouse as Prospero, and Nellie Farren as
Ferdinand.
A Sydney paper solemnly whispers the rumor of a
future partnership between Allan Hamilton and Cecil
Ward, and suggests that “the aim of such a combination
would be to fill the place in the Australian theatrical
world made vacant by the departure of the Broughs.”
Holy smoke! It is easier to take aim than to hit the
bull’s eye. If Cecil Ward is well advised he will be con-
tent with the good goods the gods have given him, and
leave theatrical management alone.
Kyrle Bellew was as vain as any woman about his
clothes. When on Melbourne Herald in 1873 (George
Arthur Walstab, editor) he rarely wore the same suit a
second time in the week, and then worked out numerous
combinations, which made his wardrobe appear inex-
haustible. Tight trousers were a weakness with him—he
fancied his legs.
Townsville Star lets fly, on good provocation, because
of the small support given by the Townsville Goths to the
gifted, but locally-grown, young violinist Ernest Toy :
“ Where were the Australian Natives last night? Where
were those who were given to talk so nicely about the
people who were born in this coutry and the people
who were not? If a violinist of the name of
Mendellsohnoski or Strasburgsausagero had arrived here
from Warsaw or Wurtemburg he would have.been received
with all the honor due to an unknown alien. The Mayor
would have officially welcomed him, and Tom Smyth or
Tom Page or some other equally eloquent Thomas would
have been laid on regardless of expense. The visitor
would have been taken for a trip round our harbor and
shown our beautiful water supply, with a Chinaman wash-
ing his leprous pants included in the scene. Ernest Toy,
alas, is a Queenslander, therefore those honors were
denied him. He’s good enough for the people of London
and big Continental cities, but hardly up to the Towns-
ville standard.”
"Pro.” on tour lands at station, leaves his wife and
luggage in waiting-room while he goes to arrange at
boarding-house. Terms fixed satisfactorily with Scotch
landlady, when he says he will go to the station for wife
and luggage. “Eh ? ” says the landlady, “ yer wife is it,
or a theatrical arrangement? If it’s the latter, et’ll be
twa shullin’s a week extry.”
"L.S.” : Re that latest Sydney “joke” (8., 17/V’OS).
How is this for cruel ? A simple young chap, shunter at
a Melbourne suburban tram terminus, was persuaded by
his mates there that he had a splendid singing voice, and
that he ought to give up work and take on singing as a
profession. He resigned accordingly. One of the prime
movers in the “joke” faked a subscription-list to defray
his expenses ’Ome, and sums for thumping amounts were
put down as coming from well-known local residents. I
met the victim one day in the street, and tried to un-
deceive him. “ They’re only pulling your leg ! ” I said.
“ No, they aren’t,” he replied, “ not one of them came
near me.” I gave him best then. I saw him a few months
later delivering bills at a football match, and looking
right “ down ” to it.
Ellen Mortyne, to whom “Thesp.” recently referred
in this column, was a very popular actress from England,
at old Melb. Royal, in ’39. She died rather suddenly of
consumption. A rumor having been circulated that she
had been illegally operated on by a medical practitioner
still to the fore, an inquest was held, when the late Dr.
Tracey, who made the post mortem, gave evidence that
the unfortunate lady had never even been in need of
such an operation. Public sympathy was greatly aroused,
and the funeral, which started from Mr. and Mrs. G. V.
Brooke’s residence in Nicholson-street, was attended by
over 3000 persons.
The Countess Russell, who aired her first title on the
stage and otherwise made herself conspicuous before she
and her Earl were happily divorced, has now discovered
that her second husband—" Prince de Modena” is an
impostor as regards his title and estates. To marry a
bogus Prince is not a very valuable advt. Until the ex-
countess can get another divorce and marry a genuine
duke or something of that sort, her career of fashionable
notoriety is at an end. She must have been growing
desperate when she took a “Prince de Modena’’seriously.
“J. A. 0.” : “ Fingleur ” (B. 7/2A'S) asks, re " The For-
tune-Teller,” why we cannot have “ a season of legiti-
mate comic-opera?”, and names a number of works.
The answer is that the operas indicated would, with a
couple of exceptions, mean financial disaster, and that
not because musical culture has deteriorated, but rather
because it has advanced. These operas, melodious as they
are, have become “ back numbers.” One great reason
for the decadence of many of them is the absence, partial
or total, of choral numbers. Grand and telling effects,
such as in the “ Benediction of the Poniards” (“ Hugue-
nots”), “O Carlo Quinto” ("Ernani”), " Chimi frena”
(“Lucia”), &c., cannot be obtained without a chorus.
Flotow’s “ Ombra,” a three-act opera with four charac-
ters, an eternal succession of solos, duets, trios, and
quartets, was a conspicuous failure for this very reason.
“ Fingleur ” is perhaps a little too sweeping in his con-
demnation of the “ frothy foolery of the modern French
school.” Some of the opera bouffe of Planquette,
Audran, and Lecocq, particularly the last, is, both in
construction and inspiration, of a very high standard,
while Offenbach’s “ Barbe Blue” is not a work to
be “ sneezed at.” Finally your correspondent states
“ The first of all comic-operas is Mozart’s “Le
Nozze di Figaro,” then comes Rossini’s “ Bar-
ber of Seville,” with Cimaroso’s _ “II Matri-
monio Segreto ” a good third. Now, Rossini’s “ Barber of
Seville ” is, beyond dispute, the king of comic operas, and
certainly ranks before “Le Nozze di Figaro.” Besides,
there are others which might contest second place with
the latter opera—the same composer’s “ Magic Flute,”
for instance, or Meyerbeer’s “ Dinorah,” Thomas’s
“ Mignon,” and others. As for Cimarosa’s “ Matrimonio
Segreto,” during a stay of many years in Italy I only
remember it being once played, for a short season. The
music is bright and sparkling, but antiquated, and here
again the lack of a chorus renders it somewhat
monotonous. “Fingleur” has in his list Boiidieu’s
“Caliph of Bagdad,” but the composer’s chef d'oeuvre is
“ La Dame Blanche,” an opera first produced in the
colonies by the late Martin Simonsen, and only by him,
some 22 years ago, during a tour of Maoriland.
“ F.”: We hear a lot about “ theatrical decadence,” but
nothing about the decadence of the daily press as regards
its dealings with theatrical subjects. The “ respectable”
papers are now merely mouthpieces for showmen. Any
fiction which a theatrical speculator wishes to put before
the public is dropped into the eager ear of a grovelsome
pressman, who straightway utters it in print, with exag-
gerations added, as though it were unquestionable fact.
Fifteen years ago the newspapers mixed the salt of com-
mon sense with their theatrical criticisms and gossip.
Not so now. They allow weak-minded representatives to
serve as pimps for the principal managers, who don’t
even trouble to pretend to be grateful for the “taffy”
given them. The more that the pressmen truckle to
theatrical potentates, the more contemptuously those
self-satisfied merchants speak of their benefactors. Why
the theatrical importer-merchants should receive favors
denied to other sorts of merchants who advertise more
extensively is a question for newspaper proprietors to
answer. Perhaps the explanation can be found in the
managerial free list and whisky bill. Anyway, this
truckling to the men who run the shows is, in a measure,
responsible for “ theatrical decadence.” There is prac-
tically a conspiracy to palm geese off upon the public for
swans.
Every actress who plays Zaza has her own way of
bringing the curtain down on the stormy finish of the
fourth act. Rbjane, in Paris, made for the door, calling
her departed lover to come back to her. Mrs. Carter, in
London, rushed around the room, smashing the crockery
and things with hysterical fury. Nellie Stewart swoons.
The stage swoon is untrue to nature, but, then, the
character of Zaza is a dramatic fiction, and perhaps
Nellie’s collapse is a consistent climax to a powerful false
situation. At any rate, it is strictly orthodox, and there-
fore pleasing to the women. All women like to think
they could swoon after enjoying a terrible row with their
best boy.

Concerning the dear old English nobility. A London
newspaper report :—“ The Marquis of Anglesey, whose
lavish productions at his theatre at Anglesey Castle are
like chapters from the ‘ Arabian Nights,’ produced ‘ Little
Red Riding Hood’ last night on a more elaborate and
opulent style than ever. The Marquis takes the role of
Bonnie Blue Boy. In the final scene he is positively
aglow with diamonds and other precious stones, his
magnificent dress being thickly studded with them. At
the conclusion of the pantomime his lordship gave a
snow-dance, with wonderful effect.”
Melbourne theatrical managers will not grieve to hear
that a suburban municipal entertainment (gratis) has
been unfavorably reported on. The “ show ”in question
was intended for the first of a series of free moonlight
band performances in the Victoria Gardens, Prahran,
which is Melbourne’s “ model suburb.” The city surveyor
stated that the proceedings were a pandemonium of free
music and free fights. Boys and girls misbehaved them-
selves under the influence of popular melody, and were
“chucked out” of the gardens; but they returned by
another gate and misbehaved some more. As a result
of this report, the local mayor and sundry coun-
cillors having been individually and collectively shocked,
it was decided to charge for admission at future concerts.
It is plague season now and the Australian
cities are gradually falling into line. Brisbane
reported an outbreak this week.
A huge turtle was caught, recently, in
Swan River (W.A.), through having got stranded
in shallow water. It required six men to capsize
it —is 7ft. long, and weighs close on a quarter of a
ton.
The provisional council of the “party”
which Mr. Joey Carruthers is trying to rear in
N.S. W. on the unsubstantial basis of the fragrance
left by a departed Dry (and unwashed) Dog, in-
cludes hatter Storey, furniture-man Von Hagen,
Mr. “Florrie” Hanslow, Mr. George Pile (of the
Other Dog), Dr. “Square-head” Hodgson, and
most other of the struggling shrubs of N.S.W.
politics who are ever striving vainly to blossom
into Parliament. It is a hopeless-looking party,
the largest section of which is of the dismal
“single-tax” faith.
The exceedingly virtuous Joseph Car-
ruthers, at Sydney, on Monday night:—
He would never accept the responsibilities of office
unless he had a solid party pledged to his platform sitting
behind him in Parliament. . . . there could be no good,
honest, and pure Government unless there was one party
sitting on one side of the House and another party sitting
in opposition to it.
The same Joseph made a desperate effort to get
into office with Premier See as one of a Cabinet
bossed by the Labor party. It is safe to predict
that he and his followers will “accept the respon-
sibilities of office” the first day that Mr. John
See is so unguarded as to leave those responsi-
bilities not nailed and chained down, _ They are
very hungry for office, are Joseph and his brethren.
Something of the real truth as to
Joseph Chamberlain’s S’African “mission” is
now beginning to leak out. The London papers
—followed dutifully by the Australian dailies—
did their best to put the complexion of a triumph
on to a failure; but the facts have become too
stubborn. It is now evident that with the excep-
tion of the Rand Cohens (who have been let off
with a nominal war-contribution and bribed with
a reversal of the Boers’ “White South Africa”
policy) and the greasers, trimmers and traitors of
the National Scouts type, Mr. Chamberlain has
pleased nobody. And what else could have been
expected ?
A company is being floated in Sydney to
supply Australia with automatic restaurants, such
as are in use in Europe and U.S.A. These
establishments have no attendants. The different
viands are on plates in glass compartments, the
requisite coin isdropped intotheslotoppositeacom-
partment, and the plate is automatically lowered
to an opening through which it can be taken out.
Drink is obtained by holding a glass under the tap
of the beverage selected, and placing a coin in the
slot, when the glass is automatically filled. What
a neat, orderly, reasonable contrast to the counter-
lunch horror which has grown to such proportions
in Sydney; what was originally intended as a
mere relish with drinks has now become a huge
gorge—expected as a right and grumbled over as
if munificently paid for.
“ D.” : One of the most difficult problems
for educationists in modem city aggregations is
how to secure the individual attention to each
child’s wants that the ordinary child needs in
order that its capacities may be “educed.” Till
solved, monotony of type must be the rule with
city children, with corresponding dangers in the
direction of mob-production. Practical sugges-
tions that would also be inexpensive in working
out would be welcomed by the many earnest
State school reformers in our midst, and would
probably effect more radical reforms in national
character than many Police Offences Acts and
perhaps even Declarations of Independence.
[There are no practical suggestions that would
also be inexpensive in working-out. The crux of
the matter is money. The community gets the
education it pays for. When it is willing to pay
more it can get better.—Ed. B.]
Mr. Guthrie, the retiring general manager
of the London Bank, seems to be a thorough cynic
as regards “farewell demonstrations,” anyhow.
One day last week the clerks of the institution
ventured to approach him in his upstairs sanctum
at the Melbourne office to present him with the
usual illuminated address, expressing their loyalty
to his sacred person in the past and their hopes
for his happiness hereafter. When a retiring boss
of an Australian bank has booked his final passage
for Great Britain—where they mostly all go,
health permitting—it is usual for the long-suffer-
ing employees to commit a small extravagance of
this sort. Heaven only knows why they do it,
but they do. Manager Guthrie must have ex-
pected what was coming, and nerved himself to
discountenance a wasteful expenditure of plati-
tudes and money, for when the clerks waited upon
the potentate he curtly declined the compliment
and drew their attention to the Door. So they
went down stairs, taking the illuminated address
with them. It cost 15 guineas, and anyone in
need of a decorative document can purchase the
souvenir at an alarming sacrifice.
An English doctor, at a recent inquest on
the body of a collier, said that deceased had the
usual collier’s black lungs, and that his heart was
as large as a bullock’s, while his kidneys and liver
were abnormally distended.” Such growths and
conditions, the doctor affirms, are peculiar o
coal-mining.
“F.A.R.” writes from Yodda Valley,
British New Guinea :—
Following my prediction of a month ago, comes the
news of a raid, on 1st Nov., by the natives in Tue Goia
district, and the murder of two unfortunate diggers.
One, “ Jimmy the Austrian,” had several spears driven
through him, his pick driven through his neck, and his
head clubbed into smash. The other, Brackenbury, had
been stoned to death. His head was frightfully batt® re jb
and his body was forced into a narrow race, at which " e
had been working. Although with hardly a whole bone
in his body when found, he was still breathing, and the
two finders had but time to ascertain this fact when thej
in turn had to run for their lives. There’s much doubt
as to the safety of other diggers, who are much scattered,
and would fall an easy prey. When gold is being mined
for on Gira, no natives are ever seen there ; tl b e " ea b est
villages being many miles away, and the so-called Warden
and his police being stationed at Tamatai, 20 miles from
the workings. The diggers therefore in no way encroach
on native rights, and it would need more eloquence than
Bishop Stone Wigg’s to make out these deeds as other
than wanton murders. Government is morally res-
ponsible for the slaughter of these unfortunate
men, and should be held accountable. The style of
the officials who have been in charge of the
district since Governor M‘Gregor’s departure has imbued
the police with such contempt for their superior officers
that one wonders they have even kept within the
semblance of authority. Two have lately been away on a
month’s rapine expedition. One turned up at Kumasi
just before the murders, and was put in irons ; the other
remains at large. Reported that they killed four natives
—three males and one female. It may be that the
murder of the whites was in retaliation for the offences
of the police. A searching inquiry should be held by the
Australian Government. 'I he facts that will be brought to
light will astound them, Let a member ask for a return
of the money spent on the goldfields and that expended
on Samarai by the present administrator, and the reply
will convince the House that further information was
necessary before granting any more money for the mis-
government of the Possession.
Things are going from bad to worse on
Victoria’s comic-opera railways, but the climax
was reached when four old engines broke down in
succession while struggling to get the Adelaide
express as far as Ballarat, and at last a fifth one
had to be sent out from the Garden City to look
for the lost train and bring it in. Minister Bent
now issues an official bellow to the effect that if
trains don’t cease being late he will suspend every-
body concerned. Therefore the ungainly figure
of Thomas may be found hanging from a nail at
any moment.

The first report of the N.S.W. Public
Accounts Committee is out. Judging by the
tameness of the document, which would feed out
of the hand any day, N.S.W. was just as well
without a Public Accounts Committee. About
all the large financial troubles which raged last
year the Committee says nothing. It seems it
can only deal with questions of “unauthorised
expenditure” and with matters referred to it by
certain authorities, and as nothing of any import-
ance was referred to it, its report is a sinful waste
of good paper and ink.
Melbourne haa a Chinese newspaper which
celebrated the first anniversary of its hieroglyphical
nativity the other day. Chang Luk, late of the
Chinese Times in Sydney, runs the new organ on
a broadly patriotic policy—“ Australia for the
Chineeman and evlybody else who gets there.” A
cheery, plump, enthusiastic Confucian is Chang
Luk. Also a believer in the Blitish Empire next
to the Chinese Empire. He tells the 600 sub-
scribers to the organ that if their ancient country
is going to be bossed by foleign devil, they had
better vote for the Blitish foleign devil they
know, rather than the other foleign devil they
don’t know. But Chang Luk takes an Australian
jingo view of the Hempire. He hasn’t seen as
much of Blitish rule in China as a young cabinet-
maker who arrived in Melbourne lately. This
recent addition to the Commonwealth is a fair-
skinned youth, born of English parents at Hong-
kong. Brought up among the natives, he speaks
Chinese fluently and English hardly at all, and
when reminded of his parentage he doesn’t like it.
He has already started to grow a pigtail. Says
he is a Chinaman, and wants to live happily
among his own people in Lilly Bulk-street. His
views of the Blitish Empire were formed from
experience of the—aw —haw-haw rule at Hong-
kong.
WILLIAM ELTON.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ring down the rag, boys ; Billy Elton’s dead ;
Gone home and left us long before his time ;
No waiting for his cue—no good-bye said.
He should be in this act, the merry mime
Who wooed with wit the glimpses of the lime . .
The house would guy us if his lines were read,
But—Billy’s dead.
The play falls flat for want of such a wag
To keep the jesting crisp, the laughter clear;
For he was one of those that never lag
Superfluous on the stage. He should be here
To close the piece, and take his call and hear
The shouts once more. Now, who can speak the tag?
Ring down the rag.

Under the heading : “ The Factories Act—
Vexations Operation,” Melbourne Abgus has
been wailing because certain shameless agri-
cultural sweaters, who had sent their young
children (under 13) to work in the local jam-factory
during their school holidays, were compelled to
desist. If school holidays are to be periods of
hard labor in a factory the children would be
much better without them. The Argus says that
‘‘much irritation ” has been caused by this event.
Did anyone ever hear of a sweater, slave-driver
child-driver, burglar or other bad character who
didn’t experience “much irritation” when com-
pelled to leave off ?
Writes “ Xerxes ” :—
Not generally known that near Walli, about half-way
between Canowindra and Lyndhurst, N.S.W., there are,
on the Belubula River, some splendid caves. Recently
visited them with a party, amongst whom was a man
familiar with Jenolan Caves. He expressed the opinion
that they were quite equal in beauty to the latter. Walli
caves are, of course, quite “ unimproved,” and are not
half explored—a chance for tourists to distinguish them-
selves. In the vicinity there are hot springs, fossils are
abundant, and the river is quite close to the caves. Some
grim stories are told of olden times in the district. The
caves are on the property of Mr. Rothery, whose permis-
sion to visit them must, I think, be obtained.

Arltunga goldfield (S.A.) was without flour
at Christmas, and expected to be so for about
another month. Its festivities were carried
through on a diet of mutton, rice, and sago. It is
being urged in Adelaide that the transcontinental
railway line (when made) should digress far enough
to the east to take in Arltunga, and thereafter
run close to the Q. border.

In the Melbourne “ Boneyard,” as the
P.O. people call the Dead Letter Office, is a
Curiosity Book containing envelopes, &c., that
have at times needed special ingenuity and
patience to ensure their delivery. One super-
scription runs, “To the Chouncle Shir Hall of
the Grand View Estate Skouth Albany, W.A.—
for the rat cleeter.” Another envelope is merely
marked “118 Elis —ambe Split”; this was
accepted as correct at 118 Elizabeth - street.
“Broehenil,” on another, was meant for Broken
Hill; while “ Ridrigand-street, Rollen,” turned
out to be a Russian version of Cardigan-street,
Carlton. A letter was posted one day bearing
only a photo, and “ Victoria. ” The “phiz” was
recognised as that of a Ballarat publican and duly
delivered. Persons with a crude sense of humor
occasionally write a few words on the back of a
stamp, squeeze the address in, and post it. These
the officials generally put through—swearing the
while. The “Bone-yard” has its tragedies
and dramas, too. A letter to a contin-
genter in S’Africa was returned unclaimed.
Opened, it was found to be signed “The girl you
left behind you.” Where the sender’s address
should be was merely a number—she had forgot-
ten to fill in the street and town. But the cancel-
stamp fixed the latter —a Melbourne suburb—and
the postman was told to inquire at that number
in every street. He hadn’t to go far. Almost
the first house he inquired at was occupied by a
family in mourning for a brother killed at the
front, and staying with them was a girl-friend.
She was the “Girl he left behind him.”
Another letter, posted without an address, when
opened disclosed two =£10 notes —nothing else
whatever. No inquiries were made either by
sender or expected recipient. Soon afterwards
another letter was being opened (the addressee
being undiscoverable) when a small slip of paper
fell from the letter. This bore the query, ‘ ‘ Did
you get the two notes ? ” The writer of this —a
representative citizen —was asked to call at the
Boneyard. On being asked if he had lately sent
a letter containing two tenners, he flew into a
great rage. “It was like their dashed impudence,
opening people’s letters; he would see the Deputy
P.M.G. at once.” The officer apologised for
troubling him, he was merely trying to discover
the sender of a letter they couldn’t deliver.
“ And why not ? ” “ Because it had no address.”
The wrathful cit. suddenly cooled down and ad-
mitted that he had sent such a letter, giving the
date it was posted. “ Could you give the numbers
of the notes ? ” He could, and did, and they
were handed over. Then the two went out and
saw a man about a dog, and the citizen admitted
he had been wondering why the notes had never
been acknowledged—but he didn’t say why he
made no inquiries, nor why he got in a rage when
asked about them. “The Curiosity Book” also
contains one signature embellished with a wide
margin of red ink. The writer of this had kicked
up an earthquake once aboutan undelivered missive
which he subsequently discovered under his own
blotting-pad. He was the only man who ever
came back to the Boneyard to apologise for being
abusive without cause, therefore his signature is
preserved as that of the one real gentleman in
Victoria.
Apropos Inaudi, the human calculating-
machine. T’otherside technical journals lately
describe a calculating-machine that isn’t human,
and yet seems as marvellous :
The basis of the system is a card with a number of holes
punched in it, each hole having a definite meaning,
representing a number, a locality, a color, a crop, or
whatever else is desired. The superstructure of the sys-
tem is an electric tabulating machine, capable of taking
note of every hole a card does, or can, present; of select-
ing and grouping the cards according to the presence or
absence of certain holes, of computing the totals repre-
sented by any set of holes, and, in a word, of extracting
all the information the cards supply, and of arranging it
in a hundred different ways. A 100 horse-power
engine has been turned out, and the accounts show that
it has cost less than a similar engine constructed three
years ago. The manager wants to discover the cause of
the economy. It may be due to new tools bought in the
interval, to a lower average of wages paid, to a speeding-
up of the tools, or to better methods and discipline by
which the output of the men is increased, or to a com-
bination of two or more of these causes. The passage
through the machine of all the daily cards belonging to
the periods during which the jobs were in the shops
would pick out all the cards pertaining to the engines,
the other cards being replaced in their proper order.
Then it would be easy to dissect the accounts, and find
the number of men of each class on the job, the number
of hours worked, the average and total wages for each
class of men, and the whole amount charged for shop
expenses to each type of machine, and so on.
Society item from Pt. Douglas (N.Q.): —
Last July (as our readers remember) two Chinese ladies
arrived in Pt. Douglas and there was a wedding a few
days later. News has just reached us from Mossman that
one of the fair ones resident there last Sunday added a
unit to the population of the sugar township.
It is no small event when a Chow is born in a
N.Q. township.
Dry Dog Reid has apparently made a poor
impression in Westralia. This is how he struck
Perth Morning Herald— until very lately a
rampant Foreign-trade organ
From Mr. Reid’s speech at Fremantle on Tuesday
evening the student of political economy can deduce two
important facts in support of the policy of Freetrade.
The first is that in the U.S. of America Protection has
brought down wages to such an extent that—to quote
from the speech—“ the great mass of the laborers do not
earn more than £40 or £50 a year.” The second is that
the Australian manufacturer need not fear the compe-
tition of goods made in the U.&, because—we quote
again from the speech—“in the States even higher wages
are paid than those prevailing in Australia.” These two
arguments seem conclusive. The policy of Protection,
which makes wages so low that the worker cannot live,
and so high that the manufacturer cannot compete with
his rivals in Freetrade countries, must be a terrible thing
indeed. Of course, Protectionists may claim that Mr.
Reid’s two arguments are mutually destructive, and
that as a matter of fact, the American work-
man’ is better off than the workman of any
other country, whilst the American manufacturer is
getting more'than his fair share of the world’s markets.
Mr. Reid is only employing the usual methods of the con-
troversialist when he insists that facts popularly sup-
posed to be stubborn things—must always lend them-
selves to his own view of the case. The mistake he seems
to have made was the introduction of the two opposing
facts in the one speech. The Freetrader may make some
capital with the employer by showing that Protection
increases wages, or with the by demonstrating
that it decreases wages, but only “ Yes-No ” Reid would
think of arguing both ways in the one evening. Evi-
dently he wants to catch the manufacturer and the
laborer on the one hook.
