Ballister’s Baking; the Other
Fellow’s Story!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Necessarily the other fellow’s—because when,
at Penang, I had the temerity to ask "Bully
Ballister” for his version, his reply, in three
short words, was beneath the dignity of history.
Civilised man, commiserating the brute creation
hugely in words, ill-treats it hardly the less in
deeds; savage man’s practice accords strictly
with his theory. “One man doesn’t feel pain
inflicted on another. Why, then, should he
trouble himself about the sufferings of a brute ? ”
Klake couldn’t understand it. “ Our fathers
have always taken the shell thus; why should we
do otherwise ? ”
Crampton deliberately loaded up the magazine
of his Winchester, the other counting, in his own
tongue, the cartridges as they went in.
“For every one of those reasons, Klake ! Chief
thou callest thyself sometimes ; then, if chief thou
art, let but one more turtle go over the fire, alive,
and the first of these bullets is for thee ! ”
The trader spoke Malay, and, in a broken
version of the same, Klake —assumptive enough
in general —disclaimed the dangerous honor.
“Chief I am none. Tulopi is chief. Tulopi is
there—let him speak for himself.”
Klake’s uncle grinned sardonically. “Like the
young war-bird of the Malays is my nephew—
chief while the old cock is not by.”
Of the hearers who understood Malay, a dozen
raised the uproarious Papuan guffaw. As many
more looked dubiously—as asking leave to laugh—
at Klake, and then, as he angrily picked up his
spear, did likewise, and came over to his side.
Bows, too, were handled, and a club or two swung
up, but then over the sand-ridge came suddenly a
burly, bearded man in striped cottons, who, with
many oaths and in three tongues, commented
freely on the situation.
“Look well, you do, Crampton!—a-standin’
there to egg these cussed niggers on to a shindy
that’ll throw us back a week, perhaps—an’ the
blazin’ schooner due in a couple o’ days ! Beauty
of a pardner you are, an’ no mistake! What in
thunder’s it all about, anyhow ? ”
“It’s just this, Ballister! I know something
about tortoiseshell, I suppose?”
“ S’pose you do ! —what then ? ”
“Well —kill your turtle and scald the plates
off quick—that’s good shell. But get ’em off the
living turtle with a fire—your stuff isn’t so good,
and you’re a cruel, cold-blooded scoundrel.”
“Bully Ballister” stared with all his bloodshot
eyes. “Wli—who ?” he gasped.
“ I don’t say you ! You’re a white man after
all, I suppose, and you can’t have known how
these wretches were getting the shell for you.”
The other did a weird fandango on the sand
before he could find his astounded tongue.
“Not know! —me? Why, you contaminated
mutton-headed idiot! Let me just catch one of
’em a-strippin’ plates off a dead turtle an’ he never
gets another electrified ounce o’ shell for me !
Get back to your trepang-dryin’ as quick’s you
like! Guess I can run this here shell-racket
without any cussed mollycoddle of a Laglander to
help me ! ”
Had Crampton even been a Cornstalk the slur
might have been less gratuitous. As a Banana-
lander his wrath was great.
“You should have been a lag yourself long ago,
Mr. Bully Ballister, by all accounts. And, any-
how, you’re every word of what I called you just
now —and a blatherskiting cur into the bargain ! ”
Ballister was bully enough for twenty, but it
wasn’t cowardice that kept his hand from his
pistol. There is no keeper of the peace like a
commercial interest. “If I plug him,” thought
Ballister, rapidly, “ten to one old Tulopi’ll stick
to the young fool’s half of the stuff —or p’raps
knock me on the head and collar the whole
caboodle.”
He swallowed the rage that had mottled with
unwholesome white his brick-red face. “Just
drop that muzzle o’ yours a bit, young man, will
you ! You an’ me ain’t a-goin’ to blow holes in
each other this turn. I was a bit rough on you
first, I’cknowledge, but let’s take the thing cool
an’ business-like”—and, to the grievous disap-
pointment of the Pogonga men, eagerly expectant
of a “ white-man powder fight,” Ballister took out
his pipe and lit it. Crampton dropped his rifle
into the hollow of his arm, but then between the
two came Tulopi and planted his club-head with a
thump in the sand.
“ It shall be as my brother Crampti wishes. A
foolish notion of his is this. But it is all one to
the men of Pogonga. The shell shall be taken, as
he says, from the dead turtle, not any more from
the living.”
Ballister turned on the old man a sinister red
eye. “We’ll see about that, old cabbage-head.
Just you hold your jaw in the meantime. Now,
look-a-here, Crampton—haven’t I heard you say,
vourself, that these here hawksbill turtles is
bound to be all used up in time because they sticks
so much to the islands they’ve been hatched on ?
An’ don’t you think it’s a darned selfish kind o’
thing to kill every critter for its shell —’stead o’
lettin’ it go agin, to grow more for somebody else?
Grows agin, right enough, the shell does! Old
Fly-by-Night, here, ’ll tell you that, himself !”
But “Fly-by-Night,” otherwise Tulopi, being
questioned in Malay, shook his grizzled mop-
head. “No ; the turtles always died soon after.”
Ballister shook his fist in the old man’s face.
“It’s a lie!” he said. “What’s to kill ’em?
Warmin’ up their backs a bit? Darned if I
wouldn’t stand as much, myself, for a flamin’ ten-
dollar bill! ”
Now, to “demonstrate” at a Pogonga-man is
as bad as to strike him, and for five minutes Mr.
Ballister’s valuable brains were in imminent dan-
ger of dispersion. But the old chief—thinking
that perhaps the English words carried some
amelioration of the insult—held his hand. “ What
saith this mad fellow ? ” he asked of the Queens-
lander.
“That thou art a liar, Tulopi!—and that he
could himself endure all the turtles suffer ! ”
“It is well!” said the chief, quietly, “I will
remember! ”
• • • •
The “ Blushing Fifteen,” men said, had excel-
lent reason to blush over some of the transactions
she had been engaged in, and certain it was, in any
case, that her skipper, “ Shady ” Macdermott,
was very shady. For which reason, when he had
got his anchor down and was comfortably sampling
Ballister’s “ Blankenheim,” he swallowed as
readily as the Hollands a proposal that would have
staggered almost any man—short of an actual
pirate.
“It’s this way, Mac. ! I don’t care a rotten
banana about the way the shell’s got! But I’m
not a-goin’ to be bested by a green-witted galoot
of a half-bred Britisher! Now, I’ll get at him
two ways ! First, I’ll get Klake and some of his
party, to-night, and we’ll yank my gentleman out
of his bed an’ down to the turtle-shed. There s a
couple o whoppers there that was caught last
night, and, just to show my milk-an’-water friend
who’s boss, we’ll put ’em on the fire alive ! ”
Macdermott drank, and nodded. “Very pretty,
all this ! ’ he growled. “ But it isn’t business ! ”
“I’m a-comin’ to business, Mac. Listen!
While me an’ Klake’s at this little job what’ll you
an’ your crew be doin’ ? ”
Experience of his man gave the demi-pirate the
clue. the stuff!” he said, and winked
his solitary eye.
“ Exac’ly, old ship! So, if you’re the same
scarletty man you used to be, you’ll just take a
squint round with me to get the lay of things, an’
then you’ll get aboard an’ heave-short, so’s you
can break your gory mud-hook out at a minute’s
notice. Then you gets your boats ready for ’bout
leven o’clock ; there’ll be a bit of a moon, an’ the
whole blooming lot should be aboard by daylight
—when I slip down to you, an’ off we goes an’
leaves my bold Australian to whistle for his share
of the spondulicks ! See ? ”
“I see! Half?”
“Yes, it’s a half-share. Oh ! yours, you mean ?
Too hot, you old sinner ! Say a quarter !”
“Half!—or I’m not in it!”—and the man of
few words emptied his tumbler and got up.
Same old angle-iron as ever ! Half it is, then
—rot you ! ”
* * * •
But the parties to this precious contract had
reckoned without Tulopi.
That astute old gentleman—thinking it was
about time the supremacy-question was settled—
had provided Klake with other shadows than those
made for him by sun or moon, and was no whit
surprised to be roused up about midnight with
the news that Klake and half-a-score of followers
had accompanied the one white man to the
sleeping-hut of the other.
“Get the people,” he said, “ and let them follow
me ! To the turtle-shed ? ” he added, as another
spy came breathless in. “Good! —we will go,
also ! ”
Now Ballister, having spent the afternoon in
swiping raw gin, was by now in danger of ex-
plosion. By the time the turtle-shed was reached
his alcohol-expanded ferocity had so wholly
swamped his judgment that mere turtle-roasting
was too mild a sop for his revenge. Crainpton
himself, swore Ballister by all his gods, should
taste of the fire—and Klak6 was willing. But his
men were not. Klake wasn’t top dog yet in
Pogonga, by a long way. Tulopi loved this white
man, and Tulopi’s avenging capabilities they
trembled to recall.
“And well is it for ye that ye have so chosen ! ”
said a well-known voice from the outer precincts
of the firelight.
Ballister gazed stupidly round, and Klake
lowered the club he had raised to brain —as a
matter of principle—the spokesman of his myr-
midons, who, for their part, flopped down as one
man upon the sand. They were but ten, and
behind Tulopi the fire-light glanced upon the eyes
and weapons of the whole fighting-strength of the
island—rallied round the old man at the pinch.
Tulopi motioned Klake’s men to join the rest-
then called out a dozen in their stead. “ Crampti,”
he said, “the men of the ship take off thy goods
this night! Is this well ? ”
“It is not well, Tulopi! Some of this man’s
evil work it is ! ”
“Go down then, thou, with all these men. The
fault be with thyself if these white thieves touch
another morsel of thy substance !”
» • * •
Macdermott, pulling cautiously in for a second
boat-load, saw, in the misty moonlight, the white
sand backed by a long line of dusky figures.
“ Holy Patrick! —here’s a fightin’ set-out! —the
whole blasted island ! Aboard again, boys, as
quick as you like ! Our little game’s up ; and it’s
a flamin’ good job we’re hove-short —we wouldn’t
have much show if that crowd came off to us ! * _
The breeze was fresh off the land, and with it
came presently a long-drawn, hideous yell, that
sounded clear above the clanking of the schooner’s
windlass.
“ Friend Ballister a-gettin’ pepper ! ” the
skipper said, coolly. “ And serve the drunken
idiot dam well right! Heave away, boys, and
break him out —before it’s our turn ! ”
• * • *
To Crampton, on the beach, that appalling
screech carried a horrible meaning. He remem-
bered Ballister’s senseless boast, and off he went,
helter-skelter, through the cocoanut trees, over
the limestone ridge, and down the sandy slope
towards the turtle shed. Between him and the
red glare of the fire —like a vision of hell—black
figures struggled frantically to and fro, and then
again, as he reached the shed, arose an ear-
splitting scream of agony.
He was right!—Ballister was on the fire /
Naked, and lashed back-downwards to the bars
—the wretch was as yet but badly scorched when
Crampton rushed in and dragged the grating
bodily off the furnace.
Quoth Tulopi, placidly : “ This man said I was
a liar, and I said I would remember ! In that, at
least, I have not been a liar! But, with all, I
would not have roasted him, Crampti,. had he
not sought to roast thee. The matter is thine ;
take the beast away if thou wilt—and quickly—
lest I send him after Klake faster than 1 had
thought to do ! ”
“ Klake ? Where is Klake gone ? ”
The old man swung his club carelessly towards
a corner of the shed, and dimly in the shadow
Crampton made out a loosely prostrate figure.
He reached for the club and held it towards the
fire-light. Upon the ponderous head long, human
hairs were stuck with half-dried blood.
Tulopi laughed as he took back the weapon.
“That which was,” he said, “was never evil
when that which is is good ! ’’
It was his version of “All’s well that ends
well.”
* * * *
Ballister, nursing his back and his wrath, lay
low until the Dutch Commissioner came round
upon his yearly “any complaints’’’circuit.
Grievous, then, in the official ear was * Bully s
tale—more grievous still his astonishment when
the Commissioner, who had heard of Mr. Ballister
before, ordered him promptly on board the
steamer—ran him up to Amboyna, and shipped
him thence for Singapore, with the pleasing inti-
mation that if he turned up again in Dutch pro-
tected ” territory, he’d make one of the noble
army of martyrs in the antimony mines of feissik.
ALEX. MONTGONERY.
Some of the latest electric-trams have
adopted the Christensen in place of the Westing-
house brake. The Christensen works quite mde-
pendently of the tram, whilst the Westmghouse
is “ pumped ” up by the motion of the car.
The British Navy is a great raisin-eater ;
300 tons of the dried fruit are swallowed annually
on board H.M. ships. So it is good news to hear
that the Imperial authorities are going to give the
Victorian product a trial. This is the result of
representations made by Minister Taverner, ana
should open up a big field for Mildura.

The Lost Muse.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I left the crowded city :
I could no longer rest. . .
Farewell to comrades witty !
Good-bye to song and jest!
I left the crowded city
Upon a silent quest.
There strong men wrought and wrangled
For room to breathe and be ;
Hard men who could have strangled
The sweet nymph Poesy :
There strong men wrought and wrangled—
But what were they to me?
The City they are barred in
So close that no man sees
God walking in His garden
Among His pleasant trees :
The City they are barred in
By evil destinies.
The houses crouch together
Like dumb beasts terrified :
In vain the golden weather
Gilds all the world outside :
The houses crouch together
As if from God to hide.
I left the crowded City
With all its noise and glare,
Its Greed that knows no pity,
Its .loy and its Despair :
I left the crowded City
To breathe the freer air.
My heart was sore with yearning,
And visions thronged my brain
That like a wheel was turning
And spinning threads of pain :
My heart was sore with yearning
To find my Love again.
Good people, have ye seen her
Who is so fair to see ?
The grass she treads grows greener,
The leaves dance on the tree . . .
Good people, have ye seen her
Who is the world to me ?
She sings where’er she passes
The song of sweet Desire ;
The eyes of lads and lasses
She fills with tender fire :
She sings where’er she passes
And plays upon a lyre.
I heard the wild swans calling
Where northern rivers flow ;
I heard a voice enthralling
That well I used to know :
I heard the wild swans calling
Along the sunset glow.
Upon an island lonely,
Beneath a wild green vine,
For one swift moment only
I saw a vision shine—
Upon an island lonely
Of sighing reed and pine.
Was it my Lady playing
The tunes that charm the trees ?
Or but a mist-wreath swaying
And bending in the breeze ?
Was it my Lady playing
Old Orphic melodies ?
The reeds with secrets quiver
Around that lonely isle
Set in the Northern river
Where Pan reigns yet awhile :
The reeds with secrets quiver—
And oh, I saw her smile!
“ Hast thou some younger Lover,
O fickle Muse,” I cried,
“For whom thou dost uncover
Thy beauty, like a bride ?
Hast thou some younger Lover
Who will not be denied ?
“ And doth he hold thee dearer
And love thee more than I ?
And dost thou draw him nearer
To thee when ghosts go by ?
And doth he hold thee dearer
Than life ? O Muse, reply ! ”
I heard the pine-trees sighing
Like mourners stricken sore ;
I heard the reeds replying
In whispers round the shore ...
I heard the pine-trees sighing—
And I heard nothing more.
GRAFTON, N.S.W.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
Filial Tenderness.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was the first day for the payment of Old Age
Pensions. As late as half-past two in the after-
noon the crowd of applicants blocked the footpath
and part of the street.
“We’ve been ’ere since ten ’smornin’,” com-
plained one old fellow.
“The derangements is rotten,’’declared another.
“ There’s bin four or five faintin’ fits a’ready, an’
the sun’s gittin’ ’otter an’ ’otter. Them old
females oughter ’ave a hawnin’ ter wait under. It
wouldn’t cost nothink, wouldn’t a hawnin’. Jist
a few poles an’ a scrap er canvas from a verandah
out inter the street a little ways, an’ a few seats
under it If there was a hawnin’ like that ”
“It don’t want no awnin’,” interrupted an
aggressive-looking pensioner, with some war-
medals on his breast. “All it wants is fer the
Gov’ment to block them there people what ain’t
c'ot no business a-crowdin’ round the doors.
What’s all them young ’uns doin’ there?”
It was suggested that the younger people might
have charge of aged relatives,
“ Some has, some hain’t. There’s one old
woman got no less’n four middlin’ young females
to shove ’er up to the door. One’s enough.”
A policeman opened the big door and admitted
half-a-dozen of the applicants ; then closed it
again by main force against the surging little
crowd.
One old fellow came round from the exit. He
was tightly buttoned up in a long brown coat, and
carried under his arm a heavy, brass-bound box
as big as a coal-scuttle. He was almost crying
for joy. “I’ve got it,” he panted, “got it at
last.” Then suddenly realising that the wealth
he had acquired after so much trouble might
tempt robbers, he checked his exuberance and
shuffled away.
The oldest men and the youngest of the women
seemed least resigned to the long, weary waiting,
for they crushed closest to the door. The younger
men and the very oldest women (excepting those
that were being forcibly pushed to the front by
young relatives) hobbled about the outskirts of
the crowd, grumbling, or sat on the kerb with
their feet in the gutter, and looked dejectedly
resigned.
“’Tis in the crush I was an hour ago,” said an
old dame, “ but I’d be dead if I stayed, wid their
pushin’ and squazin’, so I would.”
A kindly shopkeeper across the way sent a boy
over with a few empty cases as seats for the old
women.
The passing of a fainting old woman from the
centre of the crush out into the open attracted
attention. She was half-dragged, half-supported
by a woman of about 35, a stoutish, sharp-nosed
person, wearing a red toque with yellow flowers,
and a blue dress with the placket gaping. Her
voice was as sharp as her nose. She was evidently
annoyed at the collapse of her aged companion.
“’Ere, sit down a bit on this box. Now yer all
right. I’m sure I dunno what’s the matter with
yer; there’s somethink ’appenin’ yer every day
lately. Now, ’old up yer ’ead, don’t go slippin’
inter the gutter all of a heap.”
But the old woman persisted in slipping help-
lessly down beside the box. Somebody brought
watev, but it failed to revive the patient.
“Git’er some brandy,” suggested an old pen-
sioner wearing two medals.
The sharp-nosed woman snapped angrily,
“Brandy ! Where’s brandy money to come from,
d’ye think? People can’t get even what’s their
own after waitin’ ’ere since afore 12, let alone
brandy money.”
The old soldier beat a retreat.
“ ’Ere, ’old up, can’t yer! ” continued the virago,
at the same time shaking her charge rather
roughly. “You ain’t as bad as all that. I
declare them old folks is tryin’.”
They got the patient on the box again and
propped her back against a verandah post. The
sharp-nosed woman talked excitedly and querul-
ously, meanwhile vigorously fanning the wrinkled
face of the old woman with a newspaper. “It
ain’t bad enough ’avin’ ter drag all the ways up
from the Point and gittin’ squashed tryin’ ter get
in fer the money, but now she must go and faint
like this.”
“ Did she walk up from Dawes Point ? ” queried
a bystander.
“You don’t suppose she was druv up George’s-
street in a’ansom, do yer?” snapped the sharp-
nosed woman. “Drat the pins ! ” she continued,
trying to unfasten a pin with which the neck of
the old woman’s dress was secured. “My God !
Ain’t she ever comin’-to?”
The yellow flowers shook with excitement;
their wearer was getting anxious. Going down
on her knees, she passed one arm around the
shoulders of the old woman, and with the dis-
engaged hand fanned more vigorously than ever.
The patient exhibited signs of recovery.
“Brandy!and ’ansoms indeed !” muttered the
other—“ Where’d I get money for brandy and
’ansoms, I’d like ter know?” Then, growing
confidential, she gave the little circle some family
history:—“l sez to my ’usband, only the other
day, I sez, * Tom, you’ll be sorry ever yer mother
come outer the ’sylum, mark my words.’ But ’e
would ’ave ’er out, and sez ’e: ‘ Look at the
pension—over two pound a month, an’ no trouble
ter get it! ’ No trouble ! That’s all a man knows
about it. We ain’t drawed no pension yet, an’
we’ve kep’ ’er over three weeks.”
“If you don’t get some brandy she won’t never
draw no pensions ; she’ll be dead,” said the old
soldier, returning to the charge.
The sharp-nosed woman turned pale. “My
God ! ” she exclaimed. The prospect of losing
the pension after all her trouble was alarming.
“Well, I can’t leave her to go and get brandy,”
she whimpered.
Several volunteered to go the message, but a
newsboy was the first one over to the hotel. He
returned empty-handed. “Won’t give it ’thout
the beans,” he announced shortly.
The woman looked appealingly round the circle,
but most faces were averted. Sixpences were
scarce in the gathering.
“Pawn yer red ’at,” suggested the newsboy,
but his impudence passed unnoticed.
The sick woman moaned feebly, and the white
head fell sideways like that of a dying fowl.
“’Tis dying the poor body is,” quavered an old
Irishwoman, who had left her seat on the kerb
and made her way to the scene. Some of those
sq ueezing towards the door of the Pensions Office
turned their heads at this announcement, but few
left their places to investigate further.
“ Why don’t you send for a dhrop of rum ? ” de-
manded the last speaker.
The patient’s daughter-in-law sought her pocket,
and as she fumbled therein the gaping placket
gaped wider than ever. After a while she con-
fusedly withdrew her hand, empty, and again
looked appealingly round among the crowd. But
none volunteered to give.
The old Irishwoman once more lifted up her
voice: “Sure, ’tis terrible, so it is, to lave an
ould woman die in the strate for the sake av a
dirty saxpence ! ”
At this, terror seemed to take possession of the
younger woman, and, with an exclamation that
was half a shriek and half a curse, she plunged her
hand into her pocket and drew forth a coin, which
she threw at the feet of the old soldier. “For
God’s sakebequick,” she screamed. Then, noticing
that the coin the man picked up was yellow, she
turned red and white by turns. She again thrust
her hand into her pocket, this time withdrawing
it with the palm hidden from any eyes but her
own.
“ It’s the smallest I’ve got,” she stammered.
The old soldier hurried away on his errand.
“ Get rum, not brandy,” she cried after him.
When the soldier returned and handed the
woman the glass, she placed it on the kerb, while
she carefully pocketed the change, 9s. 9d. Then
she found that the old Irishwoman had seized the
glass, and, with shaking hand, was trying to force
some of the spirit between the pale lips of the
patient.
“Give it here,” demanded she of the red hat,
roughly. “I’ll make’er drink it meself. You’ll
fumble and fool about till yer spill ther lot. It
costa money ! ”
JOHN DRAYMAN.
